THE RED PAGE.
BA. D. Bayldon writes on the art of
?
er if vanity be always a weakness. Mine
otten makes me ashamed ; but I’m not sure that
in many eases it has not aided me very materially.
I should never dream of giving swimming exhibi-
tions did not my vanity derive satisfaction from
them. Often when I’ve stepped forward to enter
the water under the direct gaze of spectators my
Etiveness would have made me quail but for
nnuediate interposition of my vanity aroused
pplause. Once in the water, however, and
vanity is forgotten. Ob ! the joy of the
quick stroke—of the curl and cut through the
clear cool water like a fish ; to be rendered well-
nigh helpless with straps and assert your m aster-
dom; to feel your thoughts become movements;
to see the gre«-n sunlit water whirling above you
as you dart along feet first by a mere palm-push.
Or to bask on the surface with half-lidded eyes
feeding on sky-purple ; to be alone with two im-
mensities. sky and sea ; to forget in a pagan
reverie the dingy world from which vou have
8li{ : >ped like a winged tiling from its sheath. The
gods might envy one such happiness. Byron’s
lines do him Justice-
How many a time have 1
Cloven with arm still lustier, breast more daring,
The wave all roughened ; with a swimmer's stroke
Flinging the billows back from my drenched hair,
And laughing from uiy lip the audacious brine.
Which kissel it like a wine-cup, rising o'er
The waves as they arose, and prouder still
The loftier they uplifted me; and oft,
In wamonness of spirit, plunging down
Into their green and glassy gulfs, and making
My way to shells and sea-weed; all unseen
By those above, till they waxed fearful; then
Returning with my grasp full of such tokens
_ An showed that I had searched the deep ; exulting
With a far-dashing stroke, and drawing deep
The long-suspended breath, again I spurned
The foam which broke around me, and pursued
My track like a sea-bird.
Those are the lines of a strong swimmer delight-
ing in his strength. Who will give us something
about the subtle swimmer indolently gliding,
sometimes over the surface, sometimes under it,
with lardiy an effort? Not the strong, sweeping
stroke with its rank of bubbles, not the deep
areath and superb breasting against saucy billows;
jut the insidious curve of a leisurely-lifted limb,
;he sinuous movement of a lithe, supple figure
with ut bubble or foam-fleck—who will sing of it ?
And who will paint us some huge billow, not as
we see it from the shore rolling heavily towards
us, a horizontal trunk of water; or from a steamer
deck, a hillock foam-spotted tumbling away ; but
as the swimmer sees it when it looms above him
with toppling foam-boulders, with widening
chasms and ever-changing ledges (which from a
steamer-deck ap|>e»r out gaps and wrinkles) ere
be is lifted for a momentary glimpse of the
rhythmic rocking wilderness fit) sink leisurely back
into the deep trough swaying him to and fro
Poets look upon the sea as something to be de-
lighted with, to battle with, to weave images
about; no wonder she has an imperious disdain
for such triflers. You must become her child to
understand her. She is not treacherous, but sin-
cere. She has no time for the earth-born they
must take their chance. Her children do not chide,
but intuitively accommodate themselves to her
moods. And her many moods are their many
sources of ever-changing joy. When you think
her angry she is affording them exquisite amuse
ment, They love to glide round the roots of the
great billows or Blip through their breaking
columns, or hang motionless under them in de-
licious ease. Hark ! how she booms in yonder
cavern !
* 9 • «
I Flying must be something like swimming, I
imagine. The same leisure, the same sense of
freedom, the same scenery, the same commotions
to thrill you, the same eahus to drowse you. With
all their adverse circumstances, the inhabitants of
the sky and sea seem to possess what we jaxir
jaded creatures often sigh for ; the consciousness of
rest. Look at that fish serenely suspended in
deep water; nothing troubling him. How lazily
he moves his gills! I must believe he has the
capacity to feel the color of those anemones below
. him. And there- lie'll hang for hours, blissfully
unoonscious of everything but rest —how delight-
fully envious he makes one ! It may be a
delusion to us who can see his enemy prowling
near. He is happily unaware of him. Ah ! like
Sa flash he is gone. But how soon he will for-
get his danger and again begin brooding. He
cannot lie cursed with a memory. Surely he has
no aspirations for ever spurring him. And
what pleasures are his ! If it is too hot he merely
sinks a few fathoms, or vice versd. And what de-
lirious fun it must be to slip and slide and turn
somersaults like a porpoise at the bows of a
steamer. And then, when you are bored with it,
to give a contemptuous wriggle of the tail and
plunge down into the great restful depths of grate-
ful gloom and soothing silence. And then, when
you are again bored, to throw into every muscle
a space-killing speed, Down 1 down to the ocean
bed like a silver si>ear shot from the hand of a god,
With monstrosities streaming past you like thunder-
clouds, amid showers of momentary lights, till
the gloom falls back and you slacken speed in a
faint - luminous, motionless element. And then
you will see groat eyes rolling red in sockets of
fire, stars biokering green and purple, palpitating
forma like floating moons, mountains swelling into
gorgeous bloom and sullenly retreating. How
the imagination revels in the idea of looking on the
forms engendered by the mother in her first throes
—shadowy phantasms too impalpable for color, too
immaterial for gross sustenance, too solemn for
movement—and of contemplating the vast ocean
bed stretching out silent and strangely sad, with
the white splendour of lifelessneas paced by some
solitary figure!
• • • *
Though I won some trifling prizes for quick
swimming in boyhood, my heart was never in it.
I cannot admire mere machine force and endur-
ance. I must have grace and beauty. That
double-overhand stroke so popular in Australia I
detest. J. B, Johnson’s single over-hand stroke
had a fine curve about it; but then he was really
built for a fancy swimmer. And that is one of the
great secrets of fancy swimming. A trick in itself
may be simple, but if the swimmer has a beauti-
fully-curved figure and knows how to use it he
will get applause. The roundness and lily-like
whiteness of limb and trunk tell more in a fancy-
swimming oontest than stubborn thews and hairy
br wn. And what splash and bustle and uncouth
noises those big, muscular swimmers make ! They
fet speed, to be sure, but at what a sacrifice 1
9^here is the music of movement, the poetry
of shape? Ocean’s children know all this, and
even in their roughest moods, unless they lose
their heads like a hooked shark, they are artists.
I can sit for hours watching fish in the aquarium.
They have a coquettish dalliance in their curves
and side-shoots. There is no effort, no back-
water, no disturbance ; all is rhythmically beauti-
ful. Even the octopus, hideous as he is, is no dis-
cord in the general harmony of movement. And
what a harmony it is! Let a big, strong swimmer
look on it and blush. There is the perfection of
swimming—swift dart and serene suspension, with
the rapid interplay of twist and undulation linked
harmoniously together like notes of a Moonlight
Sonata. Their very breathing apparatus seems
set to a subtle undertone of melody. And what
colours and shapes they have I—they are vain ol
them too, like a peacock. As they dart with
racing sunbeams on them, think you they don’t
know what a fine sparkle they make? It’s the old
story —vanity, ail is vanity, and at the root of it
sex-instinct. And surelv that dangerous leaping
into atmosphere arises from it. What care thej
about the sun-god sitting in his blue tent and
shaking his golden tresses over them, or the silver
sliding moon-sickle reaping down the stars? It’s
all vanity and sex-instinct, and, though infinitely
inferior to them in the art of swimming, I’ve more
than„enough of those weaknesses. Selah !
C. H. Read writes from Tasmania:—
James Hebblethwaite’s “ A Spring Ride” (26/11,’98) is
picture of Tasmanian scenery from a haughty Britisher’s
point of view. It is a pity so interesting a writer moves
in so small a circle of bush-iife. He is of the stuff to make
a bush-writer, but he requires nine years’ shaping with
bush axe. The verdant lanes and white hedgerows o
Kiulindcolour his canvas, and the tweat-tweat-glug-glug-
tootle-toos of hei* songsters mar his music.- I am an Ans
tralian, pure and simple -1 trust. And it grieves anr
Irritates me to read that the clear, liquid, throaty notes
of the Tasmanian magpie are “harsh." Amy Sherwit
should be “the Tasmanian Magpie," only “ Nightingale’
sounds more poetical. Amy has the pie’s throat, full an<
deep; not the narrow, piping, slugging thorax of the
English bird. It is worth gold to be an audience of one in
the glorious concert-hall of a moonlit forest, and to listen
to the first pie as he lifts his head and warbles a solo to
the stars. And then from far away, and gradually nearer
twenty more of his fellows take up the wondrous song
Then silence. Then once again begins the leader, to be
caught and passed by the singers haif-a-mile away. Then
silence. It is like listening, I should think, to a choru
of angels singing up in the dome of a vast cathedral.
And what does J.H. say of these warblers of the dawn
these choristers of night? Listen : “They are the free
lances of the air, merry light-hearted rogues, amTiTiiidit
to be dressed in flaming red.” Good ged ! 1 cannot do
justice to my feelings, but good ged! Then he pro-
ceeds: “Parrots . . . robins . . . fire-tails,dusky and
plum-colored like the—th Hussars (good ged !) . . . shy
diamond-birds . . . flash and dart in the sunshine. But
song is denied them. Their voices are, as a rule, harsh.
One passed the day in shrieking * Take off your hat!*
and another the night demanding * Morepork ! * while the
shag has the hoarse, hooting laugh of a wicked old man.”
I will floor James H. with one of his good old English
cross-buttocks. I will assert no more than he has, and
burst him with his own trick. I will say that the English
birds are songless (and now he squirms in my grip). And
I will give three examples (three he gave)—the rook, the
owl and the eagle (now I have him on my hip). One caws
all the day ; another hoots all night ; and the last yells in
your ear like a Victorian bookmaker (and now he is on
the broad of his British back).
Yet 1 will be more generous than J.H. The sweetest
singer I ever heard was an English linnet. Fourteen
thousand miles he travelled in my company, and for ten
years he sang to us, and knew us every one. And at his
death, the result of a deplorable accident, the whole house
mourned.
It would much please me to conduct J.H. to a certain
range not far from Mt. Bischoff, where he could look
down on a thousand acres of scarlet waratah. A sight
not to be seen In his England, the land of little meadows
sprinkled with buttercups and daisies. And I could show
him breadth of creamy white, and yellow, and gold, and
dead and delicate pink, that would move even his
British coldness. The monotony of the hush, so often
mentioned by writers who were never there, exists only
where the hand of man has touched. The fall of the axe
is beauty’s doom. There are still spots in Tasmania where
the white foot has never trodden, and where a hushman
may sit and think without the aid of print to guide his
thoughts. A lover of nature needs no book ; and no land
on this earth is without its memories— I Tasmania, of the
new world, least of all. J.H. loves his England why
not—and 1 love my Australia as 1 should—and our ideas
are as far apart as his peaceful lanes and verdant meadows
are from the lights and shades of my mountain scenery
and the greys and greens of my forests.
Discussing Parnell, the Saturday Review prints
some details which will not appear in Morley’g
|jfe of Gladstone —
By the end of 1885 Mr. Parnell, after starting as an un-
known country squire, had, while still under foi ty, with
only six years of real leadership, and without a sin id
colleague whom he could trust., brought every English
party to terms.. It was an achievement absolutely with-
out parallel in Parliamentary history, but the facts are
not in dispute. Lord Randolph Churchill was actively
negotiating ; Lord Carnarvon, the Vioeffly, was discussing
details with Mr. Parnell himself in the fa nous “ empty
house”; Lord Salisbury was expatiating on the advan-
tages of central autonomy over county councils ; Sir
Charles Dilke and Mr. Chamberlain were putting their
National Council scheme into workable shapo; Mr.. Gla-
dstone was burrowing underground as was his wont. r.
Parnell weighed everything and committed himself to
nothing. The Dilke-Cliamberlain scheme, which had the
advantage of being honest and Imperialistic, was re-
jected first, because it was too definite : its authors
were not “ squeezable." Then the Churehill-Car-
narvon - Salisbury offer was balanced against the
Gladstone intrigue, and the Tory combination kicked
the beam, for Mr. Parnell was confident that he
Grand Old Spider" and the “ Grand Old Windbag,” as he
used to call him in his hours of ease.
The last moves in the game were as exciting as a hard-
fought game of chess. There was the mysterious visit
of Mr. Parnell to Chester, of which Mr. Harry O’Brien
does not appear to have heard—and Chester is not far
from Hawarden. Not long after this a trusted emissary
arrived in Fleet-street, and next morning two kites were
flown -one from the Standard office, of all places in the
world, and the other from the National Press Agency.
The effeot was unexpected, for, while the Tory press
openly rejoiced that the stain of the “ Parnellite juice '*
was removed from their side, the Liberal press, from the
Daily News to the Mane Mer Guardian, declared that
the announcement was another infamous Tory trick, and
that their beloved and trusted leader would never be
guilty of such abject treachery as to “ surrender to Par-
nell. Tories could do such things, but, thank God, there
was one honest and high-souled party in Kngland, and so
long as Mr. Gladstone and Sir William Harcourt spoke
for that party there would be no compromise with treason
and outrage."
In face of this unanimous outburst, Mr. Gladstone
cautiously drew back a little and asserted that the so-
called agreement was all moonshine. He declared for-
mally that it was “ not an accurate representation of my
views, but,.l presume, a speculation upon them.” Mr.
Parnell had no documentary evidence, so he had to
submit in patienoe to this impudent disavowal. But he
knew his man. Parliament met in January, 1886.
Mr. Gladstone—in the interests, of course, of the
higher morality—had determined to turn out Lord Salis-
bury, but he could not do it without the Irish vote, and
the Irish vote, unfortunately, had to he bought. Mr.
Gladstone, who had hitherto taken care not to commit
himself to writing, had to make an advance. So a certain
professorial Radical was chosen as the emissary. He w as
a foolish creature, and one who could easily be repudiated
if necessarv. Mr. Gladstone wrote out the heads of his
proposed Home Rule Bill, and the document was entrusted
to the emissary, with solemn injunctions that it. was not,
under any circumstances, to be allowed out of his hands.
The unhappy man undertook the commission with a
light heart. He was irresistible at “mothers’ meetings,”
and he thought he could manage a mere Irishman.
The emissary approached the Chief, and began to formu-
late the clauses. “ Where are your credentials?” coldly
inquired Mr. Parnell. The professor tremblingly fingered
the all-important document. Mr. Parnell reached forth,
took the holograph, and deposited it in his inside pocket,
ostentatiously buttoning up his coat. “Oh,” cried the
emissary', in alarm, “ you cannot do that! I must not
let the document out of my hands.” Neglecting the ob-
vious retort, Mr. Parnell replied, “ Do you suppose that I
can give you an answer now, on so serious a matter? I
must take this paper away and read it carefully.” And
so the “Grand Old Spider” was trapped, and Mr. Par-
nell, having for the first time Mr. Gladstone’s surrender
to Home Rule in his pocket in Mr. Gladstone’s own hand-
writing, was master of the situation.

The preface to Henry Arthur Jones’s play,
“ The Tempter,” which Messrs. Macmillan have
published, resolves itself mainly (says the Academy)
into an examination bv Mr. Jones of the critical
capacity of Mr. Archer. Mr. Jones does not
admire Mr. Archer —positively. Negatively he
does. He writes : “I know of no critic who can
be safely trusted to arrive at a wrong effect with
so much precision and honest, painstaking effort
as Mr. William Archer.” This is hard; but there
is worse. Thus:
Anyone who lias followed the career of Mr. Archer’s
especial prattgi* must have noticed that they all come to
certain failure and misfortune, as surely as Sartgrado’s
patients ail died of fever. And a dramatist who wishes
for a modest- amount of success with his public may well
be alarmed lest in some unlucky moment. Mr. Archer may
lav hands on him and discover his plays to be master-
pieces. Arid if this be thought to be impossible in my
own case, I can only again refer to the astonishing list o
plays which he has discovered to he masterpieces. I
declare that not one of us is safe from him. And any
playwright who is placed in this precarious position may
well consider bow he may discourage Mr. Archer, and so
escape the disaster of his praise. But indeed I have no
such Belfish motive, and my only thought is to take Mr.
Archer out of the mean little by-ways where he lias groped
so tong, and to set him with his face forward on the main
highway of the drama’s advancement.
So much for Mr. Archer, who knows well
enough how to defend himself. Mr. Jones then
turns to the poets—
And here (he says] will he a fitting place to acknowledge
* tlje ii nmense debt which onr icnasceut English drama
owes to modern English men of letters and poets.
There is scarcely an English poet or man of letters
of the present, anti passing generation who lias not
generously gixen much valuable leisure to teach
EngliSh playwrights how not to write a play.
I have never seen any public acknowledgment
of this debt, on t lie part of the drama, and 1
mil glad that it falls to me to assure all these
eminent literary gi-nUeman and poets that.their labours
have not been in vain at hast, so far ns one playwright
is concerned. If 1 do not go into details and part icular
instances, it is because a sympathetic insijit at once per-
ceives that, in spite of a vast diversity of style and theme
and treatment, the so-called literary plays of the lasi
forty years are really animated by one sovereign aim anc
impulse—to show English playwrights what to avoid
And I claim on behalf of the Victorian literary drama
that it has splendidly accomplished this purpose.
Finally, says Mr. Jones in his very diverting de
fence: “For many years I have been in grea’
peace about the future of my soul. I am in equa
p.-acc about the quite minor question of my future
place in the English drama. ”
“P. Lnftig” writes regarding Tennyson anc
“the glorification of the commonplace ”
The ism tic temperament is essentially androgyn
ous. The woman’s sensitiveness, intuition, lier
vous refinement, join with the reserve power anc
creative vigor of the man to form the artist. As
those or these predominate, we have the major
strain or the minor appeal for human sympathy
and the proffer of it. To my mind the feminine
e train is the principal element in the Temiysonian
composition ; the masculine chord is merely sub
sidiary, Tennyson appeals to the softer emotion
[he is a great favorite with the women-folk); he
moves us continually to a gentle love or a gentle
pity, but his battle-cry and his war-blast always
struck me (making allowance for one or twoexcep
tions) as lining somewhat feeble.
Years and years ago Goethe wrote :—“ Per
sonality is everything in Art and Poetry, ” Jouber
emphasises Goethe's remark in this wise : IF
must have the man. ... It is human warmth am
almost human sulwtance which gives to all tiling
the quality that charms us.”- Yes, “ we must have
the man.” But in reading Tennyson I air
eternally impressed with the fact that in the
late Laureate we possessed not so much “ tin
man” as the (Hilished ninet - \ h century gentle
man, redolent of culture, pur,i,c and fine linen,
and dainty suggestions of lavender. The hold of
Burns on the Scottish heart is largely due to
the fact that you can hear a rugged bosom
beating time to the tune he sings ; that kid gloves
and “A man's a man for a’ that” have nothing in
common : that the scent of the hay, and the smell
of the earth netv-tmiied by the plough, are con-
tinually rising to reiniu bus of the rugged peasant
who rolls us out his rugged song. ... I am not
asserting that Tennysjn cannot touch Mother
Earth at times—“ The Grandmother ” is saffi i<*nt
disproof of that: but through much of his w<
(The Princess, Maud, Idylls of the King) yon are
ever painfully conscious of the presence of The
Eternal Gentleman. At times von find him arm
in arm with bis feminine analogue The Eternal
Lady—and then the ejvectwjle becomes almost
overpowering.
Tennyson has been charged by certain critics
with “glorifying the common)>!aee.” In a
recent criticism, that beautiful lyric, “Break,
break, break,” was cited as an example of such
glorification. But there is nothing commonplace
about the sea breaking on the crags of a rock-
bound ooost, though it does bo every hour for
countless eenturioß ; there la nothing commonplace
about the “ eternal snows of Himaleh ”; there is
nothing commonplace about the stars of heaven,
though they shine in the vault every night to the
end of time. “The Commonplace” (with which
the idea of vulgarity is inseparably intertwined) is
entirely a thing of human creation. lam seri-
ously inclined to doubt if the “Glorification of the
Commonplace ” be a feat within the compass oi
human powers ; the most serious accusation that
can be hurled against Tennyson is that, in the
days of his decline, he did deliberately attempt to
“glorify the commonplacewith results truly
memorable. Do we not recollect a certain J übilee
poem running thusly :
You the Mighty, the Fortunate,
You, the Lord-Territorial, .
You, the Lord-Manufacturer.
All your hearts be in harmony,
All your voices in unison—etc.
This was gilding the idol ; this was painting the
Joss ; tins was glorifying the Commonplace—with
a vengeance !
With regard to Tennyson’s right to stand in the
first rank ofpoete it must be remembered that the
great poets have nearly always dealt with great
themes. To Homer, Goethe, Dante, Schiller,
Shakespere, Shelley, it was essential that they
should deal in mighty matters. Great passions,
gigantic ambitions, guilty loves, glowing, magnifi-
cent virtues, fierce crimes, were usually the day
of which they wrought their poetical children.
They did not always “avoid the unseemly,”
neither did they wallow in it; they did not
pursue the “lovely and the pleasant” for ever,
neither did they give it exclusive pride of place in
their House of Song. They did not strike their
lays that dainty decency might dance a inincing
measure to the tune. The bardic heart was big;
the pulse was strong; the voice was deep and stir-
ring-—and the song was in accordance.-' Nowa-
days the little cages with their green and gold are
all a-twittor with pretty warblings, while old
Parnassus slumbers steadily all unaware of the
puny band and its ]>etulaut piping. ... An
eminent French critic once remarked: “Tenny-
son—yes. He strikes the chord of passion —with
his gloves on /” . . . . Thews is a modicum of
truth in the accusation. Call him a dainty lyrist
if you will'; grant him all the praise due to superb
poetical technique ; admire his graceful simplicity
as much as you choose j there is yet something
lacking (shall I say of pristine vigour) in the Ten-
nysoman fibre that was ever present and dominant
in the song of those old
bards who died content on pleasant sward,
Leaving great v< rse unto a little clan.
And yet his breast was all vibrant with the breath
of melody. No one knew' better than Tennyson
how
Nature beats in perfect tune
And rounds with rhyme her every rune;
Whether she work on land or sea,
Or hide underground her alchemy,
Thou canst not weave thy siaff in air,
Or dip thy paddle in the lake,
But it curves the bow Of beauty there
And the ripples in rhymes the oar forsake.
But ’tie a far cry from rugged ante Norman times
(the late Laureate traced his descent way back to
King Alfred) to the 10th century, and if British
blood lias become slightly Attenuated in the long
transition, it is but natural that British song
should also now and again lose somewhat of its
former robustness. . . . Come hither, Budyard!
Thou at least art never a lotos-eater !
A WOMAN’S THOUGHT.
I am a woman—therefore I may not
Call to him, cry to him,
Ply to him,
Bid him delay not 1
And when he come* to me I must ait quiet:
Still as a stone—
All silent and oold.
If my heart riot—
Crush and defy it 1
Should I grow bold—
Say one dear thing to him,
All my life fling to him,
Cling to him—
What to atone
Is enough for my sinning I
This were the cost to me,
This were my winning—
That he were lost to me.
Not as a lover
At iast if he part from me,
Tearing my heart from me—
Hurt beyond cure,—
Calm and demure
Then must I hold me
In myself fold me—
Lest he discover ;
Showing no sign to him
By look of mine to him
What he has been to me-
llow my, heart turns to him,
Follows him, yearns to him, \
Prays him to love me.
Pity me, lean to me,
Thou God above me.
E. W. Gilder .
THE HARBOR OF DREAMS.
Only a whispering gale
Flutters the wings of the boat;
Only a bird in the vale
Lends to the silence a note,
Mellow, subdued, and remote :
This Is the t wilight Of peace,
This is the hour of release,
Free of all worry and fret,
Clean of all care and regret,
When like a bkd in its nest
Fancy lies folded to rest.
This is the margin of sleep;
Here let the anchor be cast;
llete in forgetfulness deep,
Now that the journey is past,
Lower t he sails from the mast.
Here i he hay of content,
Heaven and earth interbleat:
Here is the haven that lies
Close to the gates of surprise;
Here all like Paradise seems -
Here is the harbor of dreams.
Frank Sherman.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We Missed Each Other.
Somehow we missed each other,
Passed each other by unknowing ;
I who sought you, you who sought mo
With hearts that throbbed to hopes and fears,
Passed each other in the early going,
Missed each other in the early years.
Somehow we missed each other,
We poor bankrupt souls —thus sowing
A harvest that we rocked not of.
Now, others’ sorrows claim our tears,
Others call us in our later going,
Others hold us in our later years.
M.J.C.
Earth. End.
Last night I dreamed I saw the swollen sun
Burst into fragments in the shivering air ;
Huge gouts of fire flecked Earth, once green and
fair,
And of a million cities never one
Escaped fierce flames that seemed to leap and run
Like Furies, all-devouring everywhere ;
While the loud roaring of the seas did bear
Witness their own destruction was begun.
Then from all lands there rose a wordless wail:
Men and wild beasts together screamed and died;
Thousands leaped in the sea on which no sail
Was to be seen, nor would again abide ;
Until it seemed a fading planet drew
Out of the void, struck me, and I died too.
R. CRAWFORD.
Dream Death.
There is a hreath at midnight that comes in
Sad as a sigh, for then the Day is dead,
And the young Morrow doth his course begin
Sowing new dreams in many a dreamer’s head.
And there are two have waked in one dark bed
Just as the last stroke fades in lonely air,
And having whispered half-awake have sped
With silent feet into Sleep’s poppied lair.
She with the morning wakes, but he is gone :
Her tears and kisses are of no avail—
Perchance it was his good-bye murmured on
The midnight in Death’s visionary dale.
Ah, sorrow ! For she thought ’twas in Sleep’s
fairyland,
When in the dark he pressed her warm, soft hand.
R. CRAWFORD.
Coquette.
They call thee false. The fools, they know no
better.
Poor whining weaklings, how they vent their
wrath !
A smile, a tender word they thought could fetter
For ever to one flame my restless moth.
But happiness is fleet and will not wait ;
He who would keep must force the hand of fate :
For constancy is peace, and peace is—sloth !
Ah, sweetheart mine, what does it matter whether
The rust of time or chance shall snap the chain.
A driftless something brought us two together,
A sullen mood may sever us again.
Yet doubly sweet the joy of love’s caress,
That ever conscious of thy fickleness
Feels through its raptures ever-boding pain !
What do I care ? Afar my spirit ranges ;
Let faithful patience worship at a shrine.
I sally forth, myself a child of changes,
To take iny chance, and fiuht for what is mine ;
That love alone doth taste of perfect bliss,
That I must rend or ravish, kiss for kiss,
From those reluctant, lying lips of thine !
VON KOTZE.
Whom Shall We Trust?
Girls whose laugh hath a liquid sweetness,
Falling soft as a lute-song old,
Girls whose step has a fairy fleetness,
Girls whose heart hath a deep untold,
Girls whose hair hath a glint of gold,
Girls whose eyes are a starlit dew,
Ye whose love is a life’s completeness,
Whom shall we trust if ye be not true ?
Say—what hope if the love-light tender
Deep in the gentle eyes should fail ?
Say—what hope if the gleaming splendour
Born of your own white truth should fail ?
Say—what hope if the suppliant’s tale
Rise in vain to your orbs so blue ?
If ye tire of the dear surrender
Whom shall we trust if ye prove untrue?
Naught we need of a day-star clearer,
If ye smile at the dear home door ;
Naught we need of a sky brought nearer,
If ye love as ye loved of yore ;
Naught we care for a greener shore,
Naught we need of a. joy-land new—
Hearts, O Hearts, than the whole world dearer,
Whom shall we trust if ye be not true ?
Brisbane.
P. LUFTIG.
Saint Hubert.
M.L.
Comrades, to the woodlands come !
Thrice afar the tasselled horn
Pours a soul's elysiinn
Thro’ the white wake of the morn.
Thrice the buck has hearkened still,
Buried in the umber shades ;
Thrice the glebv-wandering rill
Answers ere the bugle fades.
Over yonder granite peak,
Circled with a fleecy film,
Leaps the glad sun’s flaming streak,
Kissing all his verdured realm.
Unpremeditated hymns
Pour from feather-throated choirs,
Every note with joy o’erbrims,
Every heart to soar aspires !
Thrice afar the tasselled horn
Pours a soul’s elysiutn
Thro’ the white wake of the morn—
Comrades, to the woodlands come !
HUBERT CHURCH.

Gentlemen. —I have tested two kinds of your Ruberoid, which I will refer to as A and B.
Ais the P& B Pile Covering; Bis the P& B Damp-proof Course.
Sample A expanded under a pressure of 10 tons per square foot from 6" x 6" to 6.02" x
6.02", and with 12 tons per square foot to 6.026" x 6.025". The latter pressure would be just
about reached in a stone building 150 ft. high
Sample B Dampcourse showed no alteration whatever under a pressure of 12 tons per
square foot.
In regard to porosity, both samples were subjected toa head of 10ft. of water for 20 hours,
and were perfectly watertight. Both were subjected also to pressure before testing.—Yours
faithfully,
(Signed) W. H. WARREN, Wh. Sc. M. Inst. 0.E., M. Am. Soc. 0.E.,

SATURDAY, FEBRUARY 18, 1899.
A Very Small Lion.
Two weeks have elapsed since the Premiers
fixed up their Federal compromise, and so
far hardly any of the old provincial clan have
been heard from. The irreconcilable finance
editor of the Telegraph, and one or two
foolish drapers, and the equally foolish
Slattery are about all of the old blatant
crowd that have lifted their voices up to
date. All the 99 horrors that they said were
going to bring utter, irredeemable ruin on
New South Wales are still in the Bill, save
two ; but, now that the capital is secured for
New South Wales and there is a prospect of
that province getting whatever cash advan-
tages there are in it, the other 97 horrors
don’t matter. They never really existed, and
the Want and Heydon push knew perfectly
well that they never existed ; but they served
well enough to fool the people with for the
time being—and politics are mostly premedi-
tated inaccuracy in any case. If the evi-
dence of the past week or two amounts to
anything, of all the lions that were in the path
last year there is only one remaining, and
it is a weak, stumpy lion whose roar sounds
very small and lonesome along the political
strand. The Sydney Telegraph’s financial
apparition is this solitary survivor. That
paper, up to time of writing, hadn’t com-
mitted itself either way in its editorial
columns ; it has carefully dodged the great
subject of the hour, and has devoted its
space to scathing comments on the North
Pole and things like that. But away in the
cool obscurity of the back part of the organ
its financial editor has served up again his
old mass of unreliable figures, showing that
Federation means ruin, and that the poor
old Braddon Blot has been at it again.
♦ * * •
The Telegraph’s small lion in the path is
an animal that has been absolutely refuted a
dozen times already. It has been shown
that the figures are wrong, and the lamenta-
tion is ridiculous, and the inference would
disgrace the arithmetic of the smallest Jew-
hoy in a city pawn-shop. But the Tele-
graph’s strength lies in the fact that it never
takes any notice of the disproof, but merely
tells its old snake-yarn over and over again
in practically the same words. Its bogey is
that, under the Federal scheme, it will be
necessary for the six States to raise
£8,000,000 or £12,000,000 or some other
impossible sum by customs and excise
revenue, and last week it set out to say it all
over again thus :
EXPENDITURE of THE COMMONWEALTH (SIX
n . STATES).
Customs department ... ... £250 000
Posts and telegraphs 2,100,000
Defence 700,000
Minor services 150 000
Maintenance of properties 150 000
Interest on works taken over at 3
per cent. ... ... _ _ 370 000
Interest on new debt (£4,000,000) . . 120 000
Cost of Federal Government,
courts, etc 300,000
Post REVENUE of THE COMMONWEALTH 4 ’ 140 ’ 000
Posts and telegraphs £2,070,000
Minor revenues 150 ; 000
£2,220,000
Deficiency 92 0 000
Here, then, with six States included, the Commonwealth
is shown to have a deficiency of £1,920,000, which it is to
raise from Customs and excise. But, under the Braddon
blot, ttmitsf raise four tunes that sum, or £7,680,000, so
as to return £5,760,000 to the States.
That this £7,680,000 would be required to cover the
absolute needs of the Commonwealth and the States can
at once be seen. The States at present need their Cus-
they now vieldT t 0 balance their accoun ts. What do
w , Customs and Excise.
New South Wales £1,530,000
Victoria 2,110,000
Queensland 1,325,000
South Australia 610,000
West Australia 1,015,000
Tasmania 370,000
•Add—Needsof the Federal Government ’ 720,000
~ £7,680,000 covers only the absouftTiieeds of
the States and the Commonwealth. The bookkeeping
arrangement involves the necessity of raising a revenue
far above £7,680,000. Taking the probable “ propor-
tions adopted by the late Finance Committee in Sydney
and modifying them equally so as to permit of the in-
elusion of Queensland, we have the following as the pro-
able contributions of the Commonwealth toward a
revenue from Customs and excise of £7,680,01X1 :
Proportion.
New South Wales ... 37 per cent. ... £2,840,000
Victoria 27 per cent. ... 2,075,000
Queensland _. 16 per cent. ... 1,230,000
South Australia ... 8 per cent. ... 615,000
West Australia ... 8 per cent. ... 615,000
Tasmania 4 per cent. ... 305,000
VT ... , 100 per cent. .. £7,680,000
Now, it is only necessary to take the case of New South
Wales to show that a much larger revenue than £7,680,000
would be needed under the book-keeping system. Our
present Customs and excise yield £1,530,000. * Then they
would yield £2,840,000, or £1,310,000 more. Reckoning
that our share of the Federal expenditure would be
£260,000, there would be, roughly, E 1,050,003 surplus to
return to New South Wales. This simply means that the
other five States would between them have deficiencies to
a similar amount. To claim those deficiencies from the
Commonwealth would raise the total requirements from
£7,680,000 to £8,730,000. If, however, that extra sum
were to be raised from Customs and excise, New South
Wales would be entitled to her share of the excess, and
thus we go on in an ascending scale far into the regions
of impossible tariffs. In its political aspect, the Bill has
been much improved. Financially, it is as hopeless as
ever.
* * * *
It all looks well enough, yet it isn't even a
decently plausible fish-yarn after all.
• • * .
The key-note of the whole affair is the
assertion that the Commonwealth has to raise
its whole £1,920,000 or more by customs and
excise taxation, and that, as three-fourths of
the customs and excise revenue lias to he
returned to the States, to get that sum it
must raise nearly £8,000,000 by means of a
Federal tariff. And as that assertion is
utterly untrue, and pure invention, and there
isn’t a single word in the Federal Constitu-
tion which lends the slightest color to it, the
whole garbled tale falls through, and the
bogey is a lost apparition. The Federal
Government needn’t raise a penny of its
revenue by customs and excise unless it
wants to, only that stupid lie had to be in-
vented to give the other lies something to
stand on. It can begin by levying a uniform
income-tax throughout Australia, in which
case New South Wales, Victoria, Tasmania
and South Australia would have to abandon
their local income-taxes. At present Queens-
land and Westralia have no income-taxes,
and when they were made to contribute under
this heading they would need so much the
less customs revenue, and two of the largest
difficulties would be removed. Then the
Commonwealth could go on to levy a
uniform land-tax, whereby New South Wales,
Victoria, South Australia, and Tasmania would
be compelled to abolish their local land-
taxes. At present neither Queensland nor
Westralia has any land-tax, and when they
contributed under this heading another part
wof the discrepancy would vanish. (Also Tas-
mania taxes land only in the £, and Vic-
toria only taxes estates worth over £2500
and provided they exceed 640 acres in area—
therefore all the valuable city lands escape
taxation, being below the 640-acre limit.)
Queensland and Westralia have by far a
larger customs and excise revenue than any
of the other States, and (unless they impose
land and income taxes) they need it all.
Therefore, if the Federal Government decides
(which it is 1000 to 1 it won’t he so foolish as
to do) that it will pay all its expenses out of
the fourth of the customs and excise revenue
it is allowed to retain, and will still hand
back to eacli province its proper share of the
balance, and will make that share large
enough to enable Westralia and Queensland
to keep afloat without land and income taxa-
tion, and to enable Tasmania and Victoria
to get through without increasing their land-
taxes, which are at present ridiculously low
—in other words, if the Commonwealth
Government is an absolute lunatic, as the
Telegraph always assumes il must he, then
the situation is just about hopeless.
* • » *
But if the land and income taxes are made
uniform throughout Australia on a reason-
ably high scale, the situation would bo this :
There would be no serious discrepancy in the
tariff needs of the six States. Westralia and
Queensland would pay much more in direct
taxation, and Tasmania and Victoria a good
deal more, and they would need less customs
and excise revenue than they do now, and a
uniform customs-tariff of £7,000,000 or less a
year would probably keep them all afloat.
The only provinces which are not likely to
produce more revenue under a uniform
Federal land and income tax than they do
now are New South Wales and South Aus-
tralia, and they are also, the provinces which
depend least on their tariff-revenue. If by
any chance the Federal Government decided
to impose a uniform land and income tax,
say on the S.A. principle, and let the pro-
vinces which now charge these taxes
for purposes of local revenue abolish
them, then the Federal Government could
live on this source of revenue alone, and
even have something over. And if. the
Federal Government is not the absolute
lunatic that the Telegraph, for the sake of
its argument, assumes it to be, it will do so,
for Australia wants uniform direct taxation
quite as badly as a uniform tariff. Then it
can return all the customs and excise
revenue to the provinces —there is nothing
in the Constitution Bill to prevent it doing so,
though the Telegraph always assumes that
there is in order to keep its theory afloat—
and a £7,000,000 tariff would leave none of
them in any serious difficulties. And two
that are likely to be in the worst situation—
Tasmania and Westralia—have always the
alternative of levelling up their probate
duties to the Victorian level. The fact that
some provinces need so much more Customs
revenue than others is almost wholly owing
to the fact that they raise so much less direct
taxation. The Federal Parliament has only
got to level up the direct taxation, or let the
States do it themselves, and the unanswer-
able problem is answered.
* • » *
Of course the financial situation under
the Federal Bill is quite impossible if a
sufficient number of things are assumed.
The Telegraph starts by assuming eleven of
them: (1) That the Commonwealth will
decide to pay all its expenses out of Customs
and excise ; (2) that it can’t possibly think
of imposing a uniform Federal land-and-
ineome-tax; (3) that it won’t dream of im-
posing uniform prohate-duties; (4) that it
won’t attempt any new forms of taxation
whatever; (5) that the two provinces which
have no land-and-income taxes can’t possibly
impose them themselves (Heaven only knows
why), but must getenough Customs revenue to
keep themafloat or hurst; (6) that the provinces
with small land-and-income taxes can’t raise
them ; (7) that the provinces with low probate
duties can’t possibly think of increasing
them, because they won’t know how to come
in out of the wet; (8) that because some
provinces produce more customs revenue per
head than others under different tariffs,
they must needs go on doing it when they
have all the same tariff; (9) that Tasmania,
because it needs £370,000 customs revenue
now, when it. is paying about £60,000 a year
off the old deficit, will need it just the same
after the deficit is paid off; (10) that, be-
cause New South Wales can do with
£1,530,000 customs and excise now, when it
has to charge about £BOO,OOO a year of its
ordinary expenditure to loans to avoid openly
going broke, will never need more than
that ; (11) that the Federal Parliament will
be the last possibility in the way of a fool,
and will always remain so. Assuming all
these things and possibly a few others, the
Telegraph’s despairing view of the situation
is perfectly accurate. But it takes a terrible
amount of assumption.
The Commercial Bank’s Complaint.
The boss magnate of the Commercial Bank of
Australia, at the recent half-yearly meeting of
that massive institution in Melbourne, took ex-
ception to some criticisms offered by this paper
on an important question. The said mag-
nate wasn’t particularly happy in his mode of
refuting The Bulletin’s remarks, his case (as
reported by the Melbourne Age) being as
follows (Bulletin’s italics): —
Some criticism had taken place with regard to the
absence from the balance-sheet of the hank of the
accounts of the Assets Company, and in order to remove
any misapprehension which might exist, he desired to
remind them that, although it was true that there was a
connection between the bank and the Assets Company,
as provided in the scheme of reconstruction, it was not
correct to say that the Assets Company and the bank
were one. The scheme of reconstruction adopted in
1896, which was accepted by the creditors and share-
holders of the bank, and sanctioned by the courts, pro-
vided for the entire separation of the assets of the old
bank from those of the new by transferring the former to
the Assets Company ; also, that certain liabilities were to
be transferred from the bank to the Assets Company,
which liabilities were to he provided for by the proceeds
of the assets upon realisation, the bank being responsible
in the year 1916 —that was 17i years hence—to make
good the deficiency (if any ) which might result from the
realisation. The assets thus transferred stood in the
books of the Assets Company at the present time at the
sum of £6,731,359, from which lias to lie deducted
£1,200,000, amount of the capital written off, and against
which assets were outstanding deposit receipts, for the
ultimate payment of which the bank was responsible,
amounting to £3,470,079. The differenoe between these
two sums—£2,26l,2Bo—represented the margin for depre-
ciation of these assets, which must take place before any
claim could be made upon the new bank under its guaran-
tee. This amount, of course, included the hank’s direct
interest in the Assets Company. Whether this large
amount of upwards of £2,250,000 would be sufficient to
cover losses, the exact amount of which was not yet ascer-
tained, was a question upon which the directors could not
at presen t express an opinion ; but in view of the improv-
ing condition of affairs in the colony, and considering the
long currency of the Assets Company’s deposit receipts,
the amount would appear to be ample, and should it be
so, there would be no liability attaching whatever to the
new lap.k under its guarantee to the Assets Companv.
When the new directors took the affairs of the bank in
hand, at the time of the reconstruction, they refused to
take any responsibility for the losses which occurred prior
to their acceptance of office, and they had consistently
declined throughout to place any valuation upon the
assets. In proposing the scheme of reconstruction, their
object was to remove the old assets from the bank, and
place aga nst them their appropriate liability, so as to be
able to show clearly the position of the bank itself, and
they confidently reaffirmed their opinion that this was
the only practicable course in the circumstances, and one
which would effect the best results for the interests of
all concerned. To invite the hoard, therefore, to amalga-
mate the affairs of the Assets Company with those of the
bank, and practically to compel them to value the old
assets, was to invite them to act against the whole spirit
and intention of the scheme of and that
without any advantage, so far as they were able to jud*?e.
But the most important reason for maintaining? the
separation between the Assets Company and the bank
arose from the fact that the contingent liability to
make up any deficiency in the amount of the deposit
receipts by realisation was not a claim which
was to rank pari passu with the liabilities of
the bank itself, and in the interests of the bank
it was desirable that he should emphasise this
point most clearly. By the scheme of reconstruction,
all the creditors of the new bank were entitled to the pay-
ment of the full amount of their claims against the entire
assets of the institution. . . . Their duty was to see, as
the directors of the Assets Company, that the utmost was
obtained for the old assets, and that the loss to the share-
holders of the bank, who ultimately must bear the whole
of the loss, whatever it might be, should be minimised.
In the opinion of the bank there could be no question
whatever as to the ultimate security and safety of the
creditors of the old bank who held the Assets Company’s
deposit receipts. He trusted that he had made the
position clear, that the bank and the Assets Company
were two separate and distinct institutions, that the
Assets Company had the security of the bank for any
shortage which might occur in the realisation of the
assets, and that that claim, if any, was only a second
charge upon the assets of the new bank.
* * • *
The Bulletin drew special attention to the
curious proceedings of the Commercial Bank of
Australia because it appears to have struck on
an idea whereby a big bank can publish its
balance-sheet without casting any light at
all on its affairs as far as they concern the
shareholders. If its idea came to be gene-
rally adopted, then the publication of accounts
might as well be dropped altogether, so
far as their object is to show how
the shareholders stand. And the dis-
claimer of Mr. Robert Harper leaves
things just as they were. When the Commer-
cial Bank reconstructed for the second time in
1896, it transferred nearly £6,000,000 worth of
its assets (admittedly its worst assets—the
legacy of the bad boom days) to an Assets
Trust. It also put into the Trust nearly
£4,000,000 of liabilities in the shape of deposits.
The Assets Trust has to earn enough, if it can,
to pay interest on these deposits, and its assets
have to sell for enough, if possible, to pay these
deposits at maturity. If there is a deficit, the
shareholders of the Commercial Bank have to
stand the loss just as much as if the Trust had
never separated from the Bank, and just as much
as if it was the Bank’s loss, and the fact that the
bill for the loss, if there is one, doesn’t actually
fall due till 1916, makes no difference. If there
is a profit, the shareholders of the Bank annex
it just as if it was the Bank’s own profit—which
it really is. Therefore the Assets Trust, for all
practical purposes, is as much a part of the
Bank as it ever was, despiie the alleged separa-
tion.
* * * *
Last half-year the half of the institution
which is called the Commercial Bank issued a
balance-sheet showing a profit of £53,000. The
other half—the concern which has millions
of liabilities and all the old doubtful assets—
issued not the slightest information as to its
solvency or the other thing. If it made £40,000
profit, then the Commercial Bank is doing ex-
cellently. If it just cleared itself, then the
Commercial is doing well. If it made £50,000
loss, then the institution is doing badly, and if
it made £BO,OOO loss, then this paper wouldn’t
like to fully describe the situation. That is all
there is about it ; and which hypothesis is
nearer the mark the accounts don’t show.
Last half-year the A.J.S. Bank made a profit
of only £266. That might easily mean that its
good new business made £40,000 profit, and
that it lost £39,734 in pursuing the realisation
of the bad assets handed down from the old
A.J.S. Bank. If it, like the Commercial of
Australia, put all these old assets and
a corresponding amount of deposits in-
to an Assets Trust, and published no
accounts of that allegedly separate institu-
tion, which would be really the same institution
all the time, then under these presumptive
circumstances it would show £40,000 profit in-
stead of £266—which would be a very great
difference and yet no difference at all.
* * * *
The Bulletin didn’t ask the Commercial
Bank management to “ amalgamate ” the hank
and the Assets Trust, therefore Mr. Harper’s
assurances that that can’t be done are a
wild flying-off the handle and have no bear-
ing on the case. It didn’t ask the Board,
either, to furnish an estimate of the present
value of the Trust’s assets, and there Mr.
Harper merely attempts to show that the
thing which this paper never asked him to do
can’t he done. The Trust gets a certain in-
come from its assets and it has to pay a certain
amount out of it as interest on deposits. And
this paper simply asked that it should publish
an account showing the revenue and expenditure
of the Trust (as the B.N.Z. Estates Co. does),
so that the shareholders could know that the
invisible losses of the Trust ’ (if there
are any), through insufficient revenue or
through realisation of securities which don’t
fetch so much as was expected, are
not swallowing up, or partly swallowing
up, or more than swallowing up, the visible
profits of the bank. It wasn’t much to ask,
for unless that account is published the issuing,
of the hunk’s balance-sheet casts no light at all
on the situation so far as the shareholders are
concerned. The Trust represents a contingent
liability to the Commercial Bank shareholders
of over £3,409,000, so it is quite as important
to them as the bank itself. The fact that the
present directors are not responsible for the
good or had situation of the Assets Trust
makes the responsibility of the shareholders-
not one whit the less. And the fact that the
present directors are not responsible for the
position of their Assets Trust makes it all the
easier for them to tell the whole truth about its
position. Also the circumstance that the short-
age (if any) doesn’t need to be actually paid till
1916. when the Trust’s deposits fall due. is no


reason at all why the shareholders shouldn’t
know what the shortage (if any) amounts to
now. If it is proposed to keep the whole
tiling dark for 18 years, and then tell the Com-
mercial Bank shareholders all at once that they
have come out square, or have made a profit of
.£500,000 or two millions, or that they have
lost all their capital and are practically ruined,
or something like that, it will be necessary to
fetch a row of ambulances to that meeting in
1916 to carry away that part of the proprietary
which falls down insensible under the shock.
It isn’t a question of valuing the unrealised
assets, but of explaining how the revenue and
the realised assets have got on up to now.
• * • •
What The Bulletin knows about the famous
Trust is no more than this, and it isn’t much.
For three half-years its accounts were pub-
lished, though in a very abridged form and in a
way which conveyed little information. There
was no statement of the revenue or the profit,
if any, or the loss, if any, or the amount which
the assets realised fetched above or below their
paper value. For three half-years there ap-
peared a bald statement which showed that the
assets, with the aid of <£83,000 transferred from
the bank’s profits, just balanced the liabilities
to an exact copper—not a copper more or less.
Then the supply of information stopped dead,
like Jonah’s gourd, and didn’t start again.
And why a statement of the Trust’s revenue
and expenditure, and of the losses or profits
made on the properties sold up to date,
shouldn’t be issued Mr. Harper doesn’t ex-
plain. Neither does anybody else. Supposing
these accounts are of a cheerful and reassuring
character, and calculated to boom up the shares
and make everybody feel good all over, their
non-appearance is still harder to account for.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Police, Press and Crown.
The approaching trial of Detective Dungey and
Mrs. Dora Bromley, at Melb. Criminal Court,
■on the charge of conspiring to invite a felony,
promises to result in a further conspiracy to
■quash the prosecution and hoodwink the public.
1 lie story of the case thus far is a contemptible
scandal. At the trial of the alleged abortion-
ists, who are now in gaol, Dungey and his
female “phizgig” laid themselves open to a
■criminal charge, and one Wicks, a relative of
■one of the prisoners, having vainly appealed to
tlie Crown Law Dept., succeeded in obtaining
from the Full Court an order for a Grand Jury.
The Full Court was practically compelled to
grant the order, and the Grand Jury
to the probable disgust of the Crown
Law Dept.— found a True Bill against
Dungey and Bromley aforesaid. The woman
Bromley had gone to Sydney (at whose ex-
pense ?) by the time she was required to
answer a first summons for her appearance at
S. Melb. Court, and the cost of bringing her
back devolved upon the private prosecutor,
Wicks. Evidently the Crown would do nothing
to assist the righteous course of the Law in this
case. Here is a committal for criminal trial
arising out of a Crown prosecution, but private
citizen \\ icks has to pay for bringing one of
the committed parties within the jurisdiction
of the court. Good—or, rather, Bad ! Dungey
and his alleged accomplice having been “ sent
up” by a grand jury, what happens next ? In the
first place, an Age paragraphist commented on
the deafness and feebleness of these jurymen in
a way which would give the public to suppose
that physical infirmities unfitted them for find-
ing a really True Bill, anyway. Comment on
the peculiar fact that none of the 23 empanelled
jurymen were under 45 years of age would have
been much more to the point. The empanel-
ling of juries is a police matter, and the result
in this instance was a collection of objects for
newspaper ridicule. Then came the Boot Trunk
Tragedy, providing the police authorities with
a welcome opportunity to put Dungey before
the public in the character of an active and
intelligent officer. The bogus hero of the
B.T. T was Dungey, the man whom Wicks
had true-billed. Any information that looked
valuable was put into the hands of Dungey,
and nothing came of it. Finally, when the
principals in the tragedy had been pointed out
to the police, Dungey was sent to arrest them.
Meanwhile, the trucklesome newspaper repor-
ters took up the parable, and talked of Dungey,
Dungey, Dungey every day. And they are
stili talking Dungey in connection with the
Boot-Trunk l ragedy and the big reward offered
for the discovery of the principals. Also,
Chief Commissioner Chomlky, or somebody,
has dragged in the suggestion that Dungey’s
trap for catching the alleged abortionists, has
practically put an end—forsooth ! to the abor-
tion business in Melbourne. The newspapers
are for ever publishing pars, to the glory of
Dungey whilst refusing to insert letters from
prosecutor Wicks. Wicks writes to The
Bulletin —“ Neither the Age, Herald, or
Argus will print one word from me. . . .
For the forthcoming case I desired an-
other barrister to assist, but two leading
men have refused point blank to take any fee
from me, as they say they cannot afford to
offend the police. Therefore we have against
us the Government, the best legal talent, and
the whole of the Vic. press. Oh, for an honest
jury to use their own brains and do what is
right!” By the way, the public must always
hear in mind that jurymen are summoned by
the police in ordinary cases. This is not an
ordinary case. In proceeding against Dungey
and the Bromley woman, prosecutor Wicks
has put the Police Dept, on its trial, and the
Department, as practical defendant, should not
be allowed to select the jury which is to try it.
Our G-reat Tree Country.
Last week at Bourke (N.S.W.), an allegedly
civilised town situate in a dry part of this free
and enlightened country, the humane Cornstalk
law sent two Afghans to gaol for refusing to
work when ordered by their slave-driver, the
local Carrying Co. These two Afghans and
some 40 others had signed an agreement
written in a foreign language. It set
forth that they all agreed to work for the
Carrying Co. for six years at JJ24 a year and
their food. They were to get £6 a year cash,
and the other £JB a year —£108 in all—was to
be paid them at the end of their term, pro-
vided they finished it. If they didn’t finish it
—if the agreement was broken when there was
even one day of the six years to run—the
money was apparently forfeited to the boss
company, every penny of it. The men couldn’t
leave their employment till the six years were
finished without being gaoled for breach of
contract, and making themselves liable to
lose the balance of their wages, but
the boss co. could sack them any time
at a month’s notice. Then the three-fourths of
their wages which were retained by the boss
would seemingly be forfeited to the boss be-
cause the men, through being sacked by the
boss, didn’t finish their term. They were to be
fed on such food as the boss considered suffi-
cient—just as much or little of it, and as good or
bad as the boss thought fit; and if they left
work in consequence it constituted a breakage
of the agreement and involved liability to
the same old forfeiture of wages. And they
were to work seven days a week and have no
holidays whatever. The boss of the co., an
interested party, swore that this atrocious
agreement was faithfully interpreted to the
men by the Government interpreter. The
Government interpreter, who ought to know,
and who isn’t an interested party, and who is
presumably a reliable man else he wouldn’t
hold such a billet, alleged that he didn’t faith-
fully translate the agreement to the miserable
Afghans before they signed it. He said
that lie attempted to do it, but owing to
the various interruptions of the boss, he
wasn’t able to. One of the interrup-
tions was at the place where the co. was to
hold back three-quarters of the miserable wage
for six years, with practically unlimited powers
to forfeit it altogether, so probably that astound-
ing condition wasn’t properly understood by
the two aliens. Also, there were several other
interruptions. In short, this Government func-
tionary declared that the agreement wasn’t
properly explained, while the interested pro-
secutor said it was. Then an utterly incredible
magistrate elected to believe the prosecutor,
and passed sentence —10s. or 14 days. The
penniless niggers took the 14 days, after one of
them had tried to knock out his brains against
an adjacent fence. It was their second or third
term of imprisonment for the same kind of
offence ; for after being in gaol they still re-
fused to go back to their nigger-driver, so they
were prosecuted again, and apparently the
white man’s law may go on sending them to
gaol by instalments for the next three or four
years. The magistrate’s explanation was that
“ the men had signed the agreement and must
abide by it.’" This logic would apply equally
well if the bond had specified that the Afghans,
in their ignorance, were to work all their lives
for nothing; to all intents and purposes the
Bourke magistrate’s decision, if it is good law
(which it certainly isn’t), establishes nigger-
slavery in N.S. W.; therefore, The Bulletin is
of opinion that the decision is a disgrace to an
allegedly free country, and the giver of it
can’t be fittingly described by any language
now available. And it wants to know if all
that the people of N.S.W. have striven for—
their ballot, and manhood suffrage, and Re-
sponsible Government, and their Labor-party,
and their free speech, and their cry about the
most liberal Constitution in the world—all end
in N.S. W. being nigger-driver to the miserable
Asiatic who gets J£6 a year cash and any
victuals his boss thinks fit for 365 days’ work
per annum for six years—hours apparently un-
limited ? The men, by the way, allege that they
were starved and beaten, and that even their
miserable £6 a year wasn’t paid. An original
agreement under which they were engaged in
India wasn’tproduced (the co. refused to produce
it and the court said it should be handed in, but
didn’t press for it), so the merits of the case
were not really inquired into at all. Ap-
parently there were two agreements, and the
co. was allowed to produce the one that suited
it best. And the magistrate, in a burst of
cheerful irony, said that the penniless niggers
—who know as much of the forms of Aus-
tralian law as a hen does of Sanscrit—could
carry their case to a higher court, and he
threatened a heavier sentence next time. What
is the N.S. W. Government going to do with
that kind of magistrate? Also, will any
M.L.A. introduce a bill to nullify the whole
batch of agreements—if the Supreme Court
can’t be successfully invoked in the matter —
and thus end the miserable business ?
One Boiler—and One Boy.
A FEW days ago a boiler burst at an Albury
(N.S.W.) flour-mill, and killed a boy, and in-
jured other people, and did great damage to
the town. Some evidence about that boiler
transpired at the inquest. Samples herewith
(Sydney Daily Telegraph report):—
Frederick Hayes, one of the firm of Hayes Bros., flour-
millers, gave evidence that the boiler was built in Bal-
larat in 1879. It was used at a flour-mill at Jindera for
three years, when it came into possession of the firm of
.1. and J. Hayes. The boiler was a good one. There had
been a patch over the fire-box, which witness’ father had
cut out, and a fresh one rivetted on two years ago. There
was a leak in front of thg fire-box, and a new angle iron
was placed in front. This was the last of the repairs that
had been done to the boiler. The boiler had been worked
at 601 b. pressure, and he thought it would have been safe
to work it at 801 b.
John Laughton, the engine-driver, deposed that he did
not hold a certificate. Two years ago he thoroughly
examined the boiler by chipping and scraping, and
reported to the last witness that the boiler was in a
satisfactory condition. It had not been examined since.
An hour before the explosion the engine was started with
551 b. steam, and 15 minutes afterwards one of the belts
slipped off. Some delay took place, and witness looked
at the gauge, which showed 601 b. of steam. Witness
raised the damper, and balf-an-hour afterwards, just as
the belt was fixed, the explosion took place. The pres-
sure then would, perhaps, be over 601 b. In witness’
absence, the engine was looked after by mill hands. No
one was in charge of the boiler while witness was away
repairing the belt.
T. Walter, practical engineer, gave evidence that 11
years ago, when the boiler was being set, he examined it
and formed the opinion that it was weak, insufficiently
stayed, and made of inferior material. He thought it
was not safe to work the boiler at a pressure of over 361 b.
He did not examine the boiler again until after the
explosion. He might have told Frederick Hayes that he
did not consider it was safe to work the boiler as high as
he was working it, which was from 601 b. to 701 b. Only a
few weeks ago witness said, “ When are you going to get
a new boiler?” Hayes did not then make any reply. As
a practical engineer, witness gave it as his opinion that
the boiler had been unsafely worked for the past 11 years.
Witness had had 40 yeuiV experience as an engineer.
Joseph Matthews, builder arid contractor, reported that
he saw the boiler 20 yearn ufjo at Jiuderu when beitiKuned
in a flour-mill. At that time the fire-box blew out, and the
platen buckled. The boiler was repaired. Witness was
fully convinced that since then the boiler was unsafe.
John Smith, late employe of Hayes Bros., gave evidence
that Richard Hayes, one of the firm, cautioned him not
to let the steam rise over 6ulb., and that if he did he
was to draw out the wood and put down the damper.
Richard Haykh said if the steam got over 60lb. the boiler
would burst. This was in 1897. Witness was cautioned
several times in the same way. The boiler was not cleaned
out during the 18 months witness was in the employ of
the firm. At Frederick Hayes’ instructions he examined
the hues 18 months ago, and, as a result, witness informed
him the flues were getting thin. Mr. Hayes then said,
“In that case, we’ll have to get a new boiler.” In wit-
ness's opinion, there was too great a weight on the
safety valve, and witness told Laughton that he did not
think it safe. Laughton said that the boiler was weak in
places, and that some day it would blow up. The boiler
was sometimes worked up to 901 b.
John Laughton, recalled, contradicted the last witness
as to the statement that he (Laughton) had said that the
boiler would blow up some day.
Robert Carroll, an employe, said that Richard Hayes
told them that some day the boiler would burst, and blow
them off the face of the earth. He did not know whether
Hayes spoke jocularly or not, but one of them said he
was afraid to go to bed until late that night.
W. D. Cruickshank, engineer and surveyor to the
Marine Board, deposed that he had inspected the scene of
the explosion. The boiler was of poor material and
design, and badly constructed. From an examination
he made of the furnace that morning, the boiler was not
fit to work at all. The reason was that the shape of the
furnace was two or three inches out of the circle, and its
strength was regulated by’ the shape of the furnace. It
was not safe. Speaking generally it was absolutely
perished. What was left was very much cry’stalised, and
little or no fibre was to be seen in any broken sections.
There were a large number of cracks and laminations
about the furnace. He had no hesitation in stating that
the material was the worst he had ever seen. He was
quite satisfied that if any experienced inspector had ex-
amined the boiler he would have had no hesitation in
condemning it. He could not tell the primary’ cause of
the explosion, but thought it was due to the weakness
of the furnace and sheer weakness of the material, which
was as brittle as oast iron.
He could not attribute any blame to Hayes Bros., or
Laughton, simply because, in his opinion, they did not
know anything about it. (Laughter.) Hayes Bros,
having worked the boiler for 11 years, might be justified
in believing that it was safe, but if any competent person
had inspected it, it would have been condemned.
Then the jury brought in a verdict, not of man-
slaughter, but “accidental death.” Of course,
every flour-mill owner knows that every boiler
will burst some day if he keeps it in use long
enough, and that the fact that he has worked
it 11 years doesn’t justify him in believing it is
safe—it only justifies him in being dead-sure
that it isn’t nearly so safe as it was 11 years
previously. Also, if he has worked it 22 or 33
or 44 or even 100 years, that wouldn’t justify
him in being confident of its safety either.
Anyhow, the verdict was accidental death.
And here ends the tale of the 20-year-old,
second-hand boiler that burst twice, and was
bossed by an uncertificated engineer, and hadn’t
been cleaned out for 18 months or examined for
two years, and whose condition, so it is
solemnly averred, was so well-known to one at
least of the owners that he talked about its
probable bursting on more than one occasion.
Not manslaughter, but accidental death ! When
is that long-lalked-of Land Boilers Act going
to eventuate ?

Circumstantial Evidence Again.
Burgess, arrested for the Gatton outrage,
escaped hanging, amid-general malediction, by
a hair’s breadth. Had his statement not been
borne out by people who handed him a drink
of water (at a place 26 miles from the scene of
the tragedy) within an hour of the appearance
of “ the man at the slip-rails,” then nobody but
Burgess and the actual assassins would have
believed him innocent, and, on the eve of his
execution, most people would have remarked
that hanging was too good for him. Let us
look at the grip the gallows had on Burgess.
He was an ex-sailor, tramp, stranger, his body
scarred with knife and bullet wounds, and he
had, as he confessed, been “ in trouble all his
life.” He was positively identified by several
as “ the man at the slip-rails.” There were
blood-stains on his clothes. He filled the police
requirements of “a suspicious character.”
There was ample “contributory identifica-
tion ” as the man in the neighborhood
on and about the date of the tragedy.
He resisted arrest; he “performed” in his
cell ; and his language was terse and
“ terrifying” at times. He perpetrated many
jokes, and a few of his remal ks were flippant.
He changed his position frequently during the
identification farce, and actually had the in-
solence to snatch the hat off one of the dozen
who stood with him to be scrutinised by the
witnesses. All this tightened the hemp round
Burgess’ neck, but the noose was cast off when
the alibi got to work. Now, when it is recol-
lected that Burgess was wandering through
the bush in search of work by himself; that
for days and nights he was camped alone, un-
noticed by any passer-by, because it was his
habit to “cut across country that it was
quite possible, and even probable, that he
might have camped on Boxing-night, not near
a house, but in the seclusion of the bush, and
remained in the bush unobserved all Boxing-
day, it is pertinent to ask'what kind of a show
had he, barring one fortunate accident. By
the accident of his calling for that drink at
Greenmount on an easily-identifiable date, a
holiday, and at an unusual hour, after 8 p.m., he
established proof of his innocence. What now
is the value of circumstantial evidence in any
future prosecution over the Gatton business?
The testimony that was tendered to fit Burgess
into the tragedy will then be applied to some
one or others. Witnesses who positively iden-
tified Burgess as “ the man at the slip-rails ”
will be asked to identify another man in his
stead, and to be just as positive about him as
they were over the other suspect. And they
probably will do it. Once more : the average
identifier —the person who is prepared to cheer-
fully swear to anybody as the man he saw just
once, for a second, in deep shadow—deserves
ten years. And some day, under a better legal
system, he will get it.
