The 58 Nominees.
The Reid Government has decided to call a
special session of N.S. W. Parliament on Feb.
21, to consider the Federation Bill—which is
undoubtedly the right thing to do. And it has
decided not to make any new appointments to
the Council before then—which is a proceeding
of doubtful wisdom. When Barton and R. E.
O’Connor left the Upper House, very little
Federal enthusiasm remained in it. The fossils
who stay behind are cold on the subject of
Australian union; they are aggravated because
a partial referendum is left in the Bill, and
they are also inclined to kick because their
scheme for fixing the capital in Sydney right
against their own doors, where it would help
to enhance the value of their own bricks and
mortar, didn’t come off. And they loathe
the idea of referring the Bill to the people.
The Bill must be passed as it is, with no alter-
ation whatever, and referred to a popular vote
with no minimum clause to hamper or nullify
the people’s verdict, if it is to have a fair show',
and the present crowd in the House of Unpaid
Nominees may be the crowd to do this—but it*
doesn’t look like it. There are only 58 fossils
now, against 76 in ’93, so there is room to send
up a dozen reliable Federationists to make
the position reasonably safe. Entrusting the
Federal Constitution to the present Council
W'ould be a very interesting gamble, if there
was nothing more than a copper or a drink at
stake.
If the Thing had been that Wasn’t.
A weird person, one Henry Joseph, writes to
the Sydney papers to point out an allegedly
shocking circumstance in the Federal Constitu-
tion. To alter that Constitution—though there
isn’t much in it that is likely to cal! for altera-
tion—it is necessary to take a popular vote and
get (1) a majority of the whole number of votes
recorded, and (2) a majority in most of the
States. And Joseph wants to know what
would happen if the vote panned out like this :
For Alteration. Against Alteration.
N.S. Wales ... 300,000 ... Nobody
Victoria ... 300,000 ... „
Queensland ... 100,000 ... „
S.A. ... ... Nobody ... 1
Tasmania ... ~ ... 1
Westralia ... ~ . ... 1
700,000 ... 3
In this case there would be 3 votes against the
alteration of the Constitution and 700,000 ; for
it, yet the 3 would win because there w’asn’t a
majority of States on the other side. Which
looks very terrible indeed. And yet, just con-
sider this equally awful and equally impossible
possibility which has been hanging over
N.S. W. all its life without anyone even notic-
ing the fact. There are 125 constituencies in
N.S. W. returning one member each, and sup-
pose that the voting at a general election was
as follows :
Votes for Votes for
Protection. Freetrade
124 constituencies. Only
one man votes in each. All
vote for Protection. 124
Protectionists returned. ... 124
One constituency. 2700
votes recorded, all for Free-
trade. One Freetrader re-
turned 2700
124 2700
N.S.W. is actually living under a system which
makes it possible for 124 voters to return 124
members while 2700 voters return only one.
And Henry Joseph, in his anguish about the
much smaller Federal discrepancy, has never
even noticed this.
♦
According to Sydney Telegraph “it is only
among supporters of the old Convention Bill ”
in N.S.W. that the new Federal scheme is re-
ceived with favor. Well, the supporters of the
old Convention Bill in N.S.W. were 5367 more
than its opponents, and so it seems that it is
“only” the majority that is in favor of the
new scheme. But the Telegraph’s contempt
for the majority, when it isn’t its majority, is
something interesting. The only thing equally
sublime in the way of dignity that The Bulle-
tin remembers was an episode it once saw in a
monkey cage at the Zoo. There were a dozen
rushing, eager little spider monkeys in a big
enclosure ; also, there was an anubis baboon
hunched up on top of a tree-stump in the
centre —silent, clumsy, half-asleep and pro-
foundly grumpy. Keeper put a big dish of
rice into enclosure, and the spider monkeys
rushed it with an enthusiastic clatter and
waded in. After a while the grumpy baboon
slowly reached himself down from his stump
and approached with contempt on his visage to
see what kind of viand the majority was making
all the row about. Twelve spider monkeys fled
in all directions and left him in possession. He
looked at the rice; evidently remarked, “So
this is it,” picked up a sample in his hand and
disapproved of it on close inspection. Then
that minority of one baboon solemnly dabbed
the handful of rice on and about the root of his
tail, and straddled up his tree again. It was
all he said, but it was obviously meant to convey
that he didn’t think much of the majority.
And the Telegraph, \yith the old Convention
Bill on the root of its tail, is there or there-
abouts.
Miss Catherine H. Spence writes to The
Bulletin from Norwood, S.A. :
Dear Bulletin.—ln my recently-published pamphlet,
“ Effective Voting : Australia’s Opportunity,” &c., occurs :
—“The ideal Upper House in a truly democratic country
would be one elected by adult suffrage, by proportional
representation, by all the people in it.” I read something
similar in The Bulletin, with the important omission of
“by proportional representation.” If such a house is
elected by the multiple vote (that is by the scrutin-de listc
imposed on us for the Federal election) all the members at
the top of the poll would be of one party. This w-as not
seen in its absolute logical form in the Federal election,
because people did not stick to the ticket closely except
in Victoria. But in a real Upper House election the
Labor Democratic Party would bring forward a full bill
and the Conservative Capitalistic Party would do the
same. All outside parties would be negligible factors,
quite hopeless. This is clearly seen in the U.S. elections
of presidential electors, when the 36 men from New York
and the 17 for Ohio are all either Democrats or Republi-
cans. Again, the multiple vote in the Swiss cantons has
caused so much dissatisfaction that nearly half of them
have adopted proportional representation by the Freiheit
system, an inferior method to that of Hark, but which
gives a fairer proportion of members to all the leading
parties; and the division of shades of Conservatism and
varieties of Liberalism improves discussion without pre-
venting the real rule of the majority. If Mr. Reid desires
to create an Upper House representing the colony as a
whole, as a counterpoise to the “roads and bridges”
M.L.A., this proportional method is the line he should
take. For the Federal Senate, which must be elected
from each colony as a whole, the multiple vote would be
disastrous in the extreme. Since Switzerland w r as con-
stitutionally federated she has devised many methods for
the preservation of cantonal rights and individual liberty,
whereas the U.S. has allowed these rights to be en-
croached on in all directions. Government by Injunction
is a strange phase of democratic government. I was
sorry to hear so little about the Swiss republio in the
Federal debates.

Ihe long-expected “Geisha” turned up
at Her Majesty’s (Sydney) in the middle of last
week and boomed so decisively that there are no
decent seats available in the d.c. for fully a week
ahead. And the present scribe, being soured with
much writing of the “ Wild Cat Column,” gropes
dniily in the mists of poetry for the exact reason
of this furore. There is very little plot to hang
trie song and frill and flounces of the “Geisha”
upon. Somewhere in the dreamy haze of old
Chrysanthemum Land there is a tea-house run by
a funny Chow ; and sundry Jap singing-girls, and
a British _ officer who is mashed on the prettiest
singing-girl when he ought not to be, because he
is already engaged to pretty Dorothy Vane, and a
huge Japanese peer who revokes the license and
orders the girls to be sold by auction so that he
can buy the Briton’s jjerky little songstress. And
illogical Dorothjr, being all broken up by her
lover s misconduct, disguises herself as a geisha, and
auctions herself to the big Jap, and is very sorry
for it afterwards. She didn’t tnink that anything
was real in the land of the brown crockery towers,
where the small brown man is vaguely supposed
to be a dream alleviated by tea. and she finds that
her sale 1- very real indeed. The “Geisha” is a
small bust ;ug trifle with very little to stand upon
except the hilarious proceedings of Chinaman
Wun-Hi. baun has apparently built up the
comedy of \\ un-Hi on his own, for that frantic
heathen is verv unlike the rest of the book. The
dialogue, apart from Chinaman Wun, is reasonably
bright in places, and some of the songs are pretty
enough, though there isn’t one which seizes the
public by the liair, so to sjieak, and won’t let it
sleep because the refrain keeps moaning in its ear
all night. Also, some of the songs are only
commonplace. And there is much sweet music,
and some pleasant, sleepy music, and music
that sounds a little like “ Banjo’s ” river sing-
ing on its bars, and there is a little music
which is quite plain and of small account; but there
isn t one music that stands out conspicuously above
the rest.
* • • •
Still, by virtue of what music there is, and
the fine staging, and the girls and costumes and
the Chinaman and other things, the “ Geisha ” is
evidently going to be a big success. There isn’t a
J a P- Kiri in the whole crowd that the average sin-
ful man wouldn’t adopt so that he could teach her
the blessing? of civilisation. And Japan is a whole
mass of picturesque coloring, and when the
skimpy little tale drags, which it does frequently,
the clothes help it out to a wonderful extent.
The company, too, is more than worthy of the
clothes and the scenery'. Miss Dorothy Vane’s
bright smile—-the one that she certainly isn’t
going to deposit in a convent —and her acting, and
her voice, and her electric energy, more than com-
pensate for the fact that her dancing is a rather
promiscuous kind of hop. Rose Musgrove can
stand on her own merits now as a promising
actress, quite apart from the fact that she is the
Firm’s daughter; and Florence Perry fills the
clothes of the bunchy little Geisha admirably,
though her singing voice is a rather small voice at
best. Lauri is a roaring success all through ; and
Howard Vernon does about the best that is pos-
sible with a solemn, ill-fitting character. Pauli,
as the nominal hero, lifts his pleasant vocal organ
in a part that isn’t noticeably larger than a lot of
other parts; and the pretty and clever Lily
Titheradge is fitted into a boy’s suit and a charac-
ter that is far smaller than her deserts. P.
Bathurst, the gentleman who has hopes of becom-
ing the Federal capital, has little to do except
roam about in a broken heart and a spiked hat.
Various other jieople are unavoidably postponed,
but even the worst of the crowd calls for no
special obloquy. Among them the spidery little
item keeps up wonderfully, considering how
spidery it is.
•* * *
The “ English Rose ” started business at
Sydney Royal on Saturday—“ When London
Sleeps” having come to an end, owing to the
sudden breaking-down of the bed, or from other
causes. The present drama isn’t quite new, and
it isn’t likely to boom like its predecessor.
It is an Irish story, with the somewhat weary old
motive—the one which Marion Crawford dishes
up, with a slight variation, in his “ Corleone ”
about the holy father who has the story of the
murder told to him under the seal of confession,
and then secs his brother arrested and condemned
on wrongful suspicion, and feels that he must
burst with the anguish of his secret, yet daren’t
burst, because, if he did, the dreadful fact would
drop out, and, if it dropped out, his sacred calling
would be for ever disgraced. In “ Corleone,
Marion Crawford improved on this idea by making
the murderer confess his crime to the priest and
then give the priest himself in custody on the
charge. In the “ Soggarth,” the good old wheeze
is dished up again—the writer forgets who was
arrested there, but anyhow his reverence knows,
under seal of confession, that the wrong man
is going to lie hanged, and, as he goes
about with this load on his soul, the real
criminal scoffs at him from behind the shebeen, or
the dhudeen, or something. The idea is intensely
dramatics—only it is growing just a trifle worn.
And the difficulty lies in clearing up the matter
afterwards. In the “English Hose ” the real mur-
derer dies suddenly while walking home, and
confesses as he passes away. In “Corleone” he
also dies, and Saracinesca extracts the facts out
of him in his last moments. The old “Soggarth ”
has the best ending. There the assassin, in his
desire to rub it into his reverence, is beguiled at
last into repeating his statement when the seal of
confession is not on and the praste isn’t securely
closed uj) by the cork of his holy calling, so to
speak. Whereupon his reverence falls on him like
a wild cat in his private capacity, and has him
run in.
• * * *
Still, the “ English Rose ” is a pleasant,
healthy drama, with a breeze from the Irish
mountains running through it, and it has no
bright blue past in it anywhere. There is no
sex-problem, or fallen female, or unauthorised
infant, or haggard mother coming home after a
life of sin to ask the old man to let her look
through the window just once at a moderate ren-
tal, and see her child again before she goes away
and arranges to be fatally run over by a cart.
There isn’t even an eviction or an informer. The
ruined Irish jintleman is in good health and spirits,
and the new English landlord is only shot as a
matter of absolute necessity because he is going to
overhaul the agent’s accounts. Any agent might
be driven to shoot the landlord under like condi-
tions. The pictorial effects are splendid, and
the company is medium. The new English
landlord will be severely dealt with if he
again comes before the court on a simi-
lar charge. J. P. O’Neill, a gentleman of
immense circumference and one of the brightest
Irish comedians in Australia, calls for enthusiastic
graise, and after him Hans Phillips, William
ourley and C. R. Stanford deserve a kindly
word. Misses Eugenie Duggan, Ida Gresham and
Alice Deorwyn all look nice and play their parts
passably. Miss Amy Gourlay, who made such a
large-sized effect in the last production, has no part
worth speaking of; and Inman is very good in
spots. But nobody, barring O’Neill aforesaid,
makes a very conspicuous success, and- his
Reverence is a painful subject that is better
dropped.
* * * *
The Tivoli show is in fair health and con-
dition, and Lieutenant Travis has become as much
of a boom as a very clever professor of his some-
what hackneyed art well can be. Biondi’s benefit
hadn’t yet eventuated when this was written, and
will be over before this issue is in the hands of the
public, but the writer believes it came off with
peat success to-morrow, and that it is going to be
(or to was) one of the notable events of
the season. Looking partly back at it and
partly forward to it, it was a brilliant affair
(provided nothing happens to prevent it coming
off), and he can say conscientiously that he
thoroughly enjoyed himself, always supposing that
he isn’t detained at the office to-morrow. Any-
how, he was there if he is able to get away, and
Erovided the theatre didn’t get burned down
efore the event happened; and he is going to
have come away from Biondi’s entertainment with
the conviction that that artist, providing he
turned up, is a really great man in his line. Also
his show, unless things went wrong and there
wasn’t any, left all other one-man entertainments
so. hopelessly in the rear that rival entertainers
might as well give up for good.
* * * *
In view of the fact that the MacMahon
Brothers shortly retire from Sydney Lyceum, a
very influential committee is getting up a testi-
monial performance at that theatre to-morrow
(Friday, 17th February), and the Ministry, along
with other notables of great weight, are expected
to be present. The Bulletin hopes the Ministry
will be present; a look at a gaudy worldly show
should do the holy Cook and Brunker a great deal
of good. The theatrical record of the small
brothers covers a good many years, and extends
over a good part of Britain, the United States,
India, and Eastern Asia. The Mighty Atom
managed for Mrs. Scott Siddons during some years,
including two London seasons ; ran Curly Bellow
in his early days ; and looked after Henry Irving
when he did a “ Romeo and Juliet.” Also he had
a promising Dublin season blighted by the Phcenix
Park murders. His brother Atom ran Sheri-
dan, the Fighting Kangaroo, and other celebri-
ties. Also, the MaoMahons ran Melbourne
Opera House for a long season in its palmy days;
introduced “Evangeline,’ also a oertain memor-
able drinky American pug whom they look back
upon as one of the saddest spots on their career,
and did many other things, including the manage-
ment of a purely Australian co. for 19 months at
Sydney Lyceum. The Bulletin trusts that their
testimonial performance will be a profitable affair,
but it still regrets to hear no mention of any hand-
some cash effort on behalf of the purely Austra-
lian company already mentioned. It reckons that
there are a good many reasons why the purely
Australian co. should have something done for it
—one reason being that it has had mighty little
done for it in the past.
* * * *
“ Flying Scud ” is doing its second week
at the Lyceum, and the writer unreservedly with-
draws the aspersions he cast on the horse in last
issue. Good authorities who know a great deal
more about the noble animal with the india-rubber
nose than he does have informed him that they were
quite uncalled for. The rival horse at the Royal
may also consider himself approvingly smacked on
any part of his anatomy where you can smack a
horse and get away alive. Speaking personally,
this individual only knows a horse as a beast that
stands on you after you have fallen off, and whose
shoe is a good thing to hang above the door lest
otherwise 13 rocks should fall on you on a Friday.
The “Octoroon ” is due on Saturday.
4» <M * *
The gushful cards of invitation to “ Your-
self and friend ” that went out last week for a
certain pianiste’s concert, have roused a lot of ire
in musical, and otherwise, circles. When the in-
vited went round to book their seats for the con-
cert they found that this couldn’t be done without
payment of a shilling. And —well, as they explain
themselves, it wasn’t so much the shilling, as the
fact that the trick didn’t seem Nice. A somewhat
similar trap was opened up in ’Frisco lately. Free
admission was advertised in all the papers, and
crowds of honest citizens lined up, to find that
they had to pay half-a-dollar for a programme
before they could get into the hall. People who
never travel with more than their fares say this
sort of blow-from-behind falls harder on them than
on anyone. But I have heard people of unlimited
oof and pedigree speak very loudly anent the nasty
jar of parting up on an Invitation Card. Also,
Sydney absolutely loves concerts that are really
free, gratis, for nothing.
The bare-legged impostor who perpetrated
the above notice will have somethin}? more rational
to say next week concerning “The White Heather”
at Melb. Royal. There were a few hitches in the
working of the scenery on Saturday, but the draw-
ing power of the show made itself quite evident,
and Bland. Holt will have bad luck if he doesn’t
reap a profit from this plucky speculation.
** * *
“ The Adventure of Lady Ursula ” has not
caused the death of anybody through a crush up
a staircase at Melb. Princess’s, but it drew some
fairly large audiences last week, and is likely to
have a cheerful last night on Friday (17th). A
great success for Anthony Hope’s graceful little
stage-play was out of the question. It asks the
multitude to believe that an actress in blue-satin
breeches and coat to match could be accepted as a
young man by a number of scrutinising bachelors,
and it makes no attempt to distract attention
from this one point. There is no reason why the
men on the stage shouldn’t draw comparisons
between Mrs. Brough and themselves, and
conclude, like the people “in front,” that
Mrs. Brough’s disguise is too thin—the whole
performance is too thin. The furniture, costumes,
living pictures, and some portions of the dialogue
are very pleasing, but the characters in the story
are obviously puppets all the time. And several
of them, by the way, pitch their voices too high,
as though shouting carried conviction with it. A
revival of “The Liars ” is promised for Saturday,
and three nights of this will be followed by an-
other three nights of the attractive “Madame
Sans-Gene.” Then a new comedy, in which Miss
Grace Noble is again to show her pretty face to a
Melb. audience. Miss Noble will strengthen the
resources of the present Brough co. very con-
siderably.

Things are looking rather cold and gloomy
at the Bijou since the first night of the Rickards
co., and the question as to whether the same pro-
gramme might have been a stronger draw at the
shabby old opera House is now discussed with
great fury. Probably the Bijou is as suitable a
place as any other for variety business, or even
more so ; but, just at present, the show seems to
require a strong leading item, with a rough edge
to it. Bay Peggy Pryde, f’ri instance. Miss Cora
Casein s dainty style is caviare to the average
Goth, and the Lorenzi-Phillips pantomime has the
fizz of champagne rather than the froth of the
long beer which the Goth’s soul loveth. And
people begin to think that the turns of dancer
\\ heatley and the one-legged “comedian ” would
be all the better for a little compression The
bringing forward of Whitburn into first place in
the second part, instead of leaving him to the last
is a wise arrangement, anyhow. Whitburn’s
oldest humor tastes fresher than a lot of the so-
called new stuff.

Fitzgerald Bros.’ Circus and Menagerie
after a roaring time of nine weeks in Melbourne’
has struck the psychological moment for catching
1 asmania on the hop, and will accordingly pack
up the tents next Sunday. An orang-outang
which is the latest addition to this growing con-
cern, serves as a hairy link between the two
animal kingdoms of the Fitzgeralds, and the
tigress purrs again contentedly as of yore. Mel-
bourne has always been fond of this particular
circus and zoo. Its great heart is bleeding (in
moderation) at the news that all the men, women
and things depicted on the posters are about to go
rolling on. Two performances on Saturday (18th)
will break up the pitch, in a cricket way of speak-
ing.

Touching the statement that George Rig-
nold has designs upon “Othello,” there is no
reasonable doubt that G.R. might have worked
the Immortal Bard at a profit many a time when
he was running melodrama at a loss. “ Othello,”
“Richard the Third,” “ Merchant of Venice, ” and
“King Lear” are all of them likely to do good
business with Rignold in the name-part, a pretty
leading lady, and a respectable eo. And why not
“Anthony and Cleopatra ’ ? George Miln, years
ago, would have made much money out of his big
Ktacular revival but for the fatuous vanity of
ise Jordan, who persisted in putting her
scraggy personality and her broad ’Murkan twang
into tne character of the Egyptian Queen. 'Rig-
nold, as Australia ought to know, is a fine, versa-
tile actor when he chooses to be, but he chose to
go on walking through young-men parts in dull
melodrama until old play-goers almost forgot his
real capabilities, of which the rising generation
knows nothing.
Last Saturday nicht, in the warmest o’ weather,
We went in a kilt to inspect “The White
Heather, ”
And we heard Wally Baker, who bigamy plotted,
Disowning his wife when hisbairnie was “potted”
On the braw Scottish moor—’twas the season for
shootin’,
But we wadna’ express our abhorrence by hootin’:
We merely remarked, “ Weel, this go is a rum ’un!
The idea of Baker deceiving a wumman ! ”
Having known him for years as a limelighted hero
It was hard to believe him as wicked as Nero.
And pretty Miss Ross in her leggings so charming
To find her improper was truly alarming.
When the curtain cam’ doon on Act L we were
husky
And roamed to the bar for a wee drap o’ whusky.
Come gather together and toast “The White
Heather, ”
And ring the Blue Bells of Auld Scotland.
The stock-broking feyther-in-law o’ his lordship
Was told how the marriage had happened aboard -
ship,
And how the false husband was cutting queer
capers,
So he swore, by the gods, to recover some papers
From under the sea. Then this story erratic
Presented Bland Holt, as an author dramatic,
Rehearing sweet angels in music and “guyver”
For a giddy production of Lady Godiva ;
And Miss Fitzmaurice Gill looked seductively
strapping
With a wig down her back and a light gauzy
wrapping,
Intended, no doubt, to keep impudent flies off—
It would need to be thicker for keeping our eyes off.
Next a Stock Exchange tableau, depicting the
dangers
That threaten the presence of venturesome stran-
gers ;
Bland Holt had to flee from the lawless assaulters,
And Brown was a caution for Smellbourne de-
faulters.
Being pressed for a debt by the merciless Baker,
He fell dead, and settled accounts with his Maker,
And the Angel of Death cast a shadow so dusky
That we kept up our spirit wi’ more o’ the whusky.
Come gather together, and toast “ The
White Heather, ”
And ring the Blue Bells of Auld Scotland.
Now, the true marriage-lines of the stockbroker’s
daughter
Lay fifty yards deep in a “ log ” under water,
And Baker went down to the bed of the ocean ;
But Norman (a boozer, full-charged with devotion
For luckless Miss Watson, whose lord had denied
her)
Was soon on his track, like a corpulent spider,
In their ludicrous garments they loomed along
slowly,
And went for that log in a manner unholy :
They lurched and they fought, though unable to
fight fair—
Their actions suggesting a pork-supper night-
mare—
Till Norman at last got away with the treasure,
As the public perceived with the greatest of plea-
sure,
And Baker fulfilled our predictions and wishes
By falling a prey to the winkles and fishes.
So act No. 4 found the wronged woman righted
At a fancy-dress ball where she wasn’t invited,
The supers, strove hard to seem somewhat excited,
And all of the principal guests looked delighted,
Except poor Miss Ross, whose warm feelings were
mangled
In a wonderful costume, expensively spangled.
T hen the curtain came down, and by noo we were
frusky,
And ganged to the bar for some more o’ the
whusky.
Come gather together and toast “The White
Heather, ”
And ring the Blue Bells of Auld Scotland.
At Poverty Point.
“ As prognosticated in a paragraph in my early Nov.
mail,” writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent,
“the Irving-Terry split has now come off, and no one
seems to mind that it has. Ellen is to tour the provinces
with her own co. ; and afterwards—is Australia’s chance.
A young and new country seldom appreciates any of its
own growths. It yearns for the old—the tottering, dod-
dering, moth-eaten, rheum-e.ved, quavering, treble-
voiced old—when it is a * Home’’ exotic. An antipodean
national free cemetery for ‘ Yuropeen ’ artists, vocalists,
actors, and other mortifying ‘has-beens’ is all that is
wanted to make ‘ down under ’ the ‘ ’appy land, far, far

Nellie Stewart is finding full consolation for some
previous disappointments in a successful appearance as
principal pantomime boy at Drury Lane. She looks and
plays charmingly, the London critics say, and her sing-
ing is, of course, recognised as much better work than
the warble of the ordinary panto, “boy.” Nellie has
knocked ’em. Facts and figures re her provincial success
in “ The Belle of New York ” are going the rounds of the
metropolitan weeklies ; also, by way of warning off the
dukes and marquises who might otherwise lay their
coronets at her feet, a faithful chronicler announces that
“ Miss Stewart is the wife of Mr. George Musgrove.”
Jehoshaphat! By the way, Nellie has a jingo ballad that
seems bound to stir the patriotic shop-boys to wild
enthusiastic :

that Miss Boutcher, his nurse, in satisfaction of her
claim, has received from Mr. Lachaume, executor of the
deceased artiste’s estate, the sum of £231.

The Ballarat Woollen Mills lately ealled for
competitive design* and tenders from builders of steam
engines, that of the Austral Otis Engineering Company,
South Melbourne, being accepted. These engines are of
the compound surface condensing type, with cylinders
17" and 38" by 60" stroke, and wall indicate 700 h.p. on a
consumption of 151 b. steam per hour. The design is a
handsome and massive one, and they are probably the
finest example of engine-building yet produced in the
colony.*

Maoriland s 3 per cent, loan of .£1,000,000
realised about £96 10s., but allowing for accrued interest
and expenses the province is likely to net only about £94
or £94 10s., so the interest will be about 3i including

* * » •
Victoria has also plunged with a loan of
£1,600,000 at 3 per cent., but it is purely a conversion
loan to repay £1,500,000 at 4 per cent, which is falling
due. The odd £lOO,OOO is expected to go in discount and
expenses. Of course there will be a large saving of
interest; £1,600,000 at 3 per cent, costs £48,000 a year,
whereas £1,500,000 at 4 per cent, costs 160,000, making a
gain of £12,000 per annum. And if (sav) £5OOO a year of
the saving was set aside till the extra £lOO,OOO debt was
wiped off, the transaction would be a perfectly legitimate
one. Also, if the whole £12,000 a year saved were
put into a sinking fund to pay off the entire loan
at maturity it would be still more legitimate. But they
don’t do things that way in Australia. Of course, Vic-
toria, if it floated the loan at 3} per cent., could sell it at
par, or very near it; in which case it would only need
to borrow £1,500,000; there would be perhaps £lOOO a
year more interest that way and £lOO,OOO less debt. So
the extra £lOO,OOO debt is incurred merely to save present
expenditure ; and it is just aa immoral a proceeding as if
the £lOO,OOO was borrowed and put into ordinary revenue
—only that kind of swindle is always done in Australia.
The original 4 per cent, loan was sold at about £9O, and
if the renewal one goes at £95 Victoria will owe £lOO for
every £B5 actually received. The best feature is that the
new loan is repayable any time between 1929 and 1949, so
the country will have lots of breathing-time. Posterity be
damned 1
* * * *
The next big Westralian loan should be about
due now. So far as is known, the available cash was just
about finished at the end of last month, and it is under-
stood that £700,000 will be required to meet the public
works loan expenditure to the end of the financial year
(30th June, ’99). And, in addition, VVestralia is com-
mitted to public works which will cost another £3,000,000,
so it looks as if a desperate struggle to raise money was
imminent. In addition to its other troubles, too, the
Sandgroper Government has raised £1,550,000 on short-
dated Treasury bills, and money’ will have to be found in
a very few years to repay them with. Altogether, it looks
as if there were wild days ahead in W.A.
» * * »
The Australian Deposit and Mortgage Bank’s
balance-sheet for the half-year ended 31st December, 1898,
is the seventh in succession wherein the old land bank
has shown a steady improvement. The half-year’s pro-
fits are £l4OO, equal to 6s. 4d. per cent, per annum on the
shareholders’ funds, and the profits or otherwise for the
last 10 half-y’ears compare as follows :
June, 1894 (profit) £5097 Dec., 1896 (profit) £978
Dec., 1894 (loss) 5632. June, 1897 „ 911
June, 1895 „ 7566 Dec., 1897 „ 1051
Dec., 1895 „ 2616 June, 1898 „ 1207
June, 1896 (profit) 69 Dec., 1898 „ 1400
When the A. D. and M. Bank re-arranged its tangled
affairs in 1895 it was agreed that 70 per cent, of its
deposits should be renewed till 1910 at 3£ per cent, and
the other 30 per cent, bearing no interest, should be re-
payable by three instalments in January, 1896, 1899, and
1901. Therefore, to January, 1896, its old deposits
cost £2 9s. per cent, all round. During 1897
and 1898 they cost about £2 14s. 6d. per cent,
all round. Now that the second instalment has
been repaid they will cost about £3 Is. 3d. per cent,
interest, and after 1901, when the non-interest-bearing
deposits are all paid off, they will cost the full £3 10s.
This sort of thing doesn’t help to increase the profits ; the
repayment of deposits which the bank obtains gratis
leaves the total interest burden just the same, while there
is less money to earn interest with ; still the A.D. and M.
Bank keeps slowly improving. Total old deposit liabili-
ties, after repayment of instalment last month, are
£485,699, as compared with £555,528 six months ago, and
as there are £1,462,672 of assets (on paper), there seems
no doubt at all that the depositors will be paid in full.
As for the shareholders, it looks, on present earnings, as
if their nominal paid-up capital of £884,060 should be
written down to about £lOO,OOO to reduce the assets to
their present selling-value—this is on the assumption
that a bank which gets its deposit money cheaply, and
with its assets put in at their real value, should
be able to earn 3 per cent, on its actually existent
capital. But so long as the Australian Deposit and
Mortgage Bank can pay its depositors and doesn’t
profess to distribute dividends, the real value of its assets
is the shareholders’ own funeral. With time and patience,
and the gradual increase of profits, and the slow revival
in property'-values, the institution may come right again
at a much smaller sacrifice than this. ’ln fact, there is no
reason why it shouldn’t come right without any sacrifice
at all, if the shareholders like to wait 20 years or so.
Everything comes to him who waits long enough—or to
his posterity'.
» • * *
The unfortunate Land Mortgage Bank of Vic-
toria, for the half-year ended 31st December, 1898, made a
loss of £6963 on its ordinary business and on the realisa-
tion of property below book values. Which compares
with some previous losses—it is all losses with the Land
Mortgage Bank nowadays—thus
December, ’94—Loss £6038 June, ’97 .. Loss £14,425
June, ’95 ~ 6617 December, ’97 „ 8508
December, ’95 ~ 8847 June, ’9B ~ 10,915
June, ’96 ~ 9696 December, ’9B ~ 6963
December, ’96 „ 10,494
In the last three half-years the Land Mortgage Bank
realised £36,214 by repurchasing its debentures at
a discount, which has to be set against these
losses. But with all savings the accumulated deficit is
now £39,034 and though last half-year’s shortage was the
smallest since June, 1895, prospects are very bleak. The
co. owes £730,538 to English debenture-holders, at 3| per
cent.—half-year’s interest £12,784. Its gross earnings are
only £11,140 and expenses of management are to be
provided out of them. At the end of 1901 the debenture-
interest reverts to the old 4J per cent., and, unless some-
thing very glorious happens to the assets before then,
that may be the last straw. The gross earnings are equal
to £2 Is. per cent, per annum on the alleged assets (almost
wholly mortgages), and, as even the cheapest kind of live
mortgage yields 4 per cent., the inference is that, at the
outside, the assets are not worth over £540,000 at present,
against £743,000 owing to debenture-holders. There is
still £124,255 due on the last call, but, even if it can all be
collected, which is hopeless, and applied in the payment
of debentures, it will only save about £4350 per annum
interest, which won’t balance the accounts by a long way.
If the Land Mortgage Bank could get its de-
benture-interest reduced to 2 per cent, it might just
manage to pay its way, and then it could lie low till its
assets improved in value again. Or, if it could screw
£500,000 of uncalled capital out of its shareholders, and
pay off the bulk of its debentures, the result would be
the same. The assets would be certain to look up again
some day, if it could only wait, and it might again become
something remotely resembling the gorgeous 10 per cent,
dividend-paying affair it was in ’92. Hut it has to keep
selling its assets at a big loss in a depressed market to
keep its very small pot boiling, and, by the time the
revival really comes, there will probably be very little
Land Mortgage Bank left.
* * • •
The decision of the banks doing business in
N.S. W. to charge every depositor 5s. per half-year for
labor and stationery will produce at least one good result.
The average man, when he shifts his account, has a
strange tendency to leave from 3d. up to £2 behind him.
The years roll on, and banks burst, and managers bolt
with the funds and somebody eise’s wife, and auditors go
wrong, and junior clerks are found staking the petty cash
on the suburban dog-fight, and get sacked, but that
balance has to be brought forward, lest the depositor, 20
years’ later, signs a cheque for 5s. 3d., and it is dishonored
by mistake, and there is an action for £2000 damages.
But now the charge of 10s. a year will swallow up the un-
claimed balances, and enable hundreds of dead accounts
to be closed. It is the wily bankers’ answer to the agita-
tion for the seizure by Government of all balances that
have been unclaimed for seven years. In seven years the
bank will annex t'3 10s. of the money anyhow, and few
unclaimed balances are larger than that nowadays.

The report of the City Mutual Life Assurance
Society (Sydney) for 1898 shows, in most respects, a fur-
ther improvement. Some of the principal figures for 1898
and the five previous years are as under :—
Premiums In ' Total Expen- Added
rremmms. terest. Revenue. ses. to funds
1893 ..£26,001 £6,575 £32,576 £8,939 £10,021
1894 .. 27,775 6,243 34,018 11,843 13,140
1895 .. 29,179 7,670 36,849 10,697 11,078
1896 .. 31,859 7,431 39,290 9,776 13,319
1897 .. 32,845 8,384 41,229 9,305 19,200
1898 .. 36,889 8,935 45,824 10,291 12,727
The funds have grown from £97,442, at the beginniugof
’92, to £176,926 at the end of ’98.
The figures show a steady increase in premiums
and also in receipts from investments. Expenses were
27£ per cent, of total revenue in 1893 ; 34| per cent, in
1894 ; 29 per cent, in 1895 ; rather less than 25 per cent,
in 1896 ; over 22J per cent, in 1897, and rather less than
22J per cent, in 1898. The rate is still very high, but it is
being steadily reduced, despite the great increase in the
amount of new business, and new business is a most
expensive item in every assurance office’s outgoings.
During 1898, £2128 was written off the assets for depre-
ciation, making £7981 provided under this heading in the
last four years. This is a large amount to write off
£179,000 worth of assets, and looks like a genuine attempt
to keep the properties down to their real value. It has
been decided to make no further additions to that always
rather doubtful asset, “deferred instalments of annual
premiums ” ; if the old policy had been still followed in
1898 there would have been about £2500 more assets on
paper, and the revenue would have been larger and the
proportion of expenses smaller, also on paper. But
instead of increasing this item it has been written down
by £375, and it is proposed to continue the writing-down
till it is wholly extinguished. The figures may look less
beautiful under this system, but it means more solidity,
and the course is undoubtedly the right one to follow.
The amount of interest in arrear and the asset
“ Property acquired by foreclosure ” are both low, and
have been reduced during the year, which speaks well for
the soundness of the investments. The investments yield
the remarkable interest of £5 5s. per cent., which is much
above the average. How the City Mutual does it isn't
very clear, but it seems to manage it successfully enough.
Barring the increase in surrenders and the very heavy
death claims during the late unhealthy year, there are no
visible bad features in the balance-sheet, though all
balance-sheets are subject to the painful doubt implied
by the absence of any independent State audit and
valuation of assets. And outside it the only un-
pleasant thing that is known is that a late official has
been suing the manager for heavy slander damages;
that he has got a verdict which is now, so far as the
writer knows, still awaiting appeal, and that whatever the
result there will be a heavy bill to pay. According to the
daily paper report, the board “ is prepared to accept any
responsibility that can be placed upon it” in the matter.
If that means that the board takes a moral responsi-
bility it doesn’t matter much, and if it takes a cash re-
sponsibility, so long as it does it in its private capacity, it
is the board’s own hearse and funeral, and the policy-
holders needn’t mind. But if the board takes a cash re-
sponsibility in its official capacity then there is likely to
be a large hole in the 1899 profits, whichever way the case
goes. That vague “ responsibility ” should be cleared up.
The City Mutual had a law experience in 1894 which cost
it £1801 and some heartrending coppers, and too much of
that kind of thing is unhealthy.
* * • *
The Metropolitan Mutual Permanent Building
and Investment Association of Sydney (great Scott 1) pub-
lishes a rather unfortunate report and balance-sheet for
1898. There has been a loss regularly for several years, the
latest one being £2387. This is about £1000 less than the
deficit for 1897, yet it is a rather serious item all the same.
Up to 1894 the Metropolitan Mutual paid 6 per cent, on
its debentures. In 1895 it obtained a reduction to 6 per
cent., and in October, 1897, it obtained another reduction
to 3 per cent. Its chief trouble lies in the fact that most
of its properties are mortgaged, and while the land and
buildings are largely unsaleable just now, and in many
cases yield little return, the interest on the mortgage
goes on all the time. Last year the whole income from
£279,000 worth of assets was £6525 —equal to about
£2 per cent., which isn’t so very bad, all things
considered. But the interest on £138,718 of mort-
gages and debentures absorbed £6569—an average of
over £4 14s. per cent., which is the cause of the
whole trouble. In four years, the institution has
only been able to reduce its liabilities by £27,450. Its
reserve-fund is exhausted, and there is a loss of £2373
against capital. Also, there is only £7907 more capital to
call up. If the shareholders would put up, say, £150,000
of preference capital to pay off the liabilities, the co.
could afford to pay 2 per cent, dividends on it, and it
would prevent the original capital being sacrificed by the
compulsory sale of properties in a broken-down market to
pay liabilities, but probably they are not equal to the
shock. Still, it ought to be well worth their while to
do so.
• • • •
For over 20 years the Thames (M.L.) people
have talked of working the rich gold deposits confidently
believed to be hidden in the deep-levels of the fields, but,
until quite recently, nothing was done. Between
£5,000,000 and £6,000,000 of gold was won in three years
from mines working on the reefs within a radius of half-
a-mile from the Big Pump shaft, the most notable being
the Caledonian, Cure, Golden Crown, Manakau, Moana-
taiari, Waiotahi, and the Shotover. The Caledonian paid
£672,000 divs. in one year, and the wholesale stealing of
specimens from the mine, which came to light in the
early seventies, gave color to the local rumor that as
much was stolen from the mine as found its way into the
shareholders’ pockets. The Shotover paid its four
shareholders—Hunt (the prospector of the Thames),
White, Cobley, and Clarkson—£40,000 a man for a few
months’ work, but they all “dropped their bundles"
almost before they had got over the novelty of feeling
what it was to be rich. About 1875, the late H. A.
Severn, one of the first metallurgists of his day, predicted
that rich shoots would make again at about 1000ft. below
sea-level.

Cobar notes.—The field still waiting for rain and
for the railway authorities to reduce freight. The
Young Australian hopes to keep alive by cyanidingOhes-
ney tailings. Said tailings of high gold-value but very
coppery, hence returns must be comparatively small.
The Chesney has worked out an excellent scheme for re-
duction of its ore, which it is about to put into practice.
Manager Roberts is using all his ingenuity to economise.
He needs to, since the number of contributing-shares in
the co. is none too big. The Great Cobar Copper Syn-
dicate has completed its fifth water-jacket, ana a sixth
one is in course of erection. How they are rooting it out!

Victor(y).

“Malle”: Some months ago Thk Bulletin
published a comment on the loose nature of the N.S.W.
No-Liability Act, and suggested certain amendments.
What can be done, possibly with impunity, under the
existing Act, is evidenced by the Shellback G.M. Co.,
Gulgong, N.S.W. This co. consists of 100,000 shares of
ss. each, of which 32,500 were paid up, and 67,500 con-
tributing ; these latter were offered to the public at Is.
on application and Is. on allotment. It took over two
months to sell less than 6000 shares, and 10 per cent, of
contributing capital is supposed to be paid up in cash
before registration. However, the promoter, by taking
up contributing shares in lieu of cash brokerage, and in-
cluding these, brought up the amount to the enacted
minimum. Without holding a general meeting, or con-
sulting the provisional directors or the vendors, the Memo,
of Association was signed, rules framed, and the co. regis-
tered. The promoter was elected, by the signers of
Memo., as chairman, and with less than £6OO cash, of
which more than half was absorbed in printing and other
?reliminary expenses, the co. started on its wild career.
wo months after registration it was unable to pay
its miners, and its leases were mortgaged to the promoter-
chairman. Four months after incorporation, the first
general meeting was held ; no printed balance-sheet was
submitted, but this was accounted for on the ground that
the audit was not finished in time, and a statement, pur-
porting to be a balance-sheet, in which no mention of afore-
said mortgage was made, was read by the chairman, and a
glowing report of the future prospects of the co. was dis-
cussed. As some of the vendors, apparently ignorant of the
full facts, had entrusted the chairman with their proxies,
the election of a board was within the promoter’s control,
and he was re-elected chairman. One of the newly-elected
board, however, resigned inside a week, and carefully
advertised in S.M. Herald that he had no connection with
the co., and ere he could be replaced another member
died. Then the chairman proceeded to appoint a director,
who acted with him in appointing a third. At a general
meeting in Dec., called to consider an offer for the co.’s
property, one of the original vendors asked a lot of un-
pleasant questions, and when the information was not
forthcoming threatened to move at the annual meeting
“ that the co. be declared illegal, and the Crown Law
authorities be asked to take action.” When the annual
meeting came round last month, although this obnoxious
person holds, and is proxy for, some 23,000 shares, some-
how or other neither he nor the shareholders he represents
received any notice of meeting, so this little unpleasant-
ness was avoided. Now the promoter-chairman-mortgagee
has seized the leases, and they were auctioned last week
for £450.

Diamond mining is the latest threatened S.A.
industry. Experts forecast that some day a big field will
be found around Echunga, 25 miles from Adelaide.
Strange that the first mineralogist who examined Ade-
laide ranges found diamonds which were shown at London
Exhibition in 1851. None have been found since.

“Button Grass ” : What is the proper investing
price of North Lyells ? I say 255. a share. The co.’s capi-
tal is £1,500,000. In order to be as big as the big mine
crowd (who won’t have anything to do with the N. Lyell
flukers), an unnecessary railway is to be built at a cost of
£250,000, and an unnecessary ship is being constructed
for £BO,OOO. Say another £lOO,OOO for smelters, &c., and
this company stands committed to an expenditure of
£430,000 —£330,000 of which is to be spent in absolutely
unnecessary works, as connection could be had with the
Emu Bay Railway Co.’s lines at a cost comparatively
small.

“ Pyrrhatite ” : Last year’s return from Char-
ters Towers was again very big. But cyanide was
responsible for about a-third of the total (approximately
£1,250,000) —a larger proportion than ever before. There-
fore, the mines themselves are not so productive as
formerly. The solvent operated chiefly on the tailings
and sludge accumulations of bygone years, and, as these
can’t last for ever, those in the local know quite admit
that, unless some radical change sets in, the near future
will show a heavy decline. The low levels have, of
course, been the great hope of the district, but although
gold has been struck in the Brilliant and Brilliant Ex-
tended at the lowest points, it is not an accepted payable
proposition. The lodes live down all right, and the stone
is of low-grade quality—about lOdwt. average. This,
under-present conditions, is not good enough, and it
will be surprising if some attempt is not made—as a
means to making ends meet—to cut miners' wages. Sal-
vation, however, will not follow upon such a move. The
fact is that crushing charges (15s. per ton) are absurdly
high. Companies’ directors are responsible for this.
These enterprising people, in addition to drawing their
“ small fees,” have erected private mills, at which the
mine shareholders are “ put on toast.” The mills reduce
the ore won from the mines over which the directors are
supposed to hover with angelic watchfulness, and the 15s.
per ton leaves a big profit on the transaction, which is a
millstone for the industry to struggle with. Then no
attention has been paid to economy in transport or crush-
ing of ore. When it reaches the surface it has, in many
cases, to be carted far to the mill, which might just as
easily have been erected near the mine. No labor-saving
appliances seem to have been introduced, and crushing
appliances are away behind the times. This is the matter
which will require prime consideration when the question
of treating low-grade ores orops up, as it soon must. The
field itself is all right, but economy in working alone will
admit of future success. New blood is sadly wanted in
the place ; the old has become sluggish through long
prosperity and isolation from the rest of the world.
V. W. Boyle, writing from Thornborough,
N.Q., controverts statements here made by a correspond-
ent signing- himself “Hodgkinson.” In the first place,
Mr. Boyle remarks, all the well-known local mines—in-
cluding those singled out for criticism by “ Hodi> kinson ”
are being held and worked—not a bad sign. Mr. B. goes
on to point out that Govt.-Geologist Jack says that, “ The
Hodgkinson crushings have averaged as well as those of
any other field in Queensland. ... Of real prospecting
for further shoots of payable stone by the sinking of deep
shafts or the driving of exploring levels, there was scarcely
any. The field was taken up at first by miners who con-
tributed nothing but muscle . . . but muscle is not capi-
tal. It is only with the surplus of earnings over living
and working expenses that he can do ‘ dead work.’ In
other words, the development of the mine has to be carried
on out of revenue instead of out of capital. That scores
of Hodgkinson mines did so for years in spite of every
drawback is an achievement to be proud of. As a matter
of fact, the first check —the first sign of diminution
in the yield, such as is common in all mines—was
the signal for the desertion of nearly every
‘show’ on the Hodgkinson. The profits already
made had been otherwise disposed of, and there
were no funds available for prospecting work. Many
mines even seem to have been thrown up while in
the full tide of prosperity, or what should have been pro-
sperity under favorable circumstances. Disheartened by
these drawbacks, the miners, as one after another they
exchanged the ‘ flush times ’of 4oz. stone for the poverty
of l{oz., became stricken with the conviction that ‘ the
Hodgkinson reefs don’t go down,’ and began to think of
clearing out. It was a most unwarrantable conclusion,
as no attempt was made to prove the imagined negative.
Many mines were abandoned in spite of the fact that poor
crushings had been succeeded more than once by rich.
The deepest shaft (Tyrconnel) is only 414 ft. . . . The
yields speak for themselves, and if they were not authen-
tic official records they would strike one as incredible.
The agents of capitalists are running all over the world
looking for mines such as have been abandoned on the
Hodgkinson by the score. Had the field been discovered
at the present time there would have been no half-
hearted working of the mines, and the Hodgkinson would
probably take rank well in front as a gold-producing
district.”
From Croydon : During 12 years Croydon has
produced 687,317i0z. gold from 433,441 J tons of stone
crushed. Since ’94, when cyanide operations began,
89,754£0z. bullion has been got from 109,998 tons tailings.
——Croydon ’9B yield : Quartz, 80,227£ tons for 41,352|0z.;
by cyanide, 13,9230 z. from 19,609 tons. Continued
drought during last half of year hung up nearly all bat-
teries and seriously reduced yield. Up to Jan. 20 over
llin. rain fell during current year on Croydon. Dams
everywhere full, and mills all at work on stone accumu-
lated during drought. Croydon Mining News tells that
Elmslie, Ltd., have during four years procured the expen-
diture of £172,676 on Croydon field in the purchase of
properties, many of which give promise of handsome
returns._ At Waratah Co.’s properties at Tabletop a
mountain of low-grade ore is being operated on at the
rate of 50 tons a day. Economical and efficient work-
ing promises to make this a valuable investment.
First clean-up will be anxiously awaited. The most
extensive and costly mining pumping plant ever intro-
duced to N.Q. is now being erected at Croydon Consols
by Elmslie Ltd. aforesaid. When on the point of a rich
discovery, the mine became flooded. Present plant should
drain a big area of Golden Gate auriferous ground.
Prospects of Croydon for current year exceedingly bright.
Last year’s development work promises payable returns,
and several leading mines look forward to a much-
increased output. Jubilee Consols (English co.) started
work again recently. Splendid battery plant at mouth of
shaft. Ore low' in average, but it is expected that when a
junction of the Jubilee and Lady Lottie reefs is reached
good-dividend gold will be got.
The threatened Vic. gold-dredging boom will
revive in all its activities the long-dormant sludge ques-
tion, with consequent litigation and new legislation.

North Magdala-Moonlight, Vic., forfeited shares
lately advertised on account of non-payment of 134th call.
Many other similar cases in Vic., while N.S.W. has not
one single instance of call-paying endurance.
Westralia’s biggest div.-disburser last year was
the Boulder. Its product was worth about £337,500. Of
this, £200,000 was paid to shareholders.
• • * •
Bayley’ Great Oversight, W. A. Shares adver-
tised for sale for non-payment of calls. How pleasant it
must be to manage a mine with such a name—or to be
working mullock in the Croesus.
• • • *
Hamilton, manager of the Great Boulder, gave
the Big Blow, Coolgardie, its death-blow, reporting that
it was too poor to pay ; advised the sale of the plant and
the winding-up of the co. When floated there was “ one
block alone of 860,000 tons.” It had more stone in sight
than any other mine on the field, and was to be one of the
world’s greatest gold-producers. Purchase price—£2s,o3l
in cash, and 9969 paid shares. Capt. Bell M‘Taggart, the
chairman, had always a great yarn to tell at meetings,
sometimes a scarcity of water, never of gold. Writer
often laughed at it, having personally satisfied himself of
the barrenness of the “big blow” over four years ago ; but
anything was good enough to float in the boom. Recent
meeting in London got very angry with the chairman,
who resigned, and all the other directors followed. An
investigation committee was appointed to overhaul
accounts, whereupon the directors sent out a circular that
they intended to keep their seats.

* * * *
The immense slag-dump at the old Burra, in
S.A., was pecked at seven or eight years ago, but, though
machinery was erected, the low price of copper compelled
abandonment. With the rise bigger men have taken up
the work ; the Martins, of Gawler, will put up a 10-head
battery, and for the rest a wet process will be used.
There is an idea also that some of the residue may be of
value as a fertiliser.

SOCIETY
Earl Beauchamp is to be the successor of Lord
Hampden in the Governorship of N.S.W. The Hampden
dynasty has been popular. , . . The Honorable
Miss Dorothy Brand is an excellent horsewoman,
and very few professional lady riders of the show-
rings can give points to this Government House young
Wjdy m the matter of pluck and skill in the side-saddle,
ihe Honorable Dorothy Brand has earned quite a reputa-
tion in the post-and-rail country over which hounds run
in N.S.W.”— Exchange.
THE GREAT AUSTRALIAN CUSTOM.
VISITING PARSON: “Surely on this, the day ice
opened the new church, the town ought to have
kept sober.”
RESIDERNT P.: “I'm afraid that is the event they
are celebrating.”
Gov. Hampden, ere he leaves N.S.W.,
should inspect Burragorang Valley, the land of
six-foot inen and women, where the newly-found
silver-lodes are. The place has not been" visited
by any Governor since the days of Hercules
Robinson. Lady Robinson was delighted with
Burragorang—the only place in her experience,
she said, where they provided a whole duck and a
whole sucking-pig for each guest at dinner.
One very important and popular N.S.W.
personage much resents the appointment as Gover-
nor of “ a mere inexperienced schoolboy,” which
he considers derogatory to the status of so im-
portant a province.
* * • •
Lord Tennyson’s selection as Governor of
S.A. has produced mixed feelings in Adelaide.
His Ex. is believed to be pious, although
not such a hard case as Gov. Buxton ; there
is nothing to encourage the idea that he is
wealthy ; and no one knows whether he has sport-
ing or theatrical tastes. Altogether, he is some-
what of a mystery, but, in any case, it is impos-
sible to have another such utter frost as Gov.
Buxton, whose virtues were as numerous as they
were monotonous.
• * * *
The pious ex-Governor Buxton has in-
formed the London correspondent of S.A. Adver-
tiser that while in Adelaide he spent just double
his official income of “£4OOO a year, no perks
added,” which shows that it is possible to go to
great expense without giving much satisfaction.
* * • *
Charles McArthur King (ex-P.M.), the
new chief magistrate of Norfolk Island, is a man
of experience and ability ; but verging on 70 is a
bad time of life to enter on what will be practi-
cally a new career. The fact that Mr. King’s
grandfather was Lieut.-Gov. of the island, and that
his father was born there, may incline the natives
to receive him as a friend, but it will afford no
guarantee that he will succeed in curing the com-
plicated disorders of the primitive people. In
fact, even a younger and more energetic man
would probably fail in guiding the inhabitants
into paths of reason, owing to supreme authority
being divided as it is between Britain and
N.S.W.
Mr. McArthur King’s appointment is probably
the reason why poor E. J. H. Knapp, who was a
disappointed and necessitous candidate for the
position, attempted his own life the other day.
As the islanders, taken all round, are a miserable
lot of shufflers, in whom hypocrisy, self-righteous-
ness and several other things are absolutely in-
grained, their island is an excellent place to live
away from. All important public meetings there
are opened with prayer. Then everybody pro-
ceeds to call the other fellow a liar. But those
who remain to the end close the meeting with
prayer.
* ♦ • *
When Gov. Beauchamp arrives, looks over
Govt. House, straightens himself up, and goes
back to the ship to make his official landing, will
Premier Reid dare to repeat his sensible remark
when Albury invited him to open an already
opened bridge ? Gov. Hampden took his trip
ashore in ’95, bad an hour’s sleep, practised his
speech before a mirror, and went back to the ship
in time to make his official arrival at 2 p.m. And
most of his predecessors behaved similarly.
The latest queer feature of that exciting
crime, the Melb. boot-box tragedy, is the secret
bailing of Dr. Gaze, committed on a charge of
being an accessory before and after the fact.
Gaze was to have been bailed out on the day after
arrest, but one of the prospective bondsmen
d(*elined to act unless his name were kept secret.
The police and magistrate refused to accede to this;
and the following day the bondsman interviewed
the Crown Law office, which met his views. The
nominal bondsman, a mining man, is guaranteed
by a very big mining man who is also a M.P.
* • • »
Where does the sphere of influence of the
Melb. boot-box tragedy fade away? The other
day there arrived at the office of a Melb. daily a
young man with spats and a collar as high as a
sheep-hurdle, who brought the editor a letter of
introduction from an English friend. Being
warmly welcomed, lie explained that he had just
landed from the Orizoya, and thought the kaw-
hnies really splendid, dontcherknow. They con-
versed for some time, but the visitor seemed to
have something on his mind, and finally the jour-
nalist said, “ Now, is there any way in which I
can be of service to you ? ” The florid face of the
new-chum beamed. “ Yes ! ” he yelled ; “ can
you get me into the morgue to-morrow?”
* * • .
Mrs. Sara S. Nolan, President of the
W.C.T.U., says that “ there are thousands who
would not drink intoxicants but for fchp caso with
which they can obtain them.” Will Mrs Sara
produce evidence? If beer and whisky were as
cheap as water the great bulk of the drinking
population would, after they had recovered from
their first tremendous free jamboree, probably
take to cold tea as a regular beverage. It may
be the fatal ease with which alcohol is not obtained
that makes the majority of men hanker so much
CYCLE RACING UP TO DATE.
after it. Any respectable toper will tell Mrs.
Nolan that one clandestine Sabbath drink is worth
half-a-dozen straightforward liquors consumed on
any other day.
• » * *
Whisky-agent Deegan, president of Vic.
Licensed Victuallers Association, repeats the oft-
told tale that “ the most notorious criminals are
not drunkards.” He might have added that some
of the most contemptible blackguards—the fel-
lows at whom burglars point the thumb of scorn—
are staunch teetotallers. Viz., the clear-skinned,
curly-headed beauty-men, commonly known as
“ ponces,” who live upon the earnings of fashion-
able harlotry. Every big city has its infamous
tribe of such animals—strong, healthy, and sin-
fully sober.
* * • *
Ed. Bulletin.— Re your commentless in-
sertion of the Lismore Hospital advt. (“ Wanted
a nurse—<£2o a year”) in 4th Feb. issue let me say
a word. Lismore is one of the most prosperous
provincial towns in N.S.W., yet its hospital
management is proverbially mean. When I was
a physical wreck in exile in the Big Scrub, I
frequently heard of injured cane-cutters, timber-
getters, bullock-drivers, etc., who would not go
to Lismore Hospital on any account. They in-
variably went to Ball ilia Hospital, where nothing
was cheap and nasty. Some country committees
are odious skinflints, and it’s wonderful how cor-
rectly the common, no-class people gauge such
bowelless persons.—J.P.
* * * *
The rector’s daughter who recently slew
an English Sunday-school teacher for alleged mis-
conduct with his pupils appears to have been the
dupe of her own prurient imagination. Of course
the murderess is “ pious.” Religion of the ranker
sort is only an “ allotropio ” form of filthy-minded-
ness.
A correspondent on press para-
sites :
The newspaper “ Bludger” is an institu-
tion worthy the attention of the united
metropolitan press, and an organised at-
tempt to suppress him would put a nutn-
ber of loafers and cadgers to the necessity
of “ travelling" on some other racket
than that of “ connected with the press.”
Foundation-stone layings, receptions, at
homes, inaugurations, etc., down even to
the changing hands of an hotel, all serve
to obtain for these “whisperers” cheap
liquor and gorge, and, in many cases,
even cash tips, on the supposition that
they are more or less influential press-
men. The only person safe against them is
the professional front-of-the-house man at
the theatres. I have had pointed out to
me at a fifth-rate Sunday-school picnic no
less than five supposed “ leading press-
men” who had bustled in on the nod.
Something in the way of a press badge
ought to be producible as a guarantee ;
sav, a ticket like the Cricket Association
season ticket, which could be issued to
the order of the editors of the co-operat-
ing papers. The ill-odour in which press-
men are held by some people is entirely
due to these bogus journalists, who so fre-
quently disgust the givers of the enter-
tainments into which they have managed
to crawl on false pretences. Couldn’t the
press section of the going-to-be organised
Sydney Bohemian Club do something to
cope with this army of locusts?
• * * *
On Kangaroo Island (S. A.) is a
little township, now GO years old,
wtiich possesses two Jay Pees, be-
tween whom there is such bitter per-
sonal feud that they never sit in
court without disagreeing. Each is
constantly petitioning Govt, to make
another J.P. of his own faction, so
that he can over-rule the other fel-
low. Fortunately, there is little liti-
gation, and as the place is without a
pub. there are few drunks to fine.
• * » *
A new industry in Melbourne.
Dog run over by tram. Laid on the
footpath. Weeping man or woman.
“ My pet, my pet, my little dog.”
Collection, money to bury him, to
buy another or for beer. One dog
lasts three days. Rammed in bag to
another stand, every hour.
Recently a railway employe died in S.A.,
leaving a widow and child. His comrades sub-
scribed to pay his burial expenses, for the family
was t utterly destitute ; and they were shocked at
the demand made by an Anglican parson for
reading the funeral service. However, they didn’t
provide the fee, butg<>t Dick Wood, M.P., an old
railway servant, to officiate in his stead. They do
not fear the result on the soul of their old fellow-
workman.

AN ENTHUSIASTIC SPORTSMAN ENTHUSES.
So now the Brands
Seek other lands;
Alack ! long ere they reach
’em
A fickle crowd
Will cheer as loud
For godly Governor Beau-
champ.
’Twill be Hampden’s lot
To be soon forgot,
Now an Earl is his succes-
sor ;
But the new-chum Earl
Will bring no girl
Like Dorothy Brand—God
bless her 1
Then let it be known
That all of us own
Since her dad to Australia
brought her.
That there has not been,
Nor will there be seen
Another such Governor's
daughter.
Yes ! we all concur
(In respect to her
Ideas are not dividing)
There’s no “ seat,” nor
“ hands”
Like Dorothy Brand's
When she goes out a-rid-
ing!
And each Australian,
And every alien
Who rides as straight as he
“ oughter,”
Has been fain to yield
In the hunting-field
To the prowessof Hampden’s
daughter.
So, doff your “cady”
To this young lady !
Hooray—with your hat in
your hand ;
Australia’s pride
Was the girl who could ride
Like the Honorable Dorothy
Brand !
FOR FUTURE USE.
The poet wrote a little note,
And phrased it well in dainty rhyme,
To tell the girl with hair a-eurl
And eyes that made his senses swirl
That he loved her wildly all the time.
He rhymed to love with “ glove ” and “ dove,”
And swore his flame would ever burn.
He wished to wed, the missive said :
“ Herewith a penny stamp,” she read ;
“ If not accepted, please return.”
THE ETERNAL FEMININE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
My little girl,
How kind your every thought I
The light of Truth irradiates your face ;
So free from guile you are, and quite untaught.
Your figure takes a pose of perfect grace,
My little girl;
Your pretty lips are curled,
Yon laugh at care, yet weep to see distress ;
No short-frocked maiden tripping through the world
Surpasses you in mental loveliness,
My little girl.
Your years amount to ten,
The “ boys ” are boys of whom you gaily speak ;
One day you will apply the term to men,
And make a hero of a mortal weak,
. My little girl.
May God remain your friend,
Giving love's blessing to the mother’s pain,
And happy thoughts of life that has no end,
When in your daughters you are born again,
My litile girl !

BRISBANE, QUEENSLAND.
Rand wick Asylum, one of Sydney’s
refuges for destitute boys and girls, and at present
sheltering 158 waifs, enjoys the splendid privilege
of being able to say that there has been no death
in the institution since 1887 —almost 12 years !
* • * •
The struggle for a crust. Latest Sydney
development of dental surgery is prowling around
to beguile unsuspecting canines and pull out their
incisor teeth (saleable at about 3d. a-piece to the
fakers of dog-tooth necklaces “from the Islands”)
The family poodle had, therefore, better be
secluded for the present lest he fall a prey to the
dog-doggers.
* * * *
Elections of country hospital committees
are mostly occasions when the local bank-manager
says nice things about the local parson ; and the
local parson returns the compliment and includes
the local grocer ; and the local grocer, who is a
J.P., thinks that the services of the local baker
have been invaluable—and so on. But it’s a dusty
day when they can’t raise a row at Broken Hill.
This year the auditors in their report accused the
chairman of supplying the institution with goods,
and the chairman forgot to bring up the report;
then the chief fly-in-the-ointment demanded it and
was called a blanky old scoundrel, to which he
replied that the chairman was a blanky old liar.
The chief fly next responded gaily to an invitation
to fight; but, to the intense disgust of the audi-
ence, the chairman fell back on the ancient excuse
that he “ wouldn't soil his hands,” and later on
one of the auditors took a hand in the game.
However, medical comforts were freely intro-
duced ; and at 2 a.m., when the meeting con-
cluded (it began at 8 the previous evening), every-
body drove his chief enemy round town at a
furious pace, while constables on duty discreetly
looked up the darkest back-streets for the burglar
that wasn’t likely to be there.

laugh?” Some years ago one
of the large Chinese secret
societies in Sydney decided
to disband, and having nearly
<£800 in the bank, a meeting
was held to decide on the
disposition of the funds. Pro
rata division amongst the con-
tributories was out of the ques-
tion—it would have been “un-
lucky.” The momentous matter was
discussed in high falsetto for some
hours. Some wanted to make a present
of ic to the Chinese newspaper as a recom-
pense to its proprietors for “ tellee all about
war.” Others thought the best thing to do was to
“ blew " the whole amount on a religious festival
largely composed of devotional fire-crackers and
rockets of soul-satisfying holiness. The two
parties were so evenly divided that there seemed
no prospect of agreement. Eventually someone
suggested that the only proper way to spend the
money was to produce a Chinese drama of a moral
tendency, and that the representation should con-
tinue until all the money was exhausted. This
programme was actually carried out, and for
months afterwards the boards of a hall in Castle-
reagh-street were occupied with pig-tailed kings
and bumble-footed queens, while slant-eyed market
gardeners drank in the beauties of the national
drama and didn’t go out to see a man between the
acts.

An orator at a recent Queensland railway
banquet “ regretted that the ladies were not pre-
sent to see how the gentlemen enjoyed themselves
when they were alone."
Passengers by train to Sydney from an
Illawarra suburb the other day were haunted
persistently by an intolerably sickening odor.
One man who at last resolved upon changing cars
was astonished to find a stretcher on the platform
of the next carriage, and a constable in charge of
a swollen corpse which had been in the water for
several days. If it is necessary to drag a very
decomposed cadaver to its bereaved domicile,
surely a special truck should be hitched on at the
rear-end of the train. Also (this is an after-
thought, based on an experience the writer had
when last in Victoria), if there are only two pas-
senger-cars on a mixed train, it isn’t advisable to
put a large truck of bonedust between them.
* * * «
There is said to be a move among Sydney
pressmen to form an Australian branch of the
English Institute of Journalists, which several
“ daily ” scribes have recently joined. The char-
tered Institute is a large power in British news-
paperdom, but frequent efforts to start Australian
combinations of the same kind have failed
ignominiously through want of the cohesive
quality, or something, in the local pressman’s
nature. Maoriland, where reporters and others
are usually domesticated and stickative, is the
only province which has a successful journalists’
union of its own.

The billet of Council Clerk at Drummoyne
(Sydney) was recently on offer at £2 8s. per week.
There were no fewer than 70 applications for a
responsible post which would probably be regarded
with a scornful sniff by the Drummoyne Councd’s
own scavengers—unless they are themselves very
badly paid.

“ C.F.” to The Bulletin on certain social
aspects of the Jackwant country : —
In reply to J.S.T. (B. 12/ll/’98) and O.S. (7/l/’99). Have
in Japan bathed naked with naked men and wo-
men of all ages—and have thought nothing of it.
But put the same show on the far side of a cur-
tain and immediately it gets an indecent flavor.
A girl in brown bloomers on a bicycle is an ordi-
nary sight which at worst would be called un-
becoming ; but substitute white
for brown, with a lace frill for
a plain band, and something
is at once suggested. It
is, in fact, in suggestion
that the whole matter
of indecency lies. A
low ball - dress
showing an area
of bare skin
at midnight
is not inde-
cent because
it is an accus-
tomed sight,
but no self - re-
specting girl at
a midday picnic
would unbutton the top fastening of her blouse,
even though an underbodiee covered her skin. Indeed,
this is probably even because of that underbodice. There’s
a good deal of confusion between decency and the reverse
and modesty and immodesty. A girl who once nearly
fainted when I brought out an unpremeditated “damn !’’
has been discussing with me the “ Yarra” and “Gatton”
horrors (in all their phases) without t urning a hair.

gravely commiserated a certain night-
watchman upon enforced absence
from his duties through in-

At Adelaide Police Court, the other day,
the first case under the Affiliation Act of ’9B was
heard, and one Sharman was ordered to pay
£6 10s. expenses incurred at the confinement of
the lady of his love. This is the sort of thin? that
will bid ’em pause, and make ’em bear the ills they
have, rather than fly to others that are liable to
put a hole into the Savings Bank account.
* . * * m
There are always compensations (good and
bad) in Nature. Queensland brags much about
its ever-declining phthisis death-rate. On the
other hand the cancer ever increasing.
The resident doctor at Toowoomba Hospital notes
the rise in the mortality from this disease, and
remarks it comes only second to heart-disease as a
cause of death in his district. Well, they won’t
inspect the meat, and they won’t look after the
abattoirs, so they must pay the penalty.

A man was arrested in Brisbane Jan. 19
and charged with trying to steal money from a
boy. Brought before the police-court on 20th, re-
manded, and committed for trial on Feb. 6. On
Feb. 7 no true bill was filed, and the hapless man
was set free after 19 days undeserved imprison-
ment. Sole evidence against him was the word of
a small boy, who said he woke up on the steamer
and found defendant’s hand in his pocket.
