The administration of the First Offenders’
Act in Queensland is a fearsome and wondrous
spectacle. Well-known criminals have been given
the benefit of its provisions and restored to the
bosom of their families. To crown all, a brothel-
keeper in a Southern town was also lately allowed
to take advantage of the aforesaid merciful law—
to the edification of the whole community.
* • * *
Catherine Ahearnand Robert Hogan, con-
victed at last Bathurst (N.S.W.) Circuit Court of
cattle-stealing, and since proved innocent, have
nevertheless been refused by Government any
compensation for having served a portion of their
sentences. Why should the community be more
immune than the individual from action for
“■wrongful imprisonment”? In this case there
was the usual absurdity of a “ free pardon ” —for
an offence never committed !
* • • •
The Claremont (W.A.) murder looks as if
it might be the tail-end of a life’s drama. Accord-
ing to the report of the black-trackers, Mrs. Fox
left her house with an axe, presumably to get
wood. About 400 yards from the house a man
met her behind a tree and they stood there some
time, apparently in conversation. Then the
woman ran and the man followed—at least the
assumption that the man ran and the woman
followed seems too improbable—for sixty yards,
and at the end of that space there was a struggle.
The woman ran twenty yards further and fell
dead. In the melodrama the man who meets a
woman unexpectedly behind a tree is mostly her
first husband just returned after being 20 years in
gaol, or supposedly dead, or wrecked on an
island ; or else he is the person who knows about
her past; or he is the individual who wants the
information about somebody else’s past which in-
formation she alone can supply, and she refuses to
supply it and defends herself with the axe. But
quite as probably he was only a person who started
with the intention to commit highway robbery of
2s. 6d., and the unfortunate woman hadn’t 2s. 6d.
about her.
* * * *
The interesting chronicles of the Full
family have been lately relieving the monotony of
existence in Melbourne. Full p'erc has on various
occasions been “ dealt with ” for grievously pound-
ing his wife, or failing to properly maintain
her and his children. Also, at various times the
Full children have been “ shot in ” for running
about the world in a neglected condition. Most
of the elder children are now State wards, a small
boy having been committed two weeks ago. Then
the mother sought to make old-man Full support
the remaining two youngsters. The case was
before the S. Melb. Court four times. The first
time it came on Full was in gaol. The second
time he was also in gaol. The third time the
gentleman turned up at court and casually ob-
served that he had a home for his wife and family
—the remains of it—to which he invited them to
return. Case remanded for a week—to let matters
settle, as it were. Fourth occasion the wife was
absent, she being in the hospital, with the
youngest infant. Full, Esq., returned home,
taking the sole remaining offspring with him, but
apparently lost this small remnant by the way,
for some hours later the forlorn youngster was
found wandering about Port Melb. in a dilapi-
dated and disreputable state. He was also com-
mitted to the Industrial school. And old man
Full still flourishes.
THE PERFECT PRESENT.
So I have kissed you 1 And this hour is mine.
Its light along the level future lasts,
It crowns a drab eternity of Pasts 1
Here soul and soul have crossed the border-line
Of self, and merged. No years can e’er untwine
This hour from us ! What though to-morrow casts
The memory out, and your cold glance contrasts
With this day’s rich, red lips, need I repine ?
No. I have kissed you 1 And the brief warm flower
Born of our lips perfumes eternit3 r .
Prom the long loneliness that silently
Stretches behind, before, I am content
To cull this blossom of one perfect hour—
To snatch one star from Time’s deep firmament!
Melb.
ARHUR H. ADAMS.

“Albatross Wick.” Two of her children are down
at the present time with typhoid fever, and, al-

Melbourne papers are deluged with wild
and weary suggestions as to the name of projected
Federal capital* The champion attempt to date
is “ Anglopatria.” How relieved the author must
have felt when he got it down on paper! What’s
wrong with the national bird—Kookaburratown ;
or our old friend —Ornithorhynchusville ? A few
years ago there would have been no argument—
everyone would have voted for Carbine City.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, February 14,1899.
My Dear Myee—
For brilliancy, noise, vegetable-decoration,
house-dresses, stage-dresses, and all the other ad-
juncts to a dashing first-night, the “Geisha”
could scarcely be bettered. Moreover, it looked
as if the play started off on Her Majesty’s door-
step last Thursday, when the piece opened fire in
Sydney. The vestibule was a vestibule no longer,
but a delicately-toned Oriental bower of Chinese
lanterns, gold chrysanthemums, and genuine Jap.
draperies. The effect was charming, and was fur-
ther touched up by the glittering first-night gar-
ments of women crowding round to admire. Also,
the unwashed looked in from the street, and
thought it was getting the show for nothing.
“ This is a bit of orl-right,” one small coin-collec-
tor observed, spitting on his hands. “ It’s the
‘Geisha.’ There’s no room inside, so they ’ad to
put it hup hout here.” And all Pitt-street sneaked
up close when Goody wasn’t looking, and didn’t
think much of it. That’s the best of being only a
coin-collector. You’ve no occasion to imagine or
pretend that you’re getting good value for your
money.
From a top-storey standard, this light and gor-
geous opera isn’t an exceptionally—isn’t even a
moderately—cunning, clever, or startling perform-
ance. But it’s rosy with success, and radiates a
sort of borrowed light from long runs in other
places.
Only two acts, and one scene to each act, but
the composition of the tableaux is masterly. The
first picture is. a lovely champagne-supper dream,
draped exquisitely with purple wisteria. It shows
the tea-room from outside, and explains very
prettily why some people* want to go to Japan so
badly. The Geisha girls who burst upon you here
aren’t smitingly Jap. to look upon, but all their
dresses are more dreams, and they hop as lightly
as frogs. A thrilling exit is effected when a
pretty dancer is caught up by a naval officer, who
slings her round his waist, and carries her off that
way.
As Molly, Seamore, Dorothy Vane is very
fetching. Nun, indeed ! No, not one, as you
will agree when you see her dancing like a
pretty little houri, with her fair front-hair floating
m rhythm with her step. This is quite a favorite
trick of Dolly’s, and women with toupees and
women with the whole wig, and particularly
women who curl nobody’s hair but their own, are
lost in wonder at the free and fearless fluttering of
that long, fair fringe. It gives an artless, joyous
look to the dancer, that a stiff, still toupee could
never aspire to, and is a tremendous charm as
Dolly uses it. To an actress who is any sort of a
favorite a late entrance is a gain, and when she
at last appeared her welcome was long and hot,
and full of flowers. Her English dress of white
cashmere, buttoned at the back, and fashioned
apth a short white coat, was rather a styleless gar-
ment, to my mind, especially when worn as she
wears it, with a big white babyish hat at the back
of the head. A short tight skirt and a big flop
hat are not too becoming to a short and slender
actress. But as soon as she got into her
“kimona ” she looked adorable. This was a little
round cream gown heavily embroidered in scarlet.
Her prettiest frock was the white satin wedding-
robe decorated with amber and yellow birds of
paradise.
After Dolly Vane—perhaps I ought to say
before her, as O Mimosa San is the actual heroine
of the play—came Miss Florence Perry, the
Finn’s newest soprano. Seen through the glasses,
Mimosa is a pretty, bird-like brunette, with brown
eyes and a small amiable face with a limited
capacity for lighting up. Her look and lack of
abandon suggested an over-tight obie. What ?
Don’t you know what an obie is. The sooner you
learn the better if the “Geisha” is going to stay
long in Sydney, for we shall be all in obies and
kimonas soon. It’s that cunning wide sash that
f'oes round the waist and ties at the bsick in a bow
ike the brand on a hot-cross bun. Miss Perry’s
first frock was a pinkish-mauve satin worked all
over with gold butterflies and fans. Bunches of
yellow flowers were stuck in at each side of her
little dark head, her obie was fashioned in yellow
silk, and her mauve skirts ended in a thick yellow
hem.- The nervousness of first night may have
been exercising her soul, for she scarcely seemed to
get out of herself into the fascinations of the
wickedly demure Miss San. Even the beauties of
her scarlet fortune-telling dress failed to fire her,
although this was the genuine article from Japan,
made of finest silk and light as any thistledown.
By the way, the weight of some of the embroidered
kimonas, particularly Miss Vane’s wedding-
dress, must have been painful wearing for mid-
summer.
As for the men of the play—well, the Chinaman
is always Woman’s peculiar province, and any
sound-minded housewife who has ever reduced a
John from ninepenee to flipenny in the matter of
a “cabbagee” may be forgiven for going into
ecstasies over Lauri’s Wun-Hi. He is just the
sort of Chow who would give a woman a glorious
big field to beat him down in by starting his
cabbagee at one-and-six a head (I can go no
higher). All along the play Lauri scores as heavily
as the genuine article marking up on the wash-
house door. Quite a different breed of hero is the
naval Reggie as played by the fascinating Mr.
Pauli. Wtioden soldiers are not quite out of
fashion, judging only by Reggie’s acting, but let
him sing and he can execute notes and sounds with
anybody. His arms and legs still trouble him a
little when he isn’t speaking, but his throat is as
strong again as ever and the voice has lost none of
its beauty. Then, looking at the men from a
man’s point of view, Lily Titheradge’s brassy young
middy would probably be the most acceptable.
She makes ordinary nice boys quite uninteresting
beside her shapely, roguish little personality in
navy-blue coat and trousers ; and she tucks her
handkerchief up her cuff with an exact representa-
tion of the swagger of your smart young naval
shaver. By the way, there is one very deeply,
awfully cerulean bit in the “Geisha.” This is
brought in by those six tall Englishwomen in
glaring royal-blue suits, whose malady isn’t
soothed by gilt trimmings. Mrs. Bracy shades
herself down a little by a smart little sailor hat,
but blue caps seem to further lift up the rest of the
band’s higli coloring. However, this hue is
always looked for in some form or other in a
popular play, and in other forms there is a pleas-
mgly remarkable scarcity of it.
The bright and winsome Julie, of the “ Geisha,”
threatens an early retirement from the stage. It
is well known that her parents strongly opposed
her entering upon a theatrical career, and the
young lady took her own way in the matter while
Mr. Musgrove was absent in England. A mes-
sage has now reached poor Julie to the effect that
if she accepts another engagement after this one
terminates all sorts of dreadful things will happen
her. But generally in such cases success has a
way of softening the stoniest heart, as in the case
of Ethel Haydon and her guardians.
Little Celia Dampier’s concert introduced us last
week to a Maoriland musician in short frocks,
with a wonderful head of Titian-tinted hair. Her
fiddling is at present in its youth. It promises
well, but how hard it is to pass judgment on the
playing of a girl scarcely in her teens. If she isn’t
desperately nervous, her fiddling will keep as near
as possible to teacher’s instructions. If she is
desperately nervous she may play as she never
played before and never will again, which may be
a great deal worse, or a great deal better, than
ordinarily. One hearing, therefore, is often as
valueless as none. I near that Ovide Musin
thought highly of Miss Dampier’s promise, and
Sydney will have another chance of hearing her at
the farewell conversazione to Lady Hampden.
Celinski—she prefers it without any prefix—■
had the vice-regal suite at her recital last Thurs-
day. Lady Hampden, in black, and Mrs. Mat
Mayoress, in an embroidered dust-cloak, led in an
unusually large audience ' >r an unknown player.
Celinski turned out to be a fluffy, Frenehy-looking
little woman in white, with a chic style of sash-
tying and a not-yet paralysing genius. Her pro-
gramme was a trifle tawdry for a great performer,
and the trouble about the piano doubtless upset
her. The key of this instrument was mysteriously
forgotten ; and at eight o’clock it remained hope-
lessly locked. Maud Fitz-Stubbs started out in a
cab to find a key to fit it, but finally there ap-
peared on the platform a workman in shirt-sleeves
who proceeded to break it open. Everyone
applauded, he bowed, and, thinking it over after-
wards, the house decided that this was the most
exciting item on the programme.
The funeral-flower question is still pricking at
the consciences of “colonial” divines. The R.C.
Church is dead against the flower-custom, ap-
parently on the ground that it detracts from the
solemnity of death—that the prospective after-
world loses a little of its terror when the grave is
heaped with roses. But, mind you, it isn’t the
corpse that will suffer, it’s the florist, whom, by a
queer coincidence, Rome can’t claim for her own
with the great claim she puts on nearly all good
undertakers. Shall we ever see the One True
Church issuing an edict against the ghastly frip-
peries of that most revolting of all farces, the
“ first-class ” funeral ?
Another public floral question is “The English
Rose,” now blooming at the Theatre Royal.
Broken Irish is the feature of the play, in spite of
its title; and the struggles of the co. to get the “h”
into order, say “Father Michael, ” like a high-born
Fenian, and sprinkle its conversation generally
with home - made brogue, fall heavy on the
soul. Charles Holloway emerges from his
moustache with an excellent Hibernian mouth,
but an Irish priest and a Primitive Methody say
their public prayers quite differently, and the
whining doesn’t ring true.
For three acts Eugenie Duggan looks tidy in one
grey skirt and green polonaise, which somehow
seems a strange thing for a wild Irish girl to do.
Ida Gresham, as the English Rose herself, looks
appropriately fresh in a frilly white muslin over
tan shoes and a Leghorn hat with many colored
roses. Amy Gourlay’s good taste has no chance
beyond the leaf-green rosettes in her dark hair ;
brother Willie gets his shirt out in broad scarlet;
and Miss Deorwyn breaks her policeman’s heart
with the jet on her snappy rose-pink blouse.
Everybody, goes to the Tivoli now. Nice men
bring their wives ; and a stratum lower down the
family comes all together—mother, father, byeby
and the intervenings. Somehow a theatre-party
to the Tiv. brings into the feminine mental vision
just a sufficient dimly - floating purple - colored
excitement that isn’t in the least like the excite-
ment of doing other theatres. The large, fat, com-
fortable feeling of going to Church, or Her
Majesty’s, elevates itself here into a thin, sharp
delight, whose biggest component is a sort of edgy
uncertainty. And yet, there is very little that is
good enough to be what is called “Bad.” The
songs are vulgar always, but it’s a dull and lumpy
vulgarity that doesn’t shoot out sparks, and
it rolls off and away and leaves no im-
pression from its sheer stodginess. Sometimes
there are two or three exceptions to this statement,
but for weeks past Peggy Pryde has been the only
one. She still sends everything home to you with
a sort of finished smack, and it’s no disparagement
to say that her laugh does three-quarters of the
work. But when it comes to a love-lorn coon song
like “The Lily of Laguna,” Peggy isn’t standing
on her own legitimate heels. Carrie Moore, in
the pantomine, made ever so much more of it.
Peggy in knickerbockers and top-boots isn’t such a
cunning little creature as Peggy, in a skirt that
seems to catch her individuality. Her legs are
slightly bow-shaped—bow legs aren’t Cupid’s bow
—and if you love her, as everybody does in
Sydney, you want to beg her to go back to long
clothes.
Certain folk in Sydney are still sceptical over
the actual coming of a bachelor Governor. They
recall the appointment of Lord Lamington to the
Governorship of Queensland, and how the Queen
—so goes the yarn—sent him a private message to
the effect that he had better get married before he
undertook his duties. These persons see, in the
fact of Lady Mary Lygon’s proposed three-months’
visit, a hint of his Lordship’s matrimonial inten-
tions. Others again deride all such surmisings,
and say that the coming of a (practically) unaccom-
panied male simply means that haughty English
dames are no longer “ having any ” Sydney society,
and that returned travellers’ tales are, of course,
accountable for this.
T’othersiders who meet in Perth flock together
like so many sheep. The cliqueism that prevails
in Perth is said to beat anything of the kind in
Sydney, and Westralians look with scorn and
contempt upon the inhabitants of the other
provinces. Doctors have gone there and saved
scores of lives during the fever-plague, but they
are not called upon, nor their wives received by
the little gods of Perth and those who bow down
to them. It all comes, no doubt, of the isolation of
Perth until the last few years.
No less than three English papers which
I have seen say thoughtfully that Lady
Isabel Stanley—who, the other day, married
Hon. Gathorne Hardy (known to Sydney girls as
“the Slatepencil)—“has a sweet disposition.”
Which is not altogether as kind a remark as it
seems, for the one aristocrat for whom this com-
ment is most often trotted out—the Princess May
of York with the baa-lamb face—has been trai-
torously accused of wearing sloppy, heel-over
boots and shoes. One paper boldly asserts that
the Princess spends less on her foot-gear than any
other royal lady. A nice charge to bring against
Britain’s future Queen ! Lucky that “ Mrs.
Wettin ” lias been often and openly twitted with
one-button-gloves, the prize monstrosity of fashions
for the last 20 years, as she can’t very well read
her daughter-in-law a V.R. sartorial lecture, punc-
tuated with the pride of kings and the glory of
the Guelphs.
Quite the most fashionable church society now
in Sydney is the English Church Union. This is
the refuge of the High Church party, of which
Lord Beauchamp is a pillar at ’Ome. Since the
appointment of the new Governor the meetings
have been crowded, but of course the Archbishop,
who is very “ Low ” indeed, is always conspicuous
by his absence. At last meeting the discussion
turned upon his absent Lordship, and a debate
arose as to whether or not the Primate was
indifferent as to what was said about him in the
papers. Finally a mature spinster arose with the
air of one who has something to say worth
hearing. “Well, I know he doesn’t care,” she
announced, solemnly, “ because one of the papers
said he was going to be married again, and he has
never contradicted it.”
The queerest part of the fine lately in-
flicted upon Bishop Thornton, of Ballarat, for
fast-driving lies in the thought that a clerical
horse could make pace under any circumstances
whatever. All the clergy’s quadrupeds ever
known were as slow as funerals, and. I have a lively
recollection of one slim but sinful mare who re-
quired a whole family of boys a.nd girls, and the
maid-servant, and the man from next door, and
any stray persons that could be gathered in, to
shove the buggy wheels round, and pull from
before, and push from behind, before the animal
deigned to pass through the parsonage-gates on
her way to chapel. But I don’t wish to do
that horse an injustice. The same scene of push-
ing, and petting, and coaxing, and hooting, went
on just as maddeningly when its destination was a
funeral or a baby to be baptised. Some people
fancy it’s the feed the clergy’s horse gets, and
others fancy it’s the—well, not the feed—that’s at
the bottom of the trouble. Anyway, the par-
sonage horse whose fast ways bring its owner
into the police-court is an animal to be remem-
bered for all time.
The other cable about Olga Nethersole’s for-
tune hasn’t arrived as I write. Of course there
is still time for the £40,000 to be snatched away
by the mysterious submarine agency that kills a
man one day and resurrects him without an
apology (for the resurrection) the next. But if
the contradictory cable isn’t coming, Olga will
luxuriate as an heiress with wonderful prettiness.
Somebody from Sydney called on her at her home
in St. John’s Wood recently. Everything in the
little house was branded with the sort of simplicity
that represents the very top storey of extrava-
gance. Her black hair was coiled on her neck
with meek artfulness, and her yellow satin gown
was a marvellously - fitting princess robe from
Paris. There was a tiny garden and a big dog,
and Olga’s dearest friends said her favorite relaxa-
tion was to fill the garden with men and sit bare-
headed under a tree in a white frock that would
never betray its price to a man, while the dog lay
at her feet and the men gazed. For the benefit of
the Australian visitor, she brought out some
rather particular photographs of herself, and
exhibited them with the admiring comment,
“These are rather compromising, are they not?”
They showed her in all kinds of peculiar attitudes.
Before she handed each one over she would hold it
in her two hands and gaze on it long and lovingly,
with something brighter in her black eyes than
the ordinary wrapt interest people always keep for
their own portraits. With this keen feeling for
extravagantly outrt things, she had also put on
flesh rather plentifully, and showed signs of grow-
ing into a large, but of course extremely beautiful,
Hebress.
Writes my Melboume correspondent
“‘Here,’said that honorable four-year-old, the
other day, to the simpering male person behind
the counter of a Bourke-street soft-goodsery;
‘show me some pink—nice pink—ribbon.’ The
maid stood meekly by, and the salesman produced
four shades in the most vivid rose-color. The ex-
alted small child threw the streamers from her.
‘ Just look what he’s showing me ! ’ she said, with
a scornful snigger at the unhappy man. Looking
round her, and espying the color she wanted in a
cravat arranged carefully among a bank of laces
on the show-stand, she proceeded to pull it forth,
in spite of a rather disrespectful protest from the
figure in the frock-coat, and the fragile lace-heap
collapsed. Baby Brassey is said to occasionally
give the florists’ girls an anxious quarter-of-an-
hour with her winning little ways, but up to now
we. haven’t heard of her paying a rampageous
visit of great damage to a china-shop. That is a
joy in store.

want the general public to see them as they see

right turn, bear to the left, and stop dead at the
New Guinea photographs. Then turn your head
away, and the fuse works immediately.

“According to a recent Argus cablegram, some
‘ Captain Clark ’ or other has been visiting certain
gone-to-grass Franco-German battlefields, with a
view to increasing his strategical lore. Since the
not uncommon name of Clark flashed from conti-
nent to continent, Bulla (Vic.), where the prize
Ayrshires come from, confidently claims the
would-be strategist as a wandering militia-hero of
her own. ‘Jimmy,’ the bovine star of Bland
Holt’s recent stage cattle-show, is another be-
paragraphed product of those parts.
“ Bulla cattlemen wax quite romantic over their
Ayrshires. When big stout ‘Jamie’ aforesaid got
that Bland Holt engagement, owners of rival stock,
so say theatre-patrons, nightly haunted the scene
of his triumphs, pale as ‘ sheeted ghosts, ’ because
the gems of their own herds had not been preferred
to Mac-What’s Its Name’s fatted brute.
“ Animal-worshippers are credulous re the fine
feelings, and blind to the failings of four-footed
favorites. A suburban lady-killer, walking down-
street with the girl of his heart, noticed his pet
cur race out of a butcher’s en route, smearing
Mamzelle’s snowy skirt with a stolen sausage as
he passed. When the thief tired of running, he
lay on his back, legs in air, spoil in mouth, ab-
jectly awaiting the approach of his owner and the
aggrieved fair. The latter confidently counted on
a ‘ good hard knock ’ for the culprit on her account,
and an extra cuff or two for the sake of principle.
As the judge bent over defendant and caressingly
tickled his shirt-front, plaintiff heard the former
ask in maudlin tones, ‘Was urns fossickin’?’
“At the Princess’, Mrs. Brough makes a pet of a
boy. In Lady Ursula’s period, male plumage was
so gay, dainty and frilly that a girl only needed
a neat leg to look better in satin small-clothes than
ever she did in a skirt. Mrs. Brough, as Anthony
Hope’s ‘ Pretty Madcap, ’ is far more winsome in
skirted coat, knee-breeches, and three-cornered
hat of pale-blue satin, lace frills, silk hose, silver-
buckled, red-heeled shoes, and blonde peruke, than
in the whalebone, buckram tubby panniers, and
weighty train of feminine interludes.
“18th-century love-making, too, is twice as
effective for stage purposes as present-day hug
and kissy-kiss tactics. Lady Ursula’s manly,
quaint lover (Carne) respectfully saluting his
charmer’s finger-tips is a more artistic spectacle
than Walter Baker, say, in tight green plaid trews,
bridling and trying to seem bashful preparatory
to a declaration. Then there’s the offhand,
operatic style of embrace. Dorothy Vane is so
much shorter than adorer Pauli that, in hugged
attitudes, her head nestles on his lower vest in-
stead of his heart regions. The result calls up
ideas of dear Doctor Squills, with his ear pressed
to Baby’s front sash in an attempt to ascertain
whether Pi tty Sing really swallowed a marble, as
supposed by fond Mamma.

ERRORS IN FOOD AND DRINK.
By a Specialist.
It is dawning on the public to try and prevent,
or at least to arrest, disease. It is in prevention
that Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa plays an important
part; acting solely as a first-class nourishing food
it strengthens the system to resist, oppose, and
overcome the attacks of disease. You’ll hear
someone say, “ So-and-so has a strong constitu-
tion ” ; follow that up and you’ll find that So-and-
so follows the golden rule of being temperate in
all things, and pays attention to diet and exercise.
Does he or she keep up this strong constitution by
taking medicine or swallowing pills ? No, indeed !
They have discovered that prevention is better
than cure. Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa places a means
in the hands of everyone to build up and maintain
a sound constitution, which enables its possessor
to travel his life’s journey without the aches and
pains which are in many cases preventable. Thus
we come round again to sound common-sense
based on experience.
Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa can be obtained from all
chemists, grocers, and stores, or from Dr. Tibbies’
Vi-Cocoa, Limited, 269 George-street, Sydney.

Premier Reid’s definition of “Yes-No”:
“ Mental indecision.”
“ H.” : I encounter a statement that Red
Kelly (Ned Kelly’s father) was transported to
V.D.L. for homicide.
Lewis Carroll’s “Alice in Wonderland”
must be a big favorite in Australia. Out of the
new 6d. edition of 10,000 copies, printed by Mac-
millan’s, the English publishers, 4000 have come
to Melbourne to a single bookseller. This is the
biggest importation of one book at one time for
very many years past.

J. F. Burns, the new trustee of the Parkes
family grant, is, like the late James Martin, one
of Parkes’s close old friends, but, unlike James
Martin, is an Irishman, and may therefore pull
better than his predecessor with the principal of
the Parkes household.
The world still knows nothing of its greatest
men. Here is London Sketch saying that “ There
are two Mr. Murray Smiths known to fame.” One
is the son of the boss of the firm of Smith, Elder
and Co., the London publishers, and the other is
the editor of Glasgow. Evening .News. But the
Sketch has never heard of the Victorian Murray
Smith, though he is a C.M.G., an ex-Agent-Gene-
ral, one of the directors of the Argus, and a big
man among Melbourne Tories.
Hume Cook, who got into Vic. Assembly
on the A.N.A. ticket, gives his young friends
much sage advice re speechmaking as a trade. He
calls it “ Oratory as an Art ” ; but, then, Cook
somewhat overrates himself. “Go to the front of
the platform—let the audience see you before they
hear you—begin well, end well: the body of your
Bpeech can take care of itself.” And if, as it
generally happens, there isn’t any body whatever,
it, of course, makes no difference.
Apropos the widely-advertised fascinations
of the Dubberke, take the case of Mrs. Greer,
shot (by her husband) with her inamorata Sou-
drey, director of the French Court at the ’BO
Melb. Exhibition. [Greer fatally shot himself
afterwards.] Mrs. Greer survived. She had
offers of marriage from doctors while in hospital;
also at least one cleric badly wanted her to be
his wife. She was refused a passage by the P.
and O. ; declined with thanks by the Orient line;
last of all she got away from Victoria by sailing-
vessel. Dubberke will have her pick of the male
population for a certainty.
Re that Afghan trouble in Bourke. Abdul
Wade, manager of the so-called Carrying Co., was
imported into Victoria by Thomas M‘Leod
Palmer, a well-known Warrnambool grazier, and
with about 40 of his Afridi friends was taken to
Tooram, ostensibly to milk cows. The crowd
started killing one another, and Palmer shot
Wade’s cousin, Sirdhar Khan by name. He was
tried for his life, but acquitted, amid applause in
court. On the advice of interpreter Gilbert
Smith, Palmer got rid of his Afghan servants.
Wade then worked for Thomas Elder and Co.,
S.A. Under the influence of this firm he has
prospered, and is now virtually the Camel Carry-
ing Co.
“O’B.” to The Bulletin
Dr. Mueller, of Yackandandah, whose death received
but soant notice from the bulk of the Australian press,
died, it is worth recording, while dictating an advertise-
ment for his new book on typhoid fever. A loveable
character was the doctor. A perfect type of the
highest kind of German medical man—utterly unworldly,
enthusiastic as a great big boy, a scientist to his finger-
tips.
Sirdar Kitchener is the image of what Bill
Beach was when Bill fought his Omdurman with
Hanlan and won his title. The invincible do or
die ” expression of both faces would be a splendid
example to the rising generation but for one tiling .
the rising generation can’t pick its own face. _ it
has to be content with the scrambled-egg-looknpg
mask and pickled-tripe expression with which its
parents endow it.
“Jimmy Titles”—J. 0. H. James who
died in Perth, the other day, was a thorough type
of the high-class English sportsman. If anything
was done to purify sport in that city of many
swindles, he did it. He was also a highly-cul-
tured but pleasantly unaffected gentleman, and as
raconteur and after-dinner speaker he had few
equals. As a man of sterling honesty also, he was
a rarity in Westralian society.
Pkre Le Rennetel (just returned from
France), whose anti-Dreyfus and pro-army avowals
rippled Sydney gossip last week, was himself an
army man in early life. A French student, he
passed through his conscript term, and, as a volun-
teer, took part in the Franco-German war, which
brought France to the dust. IVlost of the IVTarist
fathers at the present time in Australia and the
South Seas are old army men, and all of them
dearly love French soldiers.
H. E. Kater, M.L.C., who figures this
year as special prize-giver to the Southern (N.S. W.)
Rifle Association, is of German extraction. His
father was “gold-staff officer ” at
the coronation of Queen Victoria.
The M.L.C. spent his early life
banking, and while in that sphere
participated in several thrilling in-
cidents. Amongst them was the rob-
bery from him of several thousands
of exchange notes. Two banks, the
Oriental and A.J.S., went to law about them,
losing thereby a considerable sum in addition.
Bushranger Lowry, one of the note-thieves, was
shot dead, and Foley, his associate, was appre-
hended. Foley surrendered his share of the
plunder, but Lowry had hidden his, amount-
ing to .£5OOO or £6OOO, somewhere in the bush
near Mudgee, and died without divulging the
plant. The Oriental Bank, which issued
these notes new, never had any of them
presented for payment.
Old Jack Robertson once went to a
Sydney bank and tendered his cheque to
the teller. The official declined to honor
it, and passed it back to the knight, who
thereupon contemptuously snorted, “ If you
won’t cash cheques, what the h—l are you there
for ! ”

rarily-convert-

Lane did not

but on arriving

was notified by
the manager of
the local bank
that a cheque
for £5OO had
been paid into
his credit by a

Tasmanian obit. : Mrs. Annie Archer ;
born at Launceston, Tas., 1825 ; widow of Wm.
Archer, F. L. S. of Cheshunt, architect and
botanist, and member for Westbury in the first
Tas. Parliament. She was daughter of the late
James Hortle, superintendent for the Longford
(Tas.) police-district, whose father landed at York
Town (River Tamar), about 1805, with the first
regiment to visit the north of the island.

New York Sun prints a spread-eagle patri-
otic screed in verse, signed “James Lindsay
Gordon.” He can hardly be a relative of the Aus-
tralian Gordon. Probably he is a Yankee youth,
whose father intended to bring him up to the
poet business, and gave him a good front name by
way of a start.
“Thos.” Andrews, the newly-appointed
secretary of Melb. hospital, is the classic-visaged
hubby of the pretty Mrs. Andrews, who occasion-
ally lectures on poetry and art. Mr. Andrews’s
clean-shaven face is that of a youth of 20, but his
hair is snow-white, and his age as great a mystery
as that of the ordinary sausage of commeroe.
Personal popularity, rather than experience, was
responsible for his selection, and he was an ex-
member of the committee which appointed him.
Father Chiniquy’s stories of his ’orrible
experiences in Australia are going into circulation
in England. Chiniquy was an adept at running
up against brutal assaults and moving incidents
by flood and field, when advertising was bad. He
had a narrow escape from a shocking death at
Ballarat, which is not likely to be found amongst
the holy man’s records. He was staying with a
pious family, and while sitting in the library one
afternoon with his host he heard a strident brogue
in the hallway, and immediately rushed from
the room. He was subsequently found in
the cellar, with a lump on his head as big
as a goose-egg, and could only be induced to come
up when it was explained to him that the owner
of the brogue was a mild little milkman who
weighed under eight stone, and was the moßb
devout of Orangemen.

Charles Williams, the war correspondent
of London Daily Chronicle, who “ gave away ”
the shooting of wounded dervishes in the
Soudan, had two sons in Australian jour-
nalism —Frank Williams, who once repre-
sented Newcastle Herald in Sydney, and
another brother who acted as secretary to
N.S. W. Protectionist party at the general
election of ’94. The latter has gone back
to Canada, where he was on the Dominion
Hansard. As to the alleged shooting of
wounded Dervishes : if old mail Williams
says that he personally knows it to be true,
then it is true.
M'Kenzie, of Cowl Cowl, Lachlan
Fiver, tells of the late David Buchanan
that when he mentioned to Parkes his
projected trip to the old country Parkes
said he could give him a letter to his
friend Thomas Carlyle which David
thankfully accepted. On his arrival in
London he hunted up Carlyle and after
much trouble was able to present his letter
in person. Carlyle took it without a word,
read it through twice, and then inquired,
“ Who is this Henry Parkes ? Is he a
bushranger?” “He he’s the Premier
of New South Wales ! ” gasped the as-
tounded David. “He called on you
when he was recently in England.”
Thomas thought a bit, then banged his
fist on tho table. “Oh, I remember!”
he said. “ That talking orang-outang ! ”
The past four years have made a re-
markably clean sweep of the Sydney stipen-
diary bench. Of the seven regular magistrates
in ’94, only one—Delohery—now presides over any
of the courts. Whittingdale Johnson was recently
removed by death —but the five other ex-P.M.’s
are still living.

-Ex.
It was on a hot day in S.A. Assembly
criut a member of Hansard staff whose turn was
approaching, scribbled a note to the leader and
tossed it over to him. It contained this plaintive
wail: “For God’s sake lend me sixpence. I must
go out and stiffen myself with a drink before I can
report this old .” A perverse draught of air
whiffed the paper over the edge of the gallery and
dropped it in circling eddies right down on the
desk in front of the orator. He picked it up and,
slowly opening it, read the mysterious message.
But he made no sign.
Premier Reid will reach his 54th birthday
on 25th inst. He began to work for himself at 13
years of age, as useful boy in a Sydney shop.
Here he may be said to have imbibed the freetrade
notions which later won him the Cobden medal.
He joined the Civil Service at 19, and entered Par-
liament at 35. He taught himself most of the law
he knows—at that time admission to the Bar was
easier than now. He received no collegiate or
university training, but from 13 till now never
missed a chance of listening to what people say
and wagging his own jaw.
Slices from weird patriotic whoop by Dr.
Hodgson, Sydney’s meat-diet apostle, during his
blastiferous campaign against the dietetic heresies
of Dr. Nebuchadnezzar Muskett: —
When the hills shall re-eoho the thunderous tread
Of legions of men who ere night shall be dead—
When sabres like lightning shall leap from the scabbard
And doubt of the issue turn men grey and haggard—
Like a bolt from Olympus they’ll sweep o’er the field—
Hacking, slashing—aye, dying there, rather than yield ;
And the wounded will cheer as they lie in their pain,
“ Those Bushies ! They saved us again and again 1"
The carnivorous Hodgson should at once put his
Muse on a meat-diet. Say, a bullock three times
daily. N.B.—Shake the bottle.
“Aberdeen” sends to The Bulletin a
Gordon-Baillie testimonial: —
Mrs. Gordon-Baillie’s five-years’ sentence for false pre-
tences has been shortened by her release on ticket-of-
leave. She was an adventuress, but so are many, charming
“ society ” women who never get into gaol. She was the
daughter of a washerwoman, and could not write—any-
how, she could not write well enough to ever send a note
in her own hand ; yet no one with whom the present
scribe—who had many conversations with Mrs. Gordon-
Baillie—is acquainted ever suspected that she was not a
woman of excellent education, as well as of what the
world calls good birth. She talked most interestingly,
with that refined Scottish accent which in a woman is
surely the most mellifluous of all intonations, and had
the loveliest cartload of children you ever saw. These
were understood to be the offspring of a certain tenor
once well known in Australia, and one of them, she used
to claim, was the original of Millais’ “Cherry Ripe.”
This was quite untrue ; but it looked so eminently pro-
bable that everyone believed it, for the child was one
that even Michael Angelo would have loved to paint.
Had Fortune made a duchess of Mrs. Gordon-Baillie, she
would have more than looked the part, and she would
otherwise have been equal to the occasion, for not only
was she phenomenally handsome and the possessor of
grand manners, but she was intrinsically the best-natured
and most generous of women. What a pity that she
hadn’t £lOO,OOO a year !

Aboriginalities.
‘ J.W.R. ’ : Recently passed through
Sydney en route for Melbourne a couple of eera-
todi. Long known to scientists as an interesting
fossil of the mesozoic period, the survival of this
_reinarkabk( type of fish came as a surprise to
science. To have thus outlasted, perhaps, thou-
sands of centuries, the creature's destiny kept it
in Australia, the land of the living-dead—as wit-
ness the Legislative Councils—where the extinct
flora and fauna of the Old and New Worlds are
still in existence. Yet, even here, its habitat has
narrowed down to two little livers in Queensland,
the Burnett and the Mary. There is but one
other living family of the dipnoi, or lung-fishes—
the lepidosiren, or mud-eel, of the Amazon and
the Gambia. The faculty of out-of-water breath-
ing has probably been acquired by a slow evolu-
tionary process, and the two pairs of flappers which
take the place of fius suggest the gradation to feet.
The flesh of the ceratodus is said to resemble
English salmon’s, the local name, in fact, being
Burnett River salmon. Large fish, up to 251 b.,
and over 3ft. long, may be easily caught, but
nothing is ever seen of the small growers, though
eggs are plentiful enough.

“ C.R. : In an out-back district, where
medicos are rare and parsons would create a seri-
ous stampede, knew a man whose wife died. He
walked 20 miles behind the remains with a 15-foot
weeper round his hat ; blew his nose at the grave
on a red table-cloth, and drove home in the hearse.
“ WE ARE SUCH STUFF AS DREAMS ARE MADE OF”
“ I dreamed. ,” said the neurotic hoarder in a hollow voice , as he glared wildly round upon the
rest of us; “I dreamed that a porous plaster had drawn my salary !”
Then he stepped across the dray-track and calmly
annexed his brother William’s missus. One would
naturally suppose that Bill resented such un-
brotherly conduct. Not so. He smiled sweetly
to himself, and next day brought home his nearest-
neighbor Badger’s spouse to his sorrowing family.
Badger said it was a bit awkward at the time, but
he reckoned to replace the old woman before the
’taters were fit. Badger and Bill and Bill’s brother
continue on most friendly terms.

“ Yarrum ” : At the small N.S. W. coun-
try agricultural show it is the same old thing over
again every year. Same old bull, same old lady
rider, same old big pumpkin, same white leghorn
rooster, and monkey, and snake-charmer. It all
makes a man very weak, and yet these tin-pot
affairs are subsidised to the tune of <£4ooo a year,
apportioned in proportion to the barracking
powers of respective M’s.P. Far better to have
(say) four show centres and four decent shows in
the province, each to handle .£lOOO of the grant.
“ Give us a show,” in fact.

“Malua” : While marking run-boundaries
once in the Gulf country sent my black-boy riding
for bag of wa.ter to a lagoon a few miles off. On
returning with it he inquired—“ Where panni-
kin, boss?” “On the saddle.” “Baal that one
sit down.” “ How did you fill the bag? ” “ Be-
longa mouth.” “ Why didn’t you use your hat? ”
“Hat too much dirty fella ; 1 wash’em mouth fust
time, you know.” He had actually filled the bag
by repeatedly filling his mouth and squirting the
water into the bag. Nevertheless, having a good
“drought ” on at the time, it was the best water
writer ever drank.
* * * •
“ R.H.” : Re Federation prospects. The
jealousy among bushmen in our different pro-
vinces all the time grows apace.. In Victoria you
are told that the “ Crow-eaters ” are beggaring
the country. The Cornstalks attribute scarcity of
work and low wages to the influx of Gumsuckers ;
and Western Q., you are always hearing, would
still be Billjim’s paradise but for the invasion of
Cornstalks; while in Far North Mongrelia all
‘ Southerners ” are detested as much as the Chows
and Japs., and accused of cutting wages and
generally impoverishing Kanakaland (ye gods!
whites spoiling Mongrelia !). Westralia deals sum-
marily with all “ t’other-siders,” and blames them
collectively for the bursting of the boom. Call
the lederal State “ The Land of Later On,” and
its capital “ Waitabit ”; but let’s have it, if only
to wipe away this absurd jealousy among men of
one blood, one tongue, and one soil.
* * * *
“ Shaker : H. Stockdale’s description of
a female aboriginal duel (B. 24/12/’9B) doesn’t
apply to the Gulf country, wherein, though the
weapon is as described, the party acting on the
defensive holds her fighting-stick one end in each
hand, and receives the blow at or near the centre.
Blows also are aimed at all parts of the body, and
stopped with wonderful skill. The gins train
their female children in the defensive use of a
stick almost from the time they can walk. Wnter
has seen a child of six whom it was impossible to
touch, provided the blow was not heavy enough
to beat down her little guard.
A foreign gentleman staying up Wagga
way was going for a day’s duck-shooting “on his
own, ’ and to that end bought a collie pup as a
splendid gun dog. He returned alone, and re-
counted his experience. “I go to that Curra-
warna lagoon, ant I see a mop of docks. I say to
zat poppy, you stay behint and I call you ven I
vill have you bring out ze docks I shoots. Zen I
goes on mine estomac ant I crawls troo de grass for
half a mile, ant gets to ze roshes on ze water. Zen
I lifts up mine gon, and zat poppy he runs into ze
vater up to his vaist, ant he bark, ant zem docks
fly avay. And zen I get so mad I jump on mine
feet ant I shoots heim mit boat barrels, and he
falls down and never say kvaek.”
“ X.” : At Mungunnia, way
back, a couple of travellers halted
at the pub. (during the excessive
summer heat) to vary the mono-
tony of scorching in their sulky
from settlement to settlement by
a week’s “ drunk.” They put in
their week of joy, and then de-
parted for Native Dog. On the
stony hills about half - way be-
tween the two places they
smashed up their trap against a
tree. The horse bolted to Native
Dog. The heat was terrible, and
the men, dazed with the sun and
grog, started hunting for water
among the stony ridges the
nearest water was miles off, in
the Warrego. The men separated,
and sought in opposite direc-
tions. It was death to bewithoub
water in that heat. Already their
tongues were swollen. When the
horse arrived at Native Dog the
men were away. One of the wo-
men let the poor beast into a
paddock. Afterwards a rouse-
ahout took the horse back. The
wrecked sulky was found, but no
men, so he continued on to Mun-
funnia. The men were not there.
t was too late that night to do
anything. Next morning a search-
party went out, and, after some
hours, a hat was found, then
boots, coats, and other clothes;
and, finally, one of the travellers,
dead, blackened by the fierce
heat, which scorched the clothes
of the searchers.

parent asked a farmer’s permis-

tation. It was granted, on con-
dition that the corpse be buried
under a certain tree “ for manuring purposes ! ”

“ F.C.” : Re animals’ “ foster ’’-freaks.
Recently our cat kittened, and the progeny was
duly drowned, whereupon I installed the bereaved
cat as foster-mother to a litter of greyhound
puppies who had just lost their dam by death.
All went well till tabby first took her newly-ac-
quired family for a promenade in the garden,
when the old tom who dominates our feline harem
summoned all the oats of the neighborhood to a
conclave; result of which was that the foster-mother
of the puppies was evidently adjudged as no
better than she ought to be. At all events, the
whole assemblage fell upon her to such purpose
that when I arrived on the scene it was only to
consign the mangled remains to the dust-box.
The foster-mother may be a very useful and desir-
able member of society, but she has a lot of ex-
plaining to do before she gets herself accepted.

“Pestle”: In reply to “0.0.,” bees
don’t gather honey, as per Dr. Watts; they
gather nectar—a peculiar secretion found at the
base of the stigmas, sepals and petals of the
flower, containing cane-sugar. This is taken into
the bee s honey-sac, or stomach, and there con-
verted into honey containing grape- sugar. If the
flower of the manuka is poisonous, the poison is
probably in the seed contained in the ovary, which
the bees cannot touch. There is no poison in the
bark.

“ Steele Rudd ” : Like the native-bear, the
centipede carts its young about on its back—not
in ones or twos but in scores. Sometimes the
body of a centipede is literally covered with
young, so small and olosely wedged as to be
scarcely perceptible.

“ Raspberry ” : Knew an old hunter once
named Adam. Erom his simple ways of living
and the plainness of his speech, he was, probably,
a direct descendant of the original gardener. He
generally lived in a hollow tree, but, as the atmo-
sphere of the camp, owing to the numerous car-
cases around, became too high-toned even for
Adam, he often shifted his belongings to fresher
latitudes. His only garments were a shirt and
kangaroo-skin breeches, so daubed with grease
and blood that they were absolutely weather-
proof, and a tiger-skin waistcoat for state occa-
sions. He was so tough a specimen that I don’t
believe you could have bled him. Have often
seen him put a large packing-needle into his
mouth and draw it out through his cheek, and the
hole would close up as though it were indiarubber.
Poor, old Adam got all his hair singed off through
embracing his fire too close on a cold night. It
HIS HAREM.
Pictured herewith a white man who, in the old days on
Fraser's Island (Q.), rejoiced in a dusky harem, and was
obviously nothing loth to have the fact placed on record by the
camera.
only burnt the hair. Skin too tough. Went
home one night with the blues, and was found a
second time burnt egg-bald. Was taken to Hobart
(Tas.) hospital, and when let out took it into his
head to smash half the windows in the city. All
the pumpkin-headed police in the capital went for
old Adam, and he was finally caged up in New
Norfolk Asylum. Here he pined away—pined
for his hollow tree and rat-tail soup—and died.
Had he lived till the present time he would have
made an excellent Federal leader, for he had the
hide of a rhinoceros and the heart of a lion. But
the bush was his Paradise, and his Eve—well, he
never took her seriously.

“ S.R.” : Reckon ’roo breeds every eight
months. When a joey’s at foot there’s generally
another in the pouch, perhaps two. (Wallaby
may have as many as four.) When “old man ”is
pursued closely by a dog he often makes for a
waterhole and jumps in—yet he can’t swim
stroke. Sometimes, though, he drowns Stumpy,
but only when he has his toe on the bottom ;
when he hasn’t he drowns.

“Nemo ” : Re par. 21/l/’99. Well-known
that pleuro makes cattle dangerous. Give an
affected beast a wide berth, even if a quiet worker
or a domesticated old milker. Apropos : There is
nothing like-being chased by a bovine to make a
man sprint. Went once with a gout-crippled old
milkman to inspect a cow down with pleuro a
couple of hundred yards from the stockyard, and
the old chap proceeded to “ persuade ” her on to
her legs by twisting her tail. She jumped up
and came at us so suddenly that I had no time to
remember my companion’s crippled condition, and
left for the fence. Had only got about half-way
when something whizzed past like an arrow, and
when I clambered on to said fence I found the
crippled milkman sitting on other side waiting for
me. The gout left him for at least a month after-
wards.
* * * *
“ A.A.A.” : Re old working - bullocks.
Curious that once a worker is properly broken in
he'seldom uses his horns either defensively or
offensively. This is probably due to the fact that
if he feels nasty in the team he can’t use his horns
and has to bring his hind legs into play. Force
of habit does the rest. “ Sool ” a dog on to an
unyoked worker, and the bullock will always keep
his rear to the enemy, lashing-out when he thinks
he has a chance of reaching his tormentor. Have
never seen a worker attempt to horn a dog, or
anything else. If a stranger appears to be giving
one of ’em more than ordinary attention (and
most of them won’t allow thertiselves to be over-
hauled by anyone but their own driver), the bul-
lock will follow the man carefully with his eye
until the latter gets where he can be duly kicked
on the knee-cap.

“ Dumaresq ” writes :
Re the Sacred Ibis and the blacks’ blankets. The
Sacred Ibis, no doubt, has a good billet, and he must
make some sort of a show for his money. Lately, I hear,
he has been collecting, by force if necessary, all the old
blacks to put them somewhere on an island. At Texas,
a few weeks ago, there was a race-meeting, and the
blacks, about haif-a-dozen, fled to the N.S.W.'side of river
to be out of reach of the Ibis. Only one of them, an old
man named Parramatta, went to the races, saying that if
they tried to send him away he would tomahawk some of
them, and then they could shoot him. All he wanted, he
said, was to be let live in his own country. This old
blackfellow is unique, as he never drinks and will always
work when he can get work, although he is nearer 70 than
60. There are not now over a dozen blacks on this river and
its tributaries above Goondiwindi. Old Parramatta keeps
out of Queensland most of the time, as he is afraid of
being made a J.P. You would understand this if you
lived here.

Bungaree, near Ballarat, is essentially
Irish. The chief local Sunday amusements are
“hurling” and attending funerals. On one
occasion the mourners got thirsty on their way to
Ballarat new cemetery, and the hearse was ordered
fin ahead while the boys refreshed. The under-
taker waited long at the cemetery but no mourners
arrived. They had “ quenched ”so liberally that
they had wandered off to the old cemetery.

“ A.E.G.” : Was looking at some snake-
charming t’other day when a bushman came up
with a red-bellied black snake in a box. Bushy
offered to give the long-haired charmer a fiver if
he would put his snake round his neck, but the
offer was declined. Apropos, why do snake-
charmers all wear long hair ? Is there anything
in it, or is it because they are all “ Professors ”?

“6x8”: Re aborigines-protection. I
advise Mr. Meston to take a turn through N.Q.
sugar districts. Not satisfied with the cheap Jap.
and kanaka-labor brought to their doors by a pie-
bald Govt., many cane-cockies rope-in the blacks
and make them sink holes and plant and trash
cane. Wages, a few shin-bones and a pound or
two of “ sheep-wash. Of course the nigs, don’t
do much work, but even that little is contrary to
the Act, and saves miserable cookies the stingy
kanaka wages.
The digestive powers of the “ go-
hanna ” are even ahead of those of the emu.
The gohanna will digest anything he can
swallow, and he is not at all particular as to
what he does swallow. A settler tells of
having come upon a large, warty dragon of
the above species who had made a valiant
attempt to swallow the freshly-pickled skele-
ton of a calf, and had stuck at the knee-joint.
The reptile was making valiant efforts to
cover that joint, but failed, 'and after a
quarter of an hour he went to sleep, in the
blessed hope of something turning up. He
recognised that he could go no further, but
was too game to retire, and so the settler
left him. Passing that way a few days
later, the man noticed that the igu. had de-
parted ; so also had the shin-bone of the calf,
which had the appearance of having been
dissolved just below the knee. The go-
hanna had simply waited till the section
he had negotiated had digested.
Herewith The Bulletin prints a por-
trait of Thekla Dubberke, the heroine of the
Melbourne boot-box tragedy. Who would
think that this sweet, nun-like creature, with
those heaven-turned eyes, could be identical
with the self-possessed witness who, describ-
ing how she heard of the finding of the
body of Mabel Ambrose, testified : “ I said
to Madame that, if I had known the body
would float, I would have ripped it open ! ”
What cheats some of these demure, sancti-
fied, gentle-voiced women are ! How much
chance has a mere coarse man against them
before an ordinary jury and an impression-
able Judge?

was still full of ink.

At Golden Point (Vic.), Lee May, an
eminent Chinese physician, being suspected of
“putting away” a fan-tan swindle, had to be
rescued by the polioe from a crowd of his infuri-
ated compatriots. Lee May then went to the
joss-house, and swore before Joss that he was
innocent, but upon being challenged to go to the
cemetery, and invoke the spirits of his ancestors,
he remarked that the memory of the dead was
not to be mixed up with such low-down matters
as fan-tan. An observation which The Bulletin
coi.i inends to the notice of the superior Christian
person, who is always asking high Heaven to
specially interest itself in his trumpery little
personal concerns.
Business, Robbery, etc.
A quaint little fraud which has flourished in
Australia for 26 years or more is that worked by the man
in nautical riff who wants to sell some doth-—just enough
for a suit of clothes. He plods about the city on foot or
haunts the country in a buggy, with his blue clothes and
his cap ; and he states that he is the mate or steward or
purser of a steamer that is now in dock being painted.
Also he has some extraordinarily good serge for sale at
3(1 a yard, worth 7/- at the least—the kind the cheap city
draper sells for 7sd. He goes round secretly to the back-
door and states in a secretive whisper that he has only
enough for one suit left, like the city newsboy who rushes
the sale of his Evbnnoobb on the ground that "it is the very
last copy. Most people lose their heads when they are
offered the very last sample of anything; they have a
dazed sort of feeling in the small of the back that if they
don’t snatch this one opportunity by the forelock they
may regret it ever after and the rest of their lives be
overshadowed by disappointment. If there was only one
attack of measles left in the whole world it would be
rushed after in the same fashion. Generally the fraud
says that he has imported the serge from some far country
where the only genuine article grows on trees, or that he
got it off a wreck, or that it is an article which his aged
mother gave to him when he left his home in England,
with instructions to treasure it up till he got to Aus-
tralia, and then go up country to Goodooga and get it
made into a suit, and wear it ever afterwards.
One mariner, who has weathered many storms
in his small clothing emporium, has pursued this game
in Sydney for 15 solid years, and acquired wealth thereby.
The faith of the purchaser in the clothes he buys in semi-
seeresy from his back-door seaman is pathetic. One of
them got his suit made up—but the very night it was
finished burglars broke into the tailor’s shop and carried
away the pants part of the outfit, along with many
other things. The other things were never seen again,
but this particular pair of pants they tore up like blot-
ting-paper, and left in the next street. Purchaser was
inconsolable, and almost wept. He said that the pants
must have been wilfully destroyed by dynamite, as noth-
ing short of that would have wrecked the indestructible
material, and declared he would never, never get another
suit like it. Wanted to know why the police hadn’t heard
the explosion when the pants blew up. Refused to take
the coat and vest, saying the sight of them only embit-
tered his grief and reminded him of his—boohoo !—irre-
parable, bitter loss. Also, he threatened to destroy the
tailor with law proceedings in the Supreme Court, and
afterwards in the Privy Council, if necessary, unless an
exactly similar pair of pants was forthcoming. Tailor spent
his spare time for three months trying to match the
material, which was of an extraordinary hue and pattern,
and at last saw the required article in a corner of a
wholesale warehouse that he dealt with. Warehouseman
said he wouldn’t charge for it, but would throw it in
as discount. So the pants were made at last, and the
customer put on the clothes in the tailor’s back room,
and the whole outfit split as he was going out of the door.
Then he accused the tailor-man of having basely poured
destructive chemicals on the clothes to destroy them
because the material wasn’t bought at his shop, but this
time the clothes-gentleman’s patience gave out, and he
threw his customer half-way across the street.
• * * *
The notes of the long-defunct Melb. Provincial
and Suburban Bank arestillgivingtrouble. Recently, aman
on his way to Perth paid a f 1-note to a business-person in
the evening, and before the coach left next morning was
threatened by the recipient with arrest for having passed
on him a valueless note. The traveller protested he had
never even seen a Provincial note before ; but the other
stuck to his point, and had nearly extracted a fiver from
the victim as hush-money, when another resident hap-
pened to get the hang of the little game and smashed it.
“Drop it, old man,” he said; “that Provincial note of
yours has been played off on too many chaps already.”
* * » »
A countryman in Melbourne for a spree was
robbed of about £lOO by a temporary' fair friend, who,
when arrested, said to another woman, “ Mary, do go and
pawn the bellows and get me a beer before I go.” Mary
duly pawned the bellows, and brought the beer; and
when, eventually, accused was discharged because no
trace of the money' was obtainable, the bellows were taken
out of pop and the cash (in notes) extracted from them.
The notes had been pushed in at the air-valve.

At the Golden Topknot battery lately they put
through a crushing for a man which did not come up to
expectations, and, the prospector not having the cash to
settle with the mill, “ Captain ” Blank took the cake to
town to send it through the bank, the digger going with
him. When entering bank digger asked to have one last
look at the cake, which the captain took from'his pocket
and handed to him. Digger (carefully putting it in his
pocket): “Very sorry, cap’n—can’t settle for crushing
this time. You’ll have to wait till we strike it a bit
richer.” The captain now say’s that if the Angel Gabriel
came along with stone to crush he wouldn’t get his gold
until he planked the cash.

“Timi”: I am fast losing all confidence in
S.M. Herald as a financial authority. On Jan. 20 I read
the following cablegram—
THE BANK RATE.
London, Jan. 19.
The Bank of England rate of discount has been
advanced to per cent.
Happening to know that the Bank of England directors
meet once a week to consider the rate of discount, I
turned up last Friday’s commercial cables in the Herald
and found that the rate then was 4 per cent. To
“ advance ” from 4 per cent, to 3$ per cent, is surely a
unique financial experience. The Sydney D.T. man
correctly rendered to-day’s cable thus":—“The Bank of
England rate is now at 3$ per cent.”
Here is another S.M. Herald commercial
vagary. On the Ist Oct. last it made this statement :
“The Bank of England rate stands at 3 per cent., from
which it was reduced from 2$ on Sept. 22.” This balder-
dash was gravely repeated in its issues of Oct. Bth, 15th,
and 22nd.
Yet another instance of the Herald’s surpris-
ing accuracy. In issue of Dec. 3rd last, in its tabulated
share-market report, it stated that 2| per cent, consols
were at 110$, on Nov. 25th, and on Dec. 2nd they were at
101$. I recollect that this information gave a dreadful
shock to my nerves. A drop of 9in Goschens—the most
gilt-edged of British securities—must at least mean a war
with a first-class power. But the general news showed
that the white-winged bird of peace was flapping its
wings as usual. True the same table carried with it a
balm for my perturbation, as it mentioned that Goschens
were unchanged !
* * * *
A recently-published list shows 51 English
companies in existence which profess to work mines in
N.S.W. The total capital is £8,918,600 —about £175,000
nominal capital for each mine to pay dividends on. Of
this £6,485,500 (£1,292,803 cash ; the rest paid-up shares)
was grabbed by the “ vendors,” leaving £2,433,000, or as
much of it as could be extracted from the shareholders,
to pay all the miscellaneous commissions and expenses,
directors’ fees, cost of London offices, and to develop the
mines. The Sydney Daily Tbleorapti’s mining writer
estimates that £200,000 would far more than cover the
amount that really came to N.S.W. for the purchase of all
these properties, and a very moderate amount more would
cover all the shareholders’ capital actually spent in
mining on them. The “ investment of British capital in
the Australian mining industry ” is the sickest fraud on
earth. About 98 per cent, of the alleged invested capital
never gets further than London. Badly as John Bull has
been let in over Australian mines, he has probably got
quite as much out of Australia as he actually put into it,
and Australia would be richer to-day if no London-bossed
company had ever stuck a pick into this country. For that
matter a lot of them never put in any larger weapon than
a corkscrew.

On this page appears a portrait of Charles Ban-
nerman, to whom the Sydney public is about to give a
testimonial prior to his intended trip to England The
photo, is a life-like presentment of the dashing batsman
as he appeared in his salad days. In England oricketers
of Bannerman s stamp invariably get a benefit after eight
to ten years play [ and Bannernian was an active repre-
sentative of N.h.W. from 71 to , 86, and ever since has
been prominent on the cricket-field as mentor and umpire.
His connection with Sydney cricket dates from ’6l, when
as a little fellow in pinafores he attended to the second
scoring-board in the English match on Sydney Domain
for m those primitive days the board was only altered
every ten runs. In the following year Charley was one
80m . e members of the old National
Cncket Club in the Domain, and at the end of practice
f o r : Pa r, k suggested that the youngster should take
a strike. Mr. Park was struck with the resemblance of
his style to that of Gaffyn, and on that day Bannerman
became a member of the Nationals. At the break-up of
the club a couple of years later he joined the Warwicks,
ana from that out his progress was rocket-like. First
year in the 3rd eleven he took first in batting and bowl-
ing, next year ditto in the second eleven, and the third
} ear saw hirri in the first eleven with first batting average
and second in bowling. Bannerman, who in his day was
looked upon as Australia’s most finished and brilliant)
Put up some big figures and giant hits. In
the 78 English tour he got 1300 runs for 59 innings, 800
CRICKETER “ CHARLIE ” BANNERMAN.
Photo., Creelman & Co.
more than any other man during that tour, then went to
the States and finished up with an average of 36. He was
the first Australian to make a century on English soil,
when at Leicestershire he made 133, run out; as also the
first to make a century in Australia against an
English team: he scored 165 and retired hurt, at
Melbourne in ’77, in the first match in which an
Australian team played the Britishers level, and which
the Australians won. Playing in a match at Montreal,
Canada, he made 125 not out. Outside the ground there
was a shop with a very large plate-glass window, and,
noticing it while going to the ground, Charley said
jokingly to his companions, “ I'll put a ball through that
glass to-day.” The right ball came along in due course,
and Charley went nap for the window, the ball striking
the brick wall within a foot of the glass. G. F. Grace and
W. R. Gilbert used to follow the team all round England,
playing with the eighteens, far which they used to get a
lot of chaffing from the Australians. One day, at Long-
sight ground, Bannerman jokingly told one of them he’d
knock the cover off the first ball he bowled to him ; and
sure enough, getting the wood on to a nicely-pitched ball,
he knocked it out of the ground into a train which hap-
pened to be passing. The ball was never found, and
Bannerman holds the distinction of being the only man
who ever lost a ball off the bat in a first-class match.
Playing at Inglewood (Vic.), he hit one into the grand-
stand, the ball unfortunately striking and breaking the
arm of a lady, on which an infant was reclining. Banner-
man, though past the meridian of an athlete’s life, is still
an active man in the cricket world as coach and umpire.

Photography as a pastime has taken deep
root on Australian soil and bids fair to go on grow-
ing luxuriantly in the future. This is not to be
wondered at, as it certainly is one of the most
pleasant pastimes that anyone could adopt, and
with the improved methods which modern science
has enlisted to aid the picture-makers the opera-
tions are simplicity itself. Anyone who contem-
plates starting photography should be certain to
use only the best materials and have a good
camera. The latter can be had at any price to suit
the pocket of the buyer and materials in any
desired quantity. It is therefore with pleasure
that attention is drawn to the advertisement of
Messrs Harrington and Co., Ltd., of this city, a
firm well known in photographic circles as study-
ing in every way the wants of their customers.
The beginner cannot do better than consult them
as to his outfit, while the extensive professional
studio will find every article for the various opera-
tions of his practice to hand at a moment’s notice.*

In the cast of a certain “ strong ” melodrama at present
running is a very strifltly-broug-ht-up maiden. Her part
in the performance is over early, when she is free to go
hbme or stay, as she likes. “ What do you think of the
piece as a whole ?" she was asked on one occasion. “ The
piece as a whole!” she repeated in horrified accents.
“ Youjdon’t imagine I would ever stay and see the acting,
do you, when you know it is a theatrical piece, and how
wicked we all think it is to go to the theatre !”
. “Holdar”: Witnessed a recent “benefit” matinee in
aid of somebody’s passage-money to elsewhere, or some-
thing. A miscellaneous bill-of-fare was stuffed with the
names of long-resting, almost-forgotten “pros.”—who
mostly appeared —and those of “ pros.” with good city
engagements-who mostly failed to answer the printed
intimation of the “ turn.” As usual the beneficiare was
down for the final item, but a scantily-stocked house
greatly thinned as the programme proceeded, and the
end was mainly vacancy, dust and a few faint, farewell
words from the more or less benefited person as the re-
maining remnant of audience filed sadly out. The “ bene-
fit ” artifice is suffering from exhaustion in this country.
“ 0.J.”: Re paragraph about Ebenezer Ward, of S.A.
Writer remembers him on the Age— not Herald. Also
recollects that Ward got soft on a very handsome actress
—now living in Melb. and still comely. She said that she
would never marry anyone but an actor. That diverted
Ward’s thoughts from newspaper work to theatrical biz.
For some time he exhibited his thin, lank figure and lean
legs in super parts, patiently waiting for the opportunity.
This scribe remembers retiring to bed one night, in
Murphy’s Castleinaine Hotel, dog-tired after a long coach
journey. Was awakened by a glare of light; and beheld
Ward arranged in some sort of mediaeval rags, posing and
spouting before the looking-glass. And he thought then, .
in his green innocence, that Ward was balmy. But he
wasn’t—only up to the chin in love. He got over that,
and chucked the stage; went over to S.A. and discovered
his line of work.
Patti, at 56, is the bride of an athletic baron of 28.
“ M‘G.”\ : How is it that the Melbourne theatres, so
perfect in*other respects, continue to serve out such eheap
and tawdry programmes? Bad paper, execrable print-
ing, and lithography which would have been deemed
second-class in the days of Johnny Fawkner?
In a highly realistic English drama, now raging in the
provinces, the villain brands the heroine on the soles of
the feet to make her confess something or other. He ties
her down after a great struggle and drags off her boots
and stockings. Then he brings on a realistic-looking
poker, and smoke goes up from her feet when he brands
them (feet soles carefully turned to the audience), and
her shrieks are ’arrowing.

Kenningham tells several good yarns of W. S. Gilbert
and his rdady, spicy wit. One morning when they were
rehearsing “ Utopia (Limited)” the librettist, appealing
to the-company in general, asked “ Has anybody seen my
wife ? ” One of the chorus girls, with deferential eager-
ness, said “She’s round behind.” “Yes, I know that,’’-
grunted Gilbert, “ but where has she got to ? ”
Two backblockers visited Sydney Criterion gallery the
other night when “Hamlet” was on. Looked on with
great interest until appearance of the Ghost made them
doubtful. “ Wot piece is this?” one of them asked the
ticket-taker. “ Hamlet,” was the reply. “ Wot! Not
‘ His Nat’ rel Life’?” “Oh, no.” “Well, I’m d dl
Dampier ought to be pole-axed.” Then they cleared out
in disgust.
“ Pro.” :Re the actress who savagely bit the actor. I
heard it was during a scene while her head lovingly
reposed on his shoulder. Intrinsically similar to an
occurrence in the finish of a Flemington horse-raoe, when
Painter seized Kite on the haunch.
“ H.” : Unusually prompt, Melb. Publio Library gives
us Rostand’s complete ‘ Cyrano de Bergerao,’ both in
French and English. The poetry is lovely, but the plot
is so absurd that it is difficult to see how this play can be
made acceptable on the English boards. The furore in
Paris is through the hero being written and wrapped so
entirely round Coquelin. It is such a fit as Rip for
Jefferson. The play will probably leave the stage with
Coquelin, but will always be treasured for its flashing
literary gems. The central idea, the pivot of the whole
thing, is utterly false. A woman’s love for a handsome
man is kept alive with language supplied to him by a
poetic ugly man, who loves her, but sacrifices himself for
the other. The outcome, in its spirit, is paltry French
sentimentalism. Yet the power of the writer carries you
through swimmingly. The house of cards comes down
on after reflection. Only genius could thus bolster up
such a paradox.
“A.0.C.”: Great grievance of a professional man
who recovered nominal damages for libel some time ago
from a N.S. W. daily is that the defendant paper
has since neglected at all times to mention his name in
“ notices.” It is asserted that his patronymic has even
been misspelt ( unintentionally , of course) in advts. But
surely a man who makes a newspaper “sit up” to the
extent of some hundreds on trivial grounds doesn’t expect
to be rewarded with nice, buttery slabs of commendation,
does he ? Newspapers are very human institutions.
“ It is improper to display a stocking on the stage,
unless there is a leg in it ."—Gilbert.
There appears in print an interesting letter from the
Rev. Walter Reynolds, of the Unitarian Church, Man-
chester, to Miss Jeanne Burgoyne, who lately appeared
as Lady Godiva in that city (presumably under the safe
cover of tights and a theatre-roof, and not a mere street
performance like the other). In said letter the rev. gent
descants at great length and with some warmth on
Jeanne's “statuesque form, graceful pose, fine, physical
proportions, and her sweet, beauteous soul upborne by
the pure, noble and divine thoughts,” &c. After which
gorgeous and fleshy revel, much repeated, he winds up
by informing her that he is going to lecture on the human
form next Sunday, and offers her a front pew where she
will be well seen. A Unitarian cleric is held to be some-
what outside the general church-pale, but that little bit
of scratch-my-back-Tve-scratched-yours is genuinely par-
sonic. And the cleric gets the best of it. The undraped
back of Godiva is much nicer scratching than the murky
coat of the preacher.
A well-known circus was in a bakblox town, and the
tent was rapidly filling. The eye of a long, slab-sided
Cornstalk was caught by the big head-line of a poster
which began, “ The Great Caravan.” Stringybark paid
his shilling, went in, and after having had a good look
round, returned to the doorkeeper—“ Look ’ere, mister,”
he said, “ I’ve seen your lions, an’ your elliphint, an’
your tiger, but where the is the beast you call the
great ca-raa-van ? ”
Rather bewildering advt. in a London paper :
GLOBE THEATRE ..Lessee and Manager, Mr. John Hare.
Mr. John L. Shine’s Season.
Mr. Lewis Waller’s
Enormously Successful Play,
The Three Musketeers,
By Henry Hamilton.
And in another advt. Lewis Waller figures as a member
of Beerbohm Tree’s co. in another “Three Musketeers”
play, by Sydney Grundy. The craze for new dramatio
versions of Dumas’ much-dramatised story seems to have
been brought about by “ Under the Red Robe.”
There is quite a boom in “Three Musketeers” in the
English thealrical world, and doubtless the backwash will
be felt here later on. There is a point interesting to Aus-
tralians in Dumas’ big novel, which the present scribe has
not seen pointed out. tn Chap. 52, Dumas represents
Miladi in captivity as “digging for herself an abyss of
dark reflections-a gloomy bell—at the gate of which she
had almost left hope behind her.” One of her reflections
is that “ it is D’Artagnan who holds her a prisoner and
who is going to transport her to some loathsome Botany
Bay, some infamous Tyburn of the Indian Ocean.” Con-
sidering that the period of the novel is the period of
Card. Richelieu, and that there was no Botany Bay then,
Miladi’s reflection is a bit anticipatory. It is as though
Scott made Ivanhoe an inmate of Pentridge.
“ Mac.” : The failure of stage Chinamen is due to the
fact that the stage Chow is invariably a comic character,
while the Mongol of real life is an earnest purveyor of
vegetables who never cracks a joke in the whole course of
his existence. A true presentment is, consequently, out
of the question, but a “ free translation ” ought to be
possible to a good artist. As for Lauri’s “ Geisha” crea-
tion, it is a woeful excrescence that could not be properly
expiated under (say) six months’ hard.
“T.D.” : References to Cesca Yollugi, the Melb. song-
stress and pupil of Signora Coy, now figuring in the
Sydney panto., indirectly recall the immense service W.
S. Lyster rendered to Australian musical art. Our best
singers have either been pupils of Lyster’s importations,
or pupils of their pupils. The band has decreased in
recent years—Madame Simonsen, Lucy Chambers, and
Armes Beaumont are dead, and Steffani is at present in
Europe. We still have Madame Venosta (in Maoriland),
Leonora Fabris (somewhere in N.S.W.), Signora Baratti
(in Sydney), Signora Coy (Melb.), and, of course, con-
ductor Zelman. Farley and one or two others must not
be forgotten, either.

he, according to rule 5, play on the yellow?—As the
game is usually played, the potter of the last pyramid-
ball has the option of playing on any ball next stroke,
after which he (or his successor) must play on the yellow.
