Unprejudiced Doctor.
In the average medical man prejudice and pro-
fessional caste is very strong. He is educated in
this atmosphere; and his associations in after
years but strengthen his early teachings. Yet
there are minds in the profession, active, alert,
vigorous, abreast and ahead of their day and
generation, who refuse to be bound or stand still.
If they cannot cure with their list of preparations,
they are willing to try other remedies, albeit they
are of another school, or perhaps no school at all.
Scores of reputable physicians, becoming discour-
aged at the results of their prescriptions, have put
aside prejudice and prescribed Warner’s Safe Cure
with the most gratifying results. They have
freely attested these facts over their own signa-
tures. Dr. Robert Shaw, of Rockhampton, holds
the highest medical honors, and is well and favor-
ably known throughout the north of Queensland,
consequently his experience will interest Austra-
lians. He writes :
“I have much pleasure in stating that I pre-
scribed Warner’s Safe Cure very frequently whilst
acting on the Cairns Herberton Railway Contract,
and obtained more satisfactory results from it than
any formula; of mv own prescribing.”—ROßEßT
SHAW, L.D.S., M. 8., D.A.*

POLITICAL
POINTS
We already have “ Brisbane.” What is
wrong, then, with “ Reidbane ” as the name of
the Federal capital ?
Sydney Telegraph is now evidently
getting ready to wallop the perfidious Joss who
went back on it so badly at the Premiers’ Con-
ference.
Australian Workers’ Union (A.W.U.),
which absorbed the Shearers’ Union, has just got
rid of the liabilities which the run of strikes and
look-outs from ’93 onwards left behind it.
“ Cook ! ” You couldn’t possibly call the
Australian Federal capital “Cook.” It would
sound as ridiculous as calling it “ Housemaid ” or
“Butler.” Sound will have to be considered as
much as meaning.
SKETCH AT MELB. OPERA HOUSE.
“ Billy” Whitburn, the only comedian who can
go down with a Melb. audience all the year round.
The Argus, throwing off at the “ anti-
quated nominee ” Upper Houses of some of the
Australian provinces, in a late Federation leader,
has been the laugh of the week in Melb. among
Billites and anti-Billites alike.
A move is being made in- Melb. Trades
Hall to have all members of the Labor-party in
Parliament ex-officio members of the Council, but
without the right to vote. This should tend to
make the proceedings of the Council of more
direct political interest.
The Premiers’ conference which put the
coping-stone on Federation consisted of three
titled (Turner, Braddon and Forrest) and three
untitled (Reid, Kingston and Dickson) Premiers.
Dickson is the loneliest of the lot —the man whose
mouth waters at sight of a knight’s ribbon.
“ I will work for the Bill with my whole
heart and soul.” — Premier Reid.
“ I’ll work for Federation now,”
Says Georgie Houston Reid, P.C.
“ In spying that I mean I will
Let Federation work for me 1"
Clairvoyant Freetraders frighten them-
selves and their clan with the whispered revelation
that on N.S.W. Parliament meeting after the
Referendum on the Federal Bill, Reid’s first
measure will be one to establish a stiff all-round
Protectionist tariff, as a forerunner of Federal
union.
The Seddon Government intends establish-
ing penny postage throughout Maoriland—the
first penny postage system coveringa whole Austr-
alasian province. It means an immediate sacrifice
of .£70,000 annually, but the M.L. postal depart-
ment showed J 635.000 profit last year, and there
are hopes that the increased business will shortly
balance the accounts again.
S.A. Government, under its recently-
established policy of resuming large estates for
oloser settlement, has already repurchased 26,000
aores, and is steadily going ahead with reasonable
success. Westralia is doing ditto, and Victoria
Jhas passed the necessary law, though the Turner
Govt, doesn’t seem to have laid its hands on any
of the big estates as yet. In this, as in almost
every other progressive idea, Maoriland led the
way and keeps anead.
The blood of the youth killed by that
rotten old Albury boiler is on the head of N.S.W.
Leg. Gounoil, which for years has steadily refused
to legislate for the inspection of land-boilers. In
the Albury explosion about 6 cwt. of boiler was
hoisted nearly 150 yards right on the top of a
3-storey hotel.
Some of the political parties in Queens-
land : A.L.F. candidates, Labor Oppositionists,
Drakeists, Independent Ministerialists, Minis-
terialists, candidates unattached to either Labor
or Drake but opposed to Govt., endorsed and
unendorsed Govt, men, Independent Labor, Inde-
pendent Opposition, Liberals, Radicals, Socialists.
A Babel of parties.
It is likely enough that in the interests of
historical justice an able member of N.S.W.
Assembly will, on an appropriate occasion, fully
recapitulate, for the benefit of Hansard, the
whole of Premier Reid’s wrigglings in regard to
Federation, so that the oily one may hereafter be
alloted exactly the credit to which he is entitled.
“Biero”: The BuLLETiN-prophecy that
either Wentworth or Parkes will be the name of
the Commonwealth capital is very likely-looking.
But Parkes sounds commonplace, and Wentworth
(“first of first-born Australians”) doesn’t, so, in the
end, Wentworth will about win. Besides, Parkes
is too recently dead to have as many partisans as
might otherwise be the case. All the British-
suggested names—Britannia, Victoria, and so on—
are right out of it. Australia has few historical
gods, and of them Federationist Wentworth per-
haps looms biggest.
Litigious Colin M‘Kay Smith declares the
action of N.S.W. in handing over any part of the
security for her ,£(50,000,000 debt to third parties
(a Federal Parliament, to wit) is legally challenge-
able. Mr. Smith’s tilt at the alleged unconstitu-
tional payment of members in N.S.W., finally
routed by the Privy Council, was an expensive
experiment, and one that needs to be recalled
when he whistles anew on the dogs of Law. As
it happens there is no security for any province’s
debt except the people’s promise to pay. And if
the public assets were the security then they are
being handed over to third parties every time the
State sells a bit of land.
One great point in selecting the Federal
capital is to find a place which can furnish an un-
failing supply of good water for a possible popula-
tion of 100,000 or so, in order that the Federal
Government may wash itself occasionally. Wagga,
with the Murrumbidgee handy, or Bombala,
with the Bombala River alongside and the
Snowy not far off, or some few spots in Liver-
pool ranges, or almost any place on the Darling
or Murray might qualify, and Queanbeyan or
Bungendore might have a look in on the strength
of their adjacent Lake George. But there are
doubts about many of the present candidates.
The Federal Government can’t live on beer alone.
The election of Worker, Higgs for the
North Ward, Brisbane, is indicative of many
things. It shows how completely the old red
Socialistn-in-our-Time bogey has ceased to appal
well-to-do citizens. It betokens a heaving of the
municipal gorge at the gross incapacity of the
old-time city aldermen who allowed every matter
of public concern—gas, trams, &c. —to drift into
the hands of syndicates. And it should warn Q.
Government that it’s high time it played another
card than the old “Take Us or Revolution”
ticket when the editor of the chief alleged revolu-
tionary organ is accepted as a city father by loyal,
Respectat >le property-owners.
Progress, the now Q. democratic weekly,
supplies two instances of how the Q. Agricultural
Lands Purchase Act is being worked :
The Minister for fluids has purchased at Port Newry,
in Mackay electorate, an estate of 6SOO acres at £3 13s.
per acre. This land has been valued, for rating purposes,
by the local Divisional Board at l£s. 6d. per acre, arid, ac-
cording to the report of Mr: Hume, of the- Land Court,
that is somewhere about its real value. If that transac-
tion has been effected in the interests of closer settlement,
it looks as though the settlers would start with a pretty
considerable handicap. Here is another example of how
the Act works. A little block of 566 acres at Clifton has
been purchased from a Mrs. Perkins at £2 per acre. This
land, the A'lora Guardian says, is nothin;/ but'a stony
mountain, not even good grazing ground. In whose in*
. t crest has that purchase been made ?

Young Australia actually has
his attention distracted for a
moment from his precious
cricketers by the extraordinary
turn of speed shown in the last
lap of the Federal handicap.
Sachse, youngest member of the truly
awful Vic. Leg. Council, has just returned from a
long visit to Europe, and admits that “ as a result
of my travels I have got rid of a lot of silly
little ideas.” If this is so, Sachse will hardly feel
at home any more in the crimson plush chamber,
where silly little ideas are all the ideas that
prevail. Why not export the whole Council with
the same object? The experiment, if at all suc-
cessful, would be dirt cheap.
“ Buckshot,” re “ Workers and Wasters ”
(Bulletin, 14/l/’99) :
Such shameless robbery is in great part obviated in
Maoriland by provisions of Contractors’ and Workmen’s
Lieu Act and Workmen’s Wages Act. Under them the
employer has to retain 25 per cent, of total contract
money for 31 days after completion of contract, to give
workmen a chance to attach same, if necessary, to extent
of wages due. Had the Australian Labor members been
in earnest, counterparts of these, and of the Maoriland
Truck Acts, would ere now have found a place among the
statutes of this country.

THE GREAT HEALER.
S.A. LASSIE: “ Well, dear brother, how is your
soul to-day? ”
PATIENT (rather deaf, thinking of his complaint) :

In at least half-a-dozen Q. electorates a
judicious “ Independent Oppositionist ” is being
bulcocked into the fray. The majority of the
rank - and - file won’t touch the contingent-vote
(which would minimise this evil) and those who
do mark their papers, with few exceptions, make
a bungle; the contingent-vote being such a three-
cornered, 15-puzzle affair that 95 per cent, of the
returning officers don’t understand it.
Henniker Heaton, under date London,
6/l/’99 :
I shall be glad if you will accept a silver penny, struck
at the Mint for me in honor of Imperial penny postage
inauguration. I know The Bulletin is generous as well
as venomous. Will you help me to persuade the Aus-
tralian Governments to allow our English letters to go to
Australia for one p-nny each? It will not cost you a
farthing, and the only reason against it is that the Trea-
surers fear your people will demand inland penny post.
Sydney Telegraph, which always goes mad
when Protection is mentioned, broke out afresh
last Monday:
In Victoria, in 1897, they valued their coal output at
10s. per ton, and in Maoriland, where there is a good deal
of serviceable fuel, the price, assisted by Protection, was
placed as high as 10s. lod. per ton. . . . Victorian coal-
mines live upon Government contracts and bonuses, and
an inferior product is thus given a price 4s. 6d. per ton
above the output of this colony (N.S.W.).
And yet, strangely enough, Maoriland doesn’t
protect its coal industry and never did, and coal
isn’t “ assisted by Protection ” there in any way.
And Victoria doesn’t pay bonuses on coal except
that it carries local coal by rail at the same rate
as N.S.W. does. And N.S.W. does pay an in-
direct bonus on coal ; it is protected to the extent
that local coal is exempted from wharfage charges
at the two great coal ports, Newcastle and
Wollongong, which means indirect assistance to the
extent of from 3d. to fid. a ton. There are so
many small spots of Protection on the glorious
sun of N.S.W. Foreign-trade that it is sometimes
difficult to be sure that there isn’t more spot than
The Drakeites are known (somewhat con-
temptuously) in Q. politics as “the Remnant.”
Drake himself asserts they are the “ Remnant that
had not bowed to Baal.” If this be so, then Baal
is about the only god they didn’t bow to—for they
bowed (Drake excepted) to god MTlwraith, god
Griffith, and every other tinpot deity that has
sprung within the last 10 years into brief and
malodorous local existence.

—almost thanks The Bulletin for recently troun-
cing it. But, then, between times, Truth has

Considering how excited Bathurst and
Goulburn are about the ownership of the Federal
capital, it may interest the inhabitants to
know that there is a doubt whether
either city can squeeze inside the dis-
tance limit of 100 miles from Sydney.
The agreement simply says that
the Federal capital must be at
least 100 miles from Sydney ; not
100 miles following the winding
of a road or a railway—if that
were allowed even Parramat-
ta could qualify, provided
the person who measured
the distance selected his
roads carefully—but sim-
ply 100 miles. Ba-
thurst, anyhow, is
about a mile the
wrong side of the
limit; though, by
The Bulletin’s
atlas, Goulburn
should get there
with just about a
mile to spare. But
even it is a tight fit.

for a denomina-

Premier Dickson (now representing Queen-
sland on the Federation question) is in a peculiar
position. He has no mandate from the people;
no message to the Convention ; no objection what-
ever to Federation ; no desire to see it established;
no intention of putting the question before Queens-
landers at the coining election. His only right to
give advice to the men who are pulling the Con-
vention waggon forward is founded upon the un-
deniable fact that he has spent all hj s feeble
energies in eternally trying to pull back. Well—
he comes from Queensland, and that explains a
lot.
A yarn is printed in Sydney that the
British Liberals are likely to be in power after
next general election, and will appoint Lord
Carington Gov.-General of Federated Australia.
If Baron Mt. Rennie’s chances depend upon a
Liberal victory, they are small indeed, for the
position of that crowd appears to be just about
this: The old, respectable, academic Liberal
party has split into two sections—the non-progres-
sive, wealthy capitalists, who were against the
“ old ” landed aristocracy because they could not
be of it, and the downright Radicals and Socialists.
And it is highly improbable that the “ Great
Liberal Party ” will ever resurrect again; The
gap is bound to get wider, and the Socialist-
Radical-Labor Party will increase, while the
remnant of the old capitalistic Whigs are forced
into the Primrose League.
The voice of George Reid, Premier of
N.S.W.: —
I found the Braddon blot much better than any other
proposal which was possible. In the first place, the
clause in no way hunts the discretion of the Federal Par-
liament as to the method of raising revenue. On the con-
trary, it presents a distinct inducement to a Federal
Treasurer to prefer direct taxation to obtaining revenue
from Customs duties, because he qets every penny of the
direct taxation, and he only gets ss. in the £ on any Cus-
toms duties he imposes. If the argument' that the pro-
vinces would require a (.'7,000,000 or £8,000,000 tariff in
order to guarantee that they should be in the same posi-
tion after Federation were true, all I can say is that there
is nothing in the Constitution which compels the Com-
monwealth Parliament to impose or the Commonwealth
taxpayer to endure any such tariff, and any statement to
tlie contrary is absolutely without foundation.
The fact that the Braddon clause gives the Federal
Government a distinct inducement to resort largely
to direct taxation is just why The Bulletin has
stood by the good old Blot all along. Australia
wants uniform land, income, and probate taxation
and stamp duties, just about as much as it wants
uniform Customs duties. And the Blot is .a dis-
tinct inducement to the Commonwealth to take a
hand in these matters and make them uniform.

THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*
Thomas Suffield addressed a very large
audience at Newcastle last week, with the Mayor
in the chair ; and a resolution was passed enjoin-
ing the Govt, to do the honest thing and hold an
enquiry. Suffield made a strong point by alluding
to the ease with which half-a-dozen people mis-
identified Burgess, and urged how easy it was for
Ann Chambers to have made a mistake in his case
—seeing she was quite unacquainted with him.
Suffield will lecture throughout the Newcastle
district before going farther afield.

“ Bagman ” to The Bulletin :
Who was Tyson ? This question is now asked in a very
sub-rosa manner. That his parents were married there is
no doubt, and that J.T. was the offspring of said union is
also certain enough ; but suppose, for argument’s sake,
that the register of the marriage cannot be found or that
of his birth—where do his relatives in Australia come in ?
Who would be the next of kin ? So far, I believe, the
question has not been answered who J.T. u-os ; and some
anxious moments are now being passed by those inter-
ested. After all, the big estate may go into Chancery as
many others have gone before. The curator of intestate
estates must have absolute proofs before he can make any
division of asset s. How cruel of the dead millionaire that
he never made a will or in any way remembered those
who helped to make his fortune—overseers and others 1
He used to say, “Never mind, my man” (a very usual
form of speech of his)—“ I shall not forget you."
The Goldsmiths’and Silversmiths’Alliance,
of 90 King-street, Sydney, has made a new depar-
ture with its “Geisha” jewellery—named after
the quaint little Japanese songstress now filling a
great want in this country. It is built in pretty
and curious Japanese designs, of the bamboo and
chrysanthemum variety, and, considering the
beauty of Jappy art in metalwork of all
kinds, it is a wonder that the idea of
running Jap patterns wasn’t thought of before.
The Alliance also trades in every other kind of
jewellery, watches, trinkets, plate, and all other
gold and silver luxuries in beautiful designs and
at low prices, and its huge plate-glass window is
an ideal place for any fellow to dream away a
minute or two deciding on the present he will buy
for his best girl after he gets his salary raised.*

There was very nearly being no Age last
Saturday, owing to the shaft controlling all the
linotypes breaking down and rendering a dozen
costly machines useless. Luckily, the operators
understood hand-setting—in a few years a smash
of this sort will be irremediable, for the ordinary
newspaper-comp, will then be as extinct as the
moa—and the paper was got out as in days of old.

Vic. Minister Taverner arranges that de-
partmental telegrams shall be duplicated and sent
to both his office and his private house ; but recent
events have almost caused him to abandon the
practice. Wiring to the head office of a Sydney
shipping firm re some frozen-rabbit consignments,
a reply came back, “All settled ; Gill will await
you on boat”—said Gill being the co.’s Melb.
agent. But when the burly Taverner got home
that night he had an experience which led to his
turning up next day a slim and haggard man.
The wire delivered at his private house read,
“ All settled. Girl will await you on boat.”
The murder of Dan Young, the Horsham
Chinaman, seems to have occurred about five
days after Matthews was murdered by Archer at
Ulupna Creek (Vic.), and there are striking points
of similarity about the two crimes. Ford, in cus-
tody for killing Dan Young, may be guilty ; but,
if so, he is about the most incompetent murderer
Australia has yet seen. Archer was a poor-
enough specimen, weighting a body with a 21b.
oven-lid and expecting it to remain under water,
but the evidence against Ford makes him out an
absolute imbecile. The police say he clove the
Chinaman’s skull, left him lying in the house
where he would have been found in a few days
under ordinary circumstances, scattered half-a-
dozen books bearing his name about the body,
and then calmly drove off in a showy turn-out
which everybody in the district knew to be Dan
Young’s. He made no attempt to leave the
locality, but hung about with the same flashy
conveyance, and wore a watch belonging to the
dead man. The ordinary circumstances referred
to did not happen, and when the building was
searched, two months after Young’s death, the
police on duty entirely overlooked a trifle like a
human body which was lying on the floor in one
room, and it was not until three months later that
two wandering swagmen happened across the
cloven skull.

A 14-year-old girl died at Traralgon (Vio.),
the other day, of paralysis, brought on by tight-
lacing. Poor little thing ! She had only intended
to paralyse the local young men.
We are threatened with another epidemic
of tales re Queen Victoria’s infinite wisdom and
impossible leaning to Liberalism in politics. Sir
Edward Russell has been spinning a yarn about
her “ affectionate ” appeal to Lord Rosebery when
that sporting capitalist was about to resign the
Premiership. She gazed at him earnestly, with a
royal wink in her other eye, and said, “Promise
me not to turn Conservative.” It is a- cause of
much sorrow to V.R. that her influential dukes
and earls are mostly inclined to Toryism. They
ought to fight like demons for the improvement of
the masses, and the removal of class distinctions —
but they don’t. And the Queen, being a sturdy
Democrat herself, is naturally grieved at this.

A Richmond Lady
delates her Remarkable Recovery.
The Case of
Mrs. Sarah Dover.
( From the Windsor “ Australian .”)
BY OUIR SPECIAL REPORTER.
A representative of this paper in the
course of his duty took a wnlk in the
direction of Francis Street, Richmond,
N.S. W., and knocked at the door of Mrs.
Dover. It was opened by a very pleasant-
faced lady.
“Do I speak to Mrs. Dover?” queried
the newspaper man.
“ You do,” she answered, with a smile.
“Then will you permit me to make a
report of your case ? ”
“ With pleasure,” and after asking our
reporter to come in and take a seat she
immediately dived into a relation of the
facts of her illness.
“I can assign no reason for it,” she com-
menced, “ but I gradually
Began to Fall Away.
Day by day I grew thinner and paler. I
was told I was suffering from anaemia, or a
want of red bio. d corpuscles in my veins.
A doctor attended me, and prescribed ior
me. I took his medicine for a long time.
His physic, however, was not able to relieve
me, and I had to give it up. As I got
worse the most frightful headaches b gan
to affect me. The pain was like an iron
band tightening round my head. Indiges-
tion came on. The fact was, I was too
weak to digest my food. The digestive
fluids in my stomach were too deficient to
break it up and dissolve it, and all I ate lay
like a heavy substance within me. Instead
of digesting it would ferment, causing wind,
swelling, and acute pain. When my meals
were placed before me I could only eat a
few mouthfuls, and then felt quite sick.
That bitter taste was always in my mouth,
my eyes became deep and sunken, dark
rings encircled them, and I began to wear
the haggard appearance of
Extreme Suffering.
My lips were a palish blue, my cheeks
pallid and sunken, and I looked years older
than I do now.”
“Did you suffer from anything else?”
asked the reporter.
“ I did, I am-sorry to say. You haven’t
heard the half of my troubles yet ? Sleep
I could not. All night I lay wide awake,
tortured with
Frightful Nervous Headaches
and yet not able to get a moment’s rest.
Night after night I was abandoned by
sleep. In the day-time I w’as listless and
indifferent to all that was going on around
me. This feeling of lassitude was so heavy
upon me that it took me some minutes
to make up my mind to even rise from a
seat. I was too tired to read, and that I
consider
A Very Bad Sign.”
“You couldn’t go on like this for long,”
said the newspaper man ; “you couldn’t
live without sleep, you know.”
“ No. I had to get sleep at any price.
I was forced to have
Recourse to Morphia
to obtain an hour or two’s respite from my
intense headaches. Of course I needed an
increased dose every time that I wished to
go to sleep. But my general health' con-
tinued to get worse, and 1 was now troubled
with the reaction of the sleeping draughts
which I had been taking. This added to
my weakness. At last it was only with the
greatest difficulty that I could walk, or
rather hobble, across the room. My nerves
began to give way. I was very timorous
and fearful. The least thing would startle
me. I was always turning my head round
expecting to see something terrible behind
me, and often enough I should be seized
with a fancy quite realistic, and which X
could not drive away, that a hand was just
about to be placed on my shoulder; and
often I would fall into
A Fit of Trembling
over nothing at aIL I had gloomy fore-
bodings as to the future. The blessing of good
health seemed so far away from me that I
never expected to attain it again. I got
weaker and weaker. I thought my vital
spark was going out. I was laid in bed, not
being strong enough to sit up. The doctors
could do nothing for me, except to continue
to'dose me with morphia.”
“I wonder you were able to support life
so long under these circumstances,” sug-
gested the reporter.
“I have a good constitution,” replied
Mrs. Dover, “otherwise I should have gone
out
Like An Exhausted Lamp,
“but I was nearly dead. It’s almost*
miracle I ever got off my bed again.”
“And how did you manage it?”
“ In this way : I was listlessly letting my
eyes wander over a newspaper, and I came
across an account of a case where a lady had
dwindled into a mere skeleton through
Impoverishment of the Blood,
but who had ultimately been restored to
perfect health through the use of Clements
Tonic. Here’s a possible chance for me, I
said to myself. God knows but that remedy
might contain that . something which is
lacking in my blood. I consulted a friend,;
and the result was that I commenced *
regular course of Clements Tonic.”
“ Did you get an immediate effect?”
“ Could I expect that in a case like mine ?
But in a few days I found I could sleep
without the morphia, and I could tell by
that, and by the fact that I never trembled
now, that my nerves were being streng-
thened. Then, as my colour improved, I
knew Clements Tonic was beginning to put;
Vitality Into My Blood,
for colour began to show in my skin. I did!
away with morphia altogether now—it was
no longer necessary. Although still very
weak i could soon digest light food, and
began to take quite an interest in what I
was to have for dinner. I took no alcoholic
stimulant whatever. I found it quite un-
necessary. The stimulating properties of
Clements Tonic were quite sufficient. My
headache-) altogether disappeared, and at
last there was nothing more for Clements
Tonic to do than to put more
Flesh On My Bones.
My nerves were —: true as steel.”
“Then I am to presume that Clements
Tonic completed your restoration to health?”
“ Most decidedly; I soon became as
robust as you see me now, and in perfect
health in every way. This is the result of
Clements Tonic, and, believe me, I am not
ungrateful.”
“ Then I can put this in the Windsor
Australian”
“ I shall have positive pleasure in seeing
it there, for humanity’s sake.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I Sarah Dovkr, of Francis-street, Richmond, in
the colony of New South Wales, do solemnly and
sincerely declare that I have carefully read
annexed document, consisting of ten folios, and con-
secutively numbered from 1 to 10, and that it contains
and is a true and faithful account of my illness and
cure by Clements Tonic, and also contains my full
permission to publish .the same in any way: and I make
this solemn declaration conscientiously believing the
same to be true and by virtue of the provisions of .an
Act made and passed in the ninth year of the reign
of her present Majesty, intituled “An Act for the
more effectual abolition of Oaths and Affirmations
taken and made in the various Departments of the
Government of New South Wales, and to substitute
Declarations in lieu thereof,• and for the suppression
of voluntary and extra-judicial Oaths and Affidavits.

weekly, issued by J. G. Drake in the interests of
honest polities. The Australian Nation, a new
Sydney paper, is a curiosity ; for in eight good-

The candor of Dubberke is evidently catch-
ing. In a recent Melb. divorce-case petitioner
stated that —
Her husband never provided a home, and they had
only cohabited in the public parks. During the whole of

husband.
Premier Bigjohn Forrest, of Westralia,
says that he isn’t sure his province will join the
Commonwealth after all. But it will join right
enough. The proviso that the Commonwealth
may lend financial assistance to embarrassed
States will fetch it if nothing else does. Westralia
is going to be an embarrassed State very shortly.
Ugly rumors current as to the manner in
which a successor to Mr. Waldron, in the City-
Solicitorship of Sydney, was elected, go to show
that the present mode of choosing officers stands
much in need of improvement. Aid. Norton, at
one stage of the proceedings, demanded that voting
should be open ; this suggestion was laughed at.
All sorts of influence were brought to bear in
favor of various candidates ; sectarian prejudices
were industriously traded upon, and, in some
cases, even ad misericordiam appeals were made
by influential outsiders on behalf of candidates for
the important office. This sort of thing is en-
couraged by the fact that no ratepayer can find
out how his representative has voted. Once place
the aldermen in such position that they must
defend their actions before their constituents, and
the gross methods of canvassing indulged in by
candidates for municipal offices will cease. At
present, there is too great a tendency on the part
of aldermen to imagine that the high offices of
the city are simply gifts, which they may bestow
on their friends at pleasure, and with a total dis-
regard for the interests of the ratepayers. The
position was finally given to Mr. Dawson, partner
of the deceased holder of the office.

N.S.W. Bowling Association’s souvenir of
the Victorian tour of 1898, written by President
John Young and others, makes a sociable pamph-
let of 48 pp. ; and between the flowing bowl and
the rolling ditto it is evident that Vic. hospitality
gave the N.S. Welshmen a jolly good time. The
visitors were treated like princes wherever they
went; and if the whole Australian population
doesn’t turn bowler it isn’t the fault of this
festive pamphlet.
Westi’alia is even in a worse way for news
than she is for money, to judge by the following
specimen, clipped from the West Australian.
The proprietor and editor of the sheet in which
this appears is Hackett, M.A., LL.B. :
Ceremony of “ Beris.”— ln accordance with the custom
in the Jewish Church, the infant son of Mr. E. S. Lazarus,
of “ Romola,” Havelock-street, West Perth, underwent
the operation known as “ beris,” or circumcision, yester-
day afternoon, at the residence of his parents. The cere-
mony was performed by Mr. B. Lenzer, in the presence of
a large number of the friends of the parents of the child.
Everything passed of! successfully, and Mr. Lenzer was
warmly congratulated upon the skilful manner in which
he had carried out his work. At the close of the cere-
mony the guests assembled for the purpose of partaking
of light refreshments provided by Mrs. Lazarus, and the
Rev. D. I. Freedman, 8.A., presided. A number of
toasts were drunk, and speeches of a congratulatory
character were delivered. The chairman proposed health
and prosperity to the infant, and the sentiment was re-
ceived with heartiness and responded to by Mr. E. S.
Lazarus. “The Land we Live In ” was proposed by Mr.
J. Lazarus, who spoke in warm and hopeful terms of the
future of the colony.
There are few things that really relieve
the uncomfortable sensation generally described
as “tickling” in the throat. “Sen Sen,” how-
ever, is claimed to be one of the exceptions. It is
an American remedy, made up in cachou form,
and gives relief to throat-irritation. The cachous
are rich in flavor, and exquisite and lasting in per-
fume, and the manufacturers declare that not
only is nothing injurious used in their composition,
but that, on the contrary, they are beneficial to
health. Singers and speakers should never be
without this simple but invaluable remedy, useful
to everybody. The cachous appear to be very
much stronger than most pastilles, and certainly a
threepenny packet contains an unusually great
number at the price. They can be obtained at all
chemists.*

• • • •
Major Wardill, manager of the Australian
Eleven to visit England, appears to labor under a mis-
apprehension in the matter of the management of the
match which it was proposed to play on Sydney Associa-
tion Cricket Ground. The contention of N.S.VV. Cricket-
ing Association, that the match must be played under the
Association’s direction and management, is merely in
keeping with the terms of the deed of grant; the Asso-
ciation disclaims any desire to vexatiously meddle with
the working of the team. The action of the Association,
it is averred, is the only legal course pursuable. The
Vic. and S.A. members of the Eleven evidently do not
understand the position taken up by the Association, or
they perhaps would not have been in such haste to
endorse their manager’s action. The N.S.VV. contingent
does not agree with the Major’s decision ; and writer is of
opinion that the match will be played as arranged, despite
all the fuss that has been made over it. If it should not
come off, the Australian Eleven will be minus a big
lump of “stuff” to start it on its way rejoicing, for
Sydney can ever knock spots off the other Australian
centres in the way of cricket “ gate.”

The mail-boat which takes the Australian team
to England at the end of March is already overbooked
with passengers. In all the cities one reads that Mr.
Soandso and Mr. Somebodyelse intend to accompany the
team. It generally happens that Mr. Soandso is a jolly
good fellow with ample funds and a Bohemian thirst, and
we may therefore conclude that gold-top will be plentiful
this trip. For which reason The Bulletin hereby unani-
mously plumps for Bill Howell as first and second bowler,
manager, captain and committee of the team. William’s
only beverage is water, and no matter what great deeds
he has done he never caps the performance by setting his
inside on fire or waking up in the morning with his legs
wrapped round his head. William should also do all the
public speaking. He is no orator ; but one of the advan-
tages of cold water is that a man’s words don’t get
caught in his teeth as they are coming out.

Ed. Bulletin.— Your par. re the great proba-
bility of. the Australian team consisting of only 13 playing
members again emphasises the spinelessness of the Aus.

in the late interprovincial, while Gregory’s strong
“ Howellistic” aversions put him out of the running. It
seems narrowed down either to Kelly or the idol of Melb.

* • * •
V. Trumper’s fine innings of 260, nob out,
against Waverley, last Saturday, must again bring the
young player’s name under notice as an “ extra" for the
English tour. Trumper's figures for the ’97-’9S season in
Sydney were 8 innings of 12,123, 125, 85, 120 not out, 191
not out, 133 and 162 not out. Trumper established
records in senior club cricket for greatest number of
centuries in a season, greatest aggregate, greatest aver-
age, and most oonsecutive centuries. That he should
have failed this season in the interprovincial matches
came as a great disappointment to followers of cricket in
Sydney. Bad luck and nervousness combined with a
laudable-desire to “go for” the bowling have made a
failure of Trumper in big cricket; but he is only 21, and
when he does get started he will make a mess of bowlers’
averages. The only fear is that his bad fortune will make
him over-careful and put an end to his beautiful free
oricket.

of his second Australian team. He is four years younger
than Noble, who is 26. Remarkable that there are hardly
any veterans in Australian cricket now, unless Worrall,
verging on 36, comes within the category.

Sturt, in Adelaide, on Feb. 4. Two other juniors, who
have been long fancied for bigger matches, got going
same day. Claxton, a colt under 20 years, made 185 (not
out), and E. Leak made 203. S.A. still looks anxiously
for the first-class bowler she needs so much.

Two steeplechases at V.R.C. autumn meeting,
with 300 and 400 sovs. added to their respective stakes,
have only 11 and 10 entries. Of the 14 different horses
nominated 8 are in both events. Victoria has before
never been so badly off for ’chasers.

Bobadil, stayer or no-stayer, ought to take the
V.R.C. Leger. Barring Lee Metford, who hasn’t run
sturdily thus far, the dandy little Derby failure has
nothing to beat. True, there’s George Frederick, but
G.F. is stated by the daily newspaper-puffers to be en-
gaged in developing himself into a “ splendid 4-year-old,”
and next season hasn’t arrived as yet.

* * * *
Symmetry has been duly struok out of the Aus-
tralian Cup, and left to win Newmarket Handicap. On
her Spring form the daughter of Trenton must have a big
chance for the six-furlongs flutter, and if W. R. Wilson’s
stable can produce a faster one at the weights it will
probably start first favorite.
A nag that will take a big race ere long is the
big black Sunrise horse, Eastern King. His dashing finish
up the straight in Warwick Farm Handicap, which he
won with 9st. up, on Saturday, was reminiscent of old
Wallace, and fairly worked-up the spectators. An Indian
buyer lately offered a big price for Eastern King, but was
told that he wasn’t for sale at any price.
* • • •
Hon. “ Bill ” Long’s horses haven’t yet lost
the bad habit of putting up several very contradictory
performances within a few days. At Warwick Farm, on
Saturday, Gentle Hermit, who started first favorite in a
6-furlong race at Rosehill the previous Saturday, and
finished among the last three, ran away with a sinii-
lar race ; whilst Hopscotch, who beat a particularly good
field at Rosehill, couldn’t get a place amongst a second-
rate lot at Warwick Farm.
•* • • •
In Sydney, there is always money awaiting the
man who brings along a good hurdle or steeplechase
horse. The aged Gozo gelding, Sadur, is anything but a
champion hurdler, but he can give away a couple of cwt.
to the old “ Robbos ” that comprise the rank-and-file of
Sydney hurdle horses. The trainer who brought him
from Queensland has won two races within a few weeks.
Sydney chasers are, if possible, worse than the hurdlers,
and, time after time, level-headed owners have come
along with a moderate horse and landed a handsome
stake.
• * • *
Saturday horse-racing about Sydney is now at
its lowest ebb, and the only chance for a recovery would
seem to be the totalisator. A good proof as to how
matters stand is the fact that the better class Of “ books”
prefer to look on, trying to pick up a bit punting or acting
as “commissioners," except at the biggest meetings ; and
the bulk of the betting is now done by layers who
flourished at Lillie Bridge in the days when £2O in-
vested on a horse at Randwick would not bring him down
to a short price. The action of the A.J.C. in vetoing
pony-racing and all its connections will not tend to im-
prove the game as played around Sydney on a Saturday
afternoon, as layers begin to find there is no money in
one day’s racing a week on a course where books are thick
as plums in a pudding, and “dollar” bets predominate.
At least two of the bigger books last week threw in their
lot with the ponies and three meetings a week.
* • * •
Not long ago, S.M. Herald killed John Cleland,
owner of Shenandoah and Wollomai, and erst host
of the old Albion, Melbourne. Those who may have read
the “melancholy news” may drink to the health and
long life of deceased, whom writer saw jolly and fat,
walking with two other ancients in Bourke-st., this day.
* * * *
Hard Times won Cootamundra Cup in record
time for local course—2.24. A horse with such a name,
and drawn by four Chinamen in “ Tatt.’s ’’ sweep, could
hardly lose on such a drought-stricken course. Notwith-
standing the starved state of the district, over 2000 rolled
up for Cup-day, which only goes to show what the local
Byron Moore, secretary Sandiman, has done for racing at
Coota. The Hurdles, on second day, was worth going
acrossN.S.W. to see. The two 6-to-4 favorites fought it
out over the last two flights under the whip, and Austin
won by a bare half-length, thanks to the horsemanship of
his.owner, Mr. Jack Cox. Out of eight rides, including
two hurdle-races, Mr. Cox has now scored seven wins on
Austin. He comes of riding blood, and, as he is only 21,
weighs 8.7, and has pluck, head and hands, he bids fair to
become the Jack Brewer of N.S.VV.

• • • •
Another attempt to work up interest in cycling
in Sydney will be made on Saturday night, 25th Feb.,
when the electric-light carnival will begin. The only
way to draw a really paying crowd would be to allow the
bookmaker to ply his vocation openly on the ground. In
Melb., where IkeyMo bawls unceasingly at cycling meets,
with his hand over his nose to soften down his features,
30,000 people come to back their fancy, and to watch
Walne, Jackson, and the rest. In Sydney, the racing can
be brilliant, but the public won’t come. The fact is that
watching cycling in Sydney is a horribly boresome under-
taking, consisting mostly of sitting on a hard seat and
seeing the same old stagers win or lose, according to
“ present form." If the bookmaker were openly allowed,
an element of excitement, however rotten, would be in-
troduced into the slow, beastly business. Possibly it
would be more expedient to let the business die—or per-
haps it would be better simply to let it be buried, for
it smells so loudly that there can be little life left in it.

* * • *
Strong man Samson is whooping things up a
bit in Victoria. At Allandale he encountered the wrath
of residents by charging very high prices for admission to
his show and the good people of that ordinarily-peaceable
town kicked in the most effective manner possible, viz,
by assembling in front of the house and seeing that no-
body paid—until some enterprising speculator happened
along, purchased the possibilities and reduced the tariff,
when the hall soon filled. There was more trouble after-
wards—everybody wanted his or her money back (the
performance fell short of local expectations); but the
mighty man of muscle buttoned up and folding his huge
arms across his chest moved further afield. Later, a
printer in a neighboring centre had the chain-breaker
before the local beaks for assault; and, latest of all, word
comes from Bendigo that Samson indulged in the little
diversion of “ a fracas ” with the police, concerning
which cross-summonses were pending.
• • • *
The mystery of strongman Samson’s chain-
breaking business has allegedly been solved, and all the
theories hitherto advanced are exploded. “It is the
simplest thing vou ever saw,” said Lucifer to present
writer, the other day, “and but for help from a well-
posted source I would never have discovered it.” If
Samson stays in Australia much longer, he will see the
boys on the street snapping chains as if they were threads.
Lucifer intends showing it through M.L. shortly, and is
not averse to satisfying the curiosity of a friend or two
by giving a private exhibition occasionally. But how is
the coin-breaking done ?
A private match was sailed in Sydney Harbor
on Saturday between the champion 22-footer Effie and
the 2J rater Bronzewing. In a fresh south-easter the
Effie flew away from the Bronzewing, and won as she
liked.
* • * *
Mr. Sam Horden has evidently given up hope,
for the present, of owning the champion 22-footer ; he
has parted with the one he imported from England, and
also with the latest Joe Donnelly, the Plover. Donnelly
seems to have overdone the “ big ” business in building
the Plover, which, save on a very light day, can have no
hope of beating her rivals Effie and Keriki.
• * • •
Swimmer Lane not only easily got away with
the quarter-mile championship at Eastern Suburbs Club’s
gala last Saturday, but put up new record figures (sm.
64 l-58ec.) for Australasia ; the previous best (6m. OJsee.)
was Percy Cavill’s. Lane’s record is within 7sec. of
Tyers’ world’s record. Save that at 100yds., which be-
longs to “ Mud ” Bishop, all the season’s premiership
titles are now in Lane’s possession. He will probably
have a cut at the 100 yards “best” shortly, and, later,
may go for the world’s 440yds., to which, if well-paced,
he will give a great shaking.
* * * *
Dan Creedon, Australia’s one-time brilliant
middle-weight champion pug., must now be written a
back number. From 'Frisco comes word that on Dec. 30
Green (“ Young Corbett”) easily out-pointed our stoutly-
built bruiser in a 20-round match. Dan purposed coming
home to see the old folks if he won that battle, and it
was reckoned a good thing for him, but he will have to
bide a wee now. Green was a 10-to-8-on favorite until
the afternoon of the fight when the Australians in Cali-
fornia and the turfites of that State came along and un-
loaded their sacks to such purpose that offers of 6 to 4 on
Creedon found no takers. Dan tried hard to mix matters,
but Green did not want it that way—he simply' contented
himself with keeping his short-armed adversary off and
peppering as opportunity occurred, which was pretty
often—for Creedon is a very open fighter.
* * * *
Obit., Tom Waldron, Melb. senior-constable,
sworn by the Vic. police-force to be the most powerful
“ copper ” on earth. Waldron was red-haired, small for
a Melb. bobby, and so broad that he looked still shorter.
But he was an utter take-down. His build did not seem
different from that of the man of mediocre strength, and
his manner was so “ soft ” that many a larrikin push took
him on from that very cause, and carry the marks of
their grievous error to this day. Waldron once carried
half a ton a distance of 20ft. -The Bulletin, by-the-way,
has seen a storeman carry a bale of 100 101 b. Dundee
woolpacks, with hoop-iron binding, 70ft. —and in the
presence of a score of people, and he did things with the
heavy stone that Donald Dinnie had to let go. Waldron’s
greatest exploit was at Diamond Creek, where he was
sent 10 years ago to subdue the hilarity of the navvies.
Walking into a bar where a wild orgie was starting, he
commanded order, and when the assembled Cousin
Jacks recovered from their first shock at the audacity
of a solitary policeman talking to them so,, a
six - foot spokesman told Waldron, with many
blankies, that they'd see him dead first. The
bold cop. just laid aside his helmet and jumper, and
the spokesman spun along the dust like a ball till he
brought up with a bang against the three-rail fence, on
the other side of the three-chain road. For the following
minute or two the scene resembled children playing
“ Charley over the water,” for the door of the pub. con-
tinued to vomit big navvies, until at last Waldron was
seen leisurely assuming his official togs. Some of the stories
of the man’s strength are just a trifle toostrong, but even
veritable Herculeses of the force—no mean talkers of
their own achievements—are heard admitting that he
“ could pull two of us to pieces like a loaf of bread.”
The Afghan Cases—An Appeal.
Deab Bulletin, —The telegraphed reports in the
metropolitan papers of the proceedings instituted
by the Bourke Carrying Co. against certain of its
wretched Asiatio employes have made the public
painfully familiar with the outrageous character
of the agreement by virtue of which the com-
pany is successfully invoking the coercive inter-
vention of the law. That the agreement is
barbarous and inhuman in its provisions, that
it is at once a disgrace to those who con-
ceived it and a reproach to the country wherein
its enforcement is possible —is but too true.
But this does not help matters very much.
Like Shylock, the co. points to its bond and
demands its many pounds of flesh. To bring the
law into, at least, approximate conformity with
natural justice and public sentiment is the work of
the Legislature. But, before Parliament can be
asked to intervene it is necessary to exhaust
the rights which, theoretically at least, the
law secures to all. With this end in view
the people of Bourke have started a fighting-
fund to enable the inarticulate and penniless
slaves of a soulless company to test the validity of
the agreement by an appeal to the higher courts.
Unfortunately Bourke is suffering from the im-
poverishing effects of many disastrous seasons, and
is likely to find the burden too heavy if required
to carry it alone. Many of those, too, in that dis-
trict who have the means are much too sympa-
thetically inclined towards the company to per-
mit of any hope that they will figure on
the list of contributors. To properly prepare
and present an appeal, at least .£l5O will be re-
quired, as interpreters have to be found and other
expenses incurred, absent in the majority of cases.
Will you throw your influence behind the efforts
being made to raise that sum ? In £l5O there are
3000 shillings. There are, I am sure, 3000 people
who will readily contribute a shilling apiece for
such a purpose if only an appeal is made to them.
The stake at issue is not merely the vindication of
all that is humane and just in the case of the par-
ticular men now in the clutches of the company ;
it carries with it the equally important conse-
quence that if this agreement is maintained it can
be imitated, while its abolition will stand as a
warning that not by such means shall the lust for
gain seek its gratification. I ask you to launch
this necessary appeal.—Yours, &c.,
E. D. MILLEN.
14 O’Connell-st., Sydney.
[The facts of the case upon which Mr. Millen grounds
this appeal are set forth upon page 7 of this issue of The
Bulletin, under the title “ Our Great Free Country.”
Slavery having theoretically been abolished under the
British flag, the law knows no difference between black
men and white ; therefore (putting the thing on its very
lowest grounds, as a mere matter of self-protection), if
dark men can still be legally sold into slavery, as these
unhappy Afghans seem to have been sold, ignorant white
men from abroad, or necessitous white men on the spot,
can also be sold into slavery in similar fashion. The
Bulletin starts with £lO the fund to test the legal powers
of the Camel C rrying Co., and asks the Australian public
to subscribe the balance of, say, 2800 shillings.]


Life—Twenty years’ Faith, twenty of
Hope, twenty of Uncharitableness.
About the last feeble kick of the Gatton
tragedy yarn in the daily papers :
The circumstances for a time seemed to favor the guilty
parties ; but the police now have linked together a chain
of evidence which is considered of great value. It ap-
pears that certain traces were left behind at the scene of
the t ragedy which possibly may prove a strong corrobora-
tion of what the police have already in hand. The autho-
rities prefer to keep from publicity all particulars of these
traces ; but they consider they will likely make conclusive
testimony of what otherwise might be doubtful either
through prejudice or error. Besides those traces actually
on the scene of the tragedy, a discovery has also been
made in the paddock where the murder occurred, which
it is considered, will prove of great value. The nature of
the discovery has not transpired. This has considerably
modi tied the necessity for further local search by them.
Again, outside the paddock, under the culvert, another
article has been found, to which the police attach con-
siderable importance, tracing its ownership proving a
hard piece of work, yet the police do not despair. Ulti-
mate success is also possible in the investigations of the
Government Analyst, who will materially assist the
police.
That was practically the last that was heard on
the subject, so presumably the traces that were
loft in the paddock, and the discovery that was
made there, when added to the circumstance that
was found in the culvert, didn’t corroborate what
the police had in hand after all, and what might
otherwise have been doubtful through prejudice or
error is still doubtful.

Missing Friends.
ENJAMIN MAYES, who left England 12
years ago, and last heard of in Georgetown,
N. Queensland, about 8 years since, write to W.
G. Payne, Kalgoorlie, Westralia.
He smartness of Queensland’s police force.
Lately a constable had arrested a lad for horse-
stealing, and was taking him to Toowoomba.
When near the police station, lad suddenly
pointed out a horse hanging up in front of a
shop as the animal he had stolen, and asked
the constable to see what the brands were.
Policeman innocently went round to the opposite
side of the nag to examine it. When he drifted
round to this side of the horse again he was quite
surprised to find that his prisoner was gone.
Prisoner is still absent, and all the efforts of the
force to diminish his goneness have been unavailing.

The opening of the New Season has brought with it some important changes in last year's models, and especially so in the
u Austral Swift f a light machine which may be called a Road Racer, or Light Roadster, according to fancy. The history of
the 11 Austral Swift” is shortly this: —
Since the commencement of what we may call the “Swift Boom,” the Australian public have consistently shown some unwillingness to accept the
recognised English models without some modification, and the modifications called for have almost invariably tended in one direction. A careful study of
the question led us to the conclusion that it would be more satisfactory to insti-uct the makers in Coventry what alterations were necessary to meet Australian
views, and to ship direct the perfected machine which we called the “ Austral Swift.”
Before going into detail, it may be as well to say that the chief feature aimed at in the “ Austral Swift ”is STYLE. This we insisted upon, as we have
found it waste of time to lay any model before the Australian public that did not at first glance claim to be STYLISH. The fact that the “ Austral Swift ”
is the most stylish machine ever seen in Australia proves to us that the Swift Coventry Company have carried out the instructions we gave them both in
letter and spirit, and the first consignment we are now unpacking satisfies us that the whole of the order is to be up to sample. Among the leading
features of the new machine we may mention the following :
A SHORT HEAD, assuring rigidity and strength.
LARGE HUBS for smoother running.
PERFECT SIMPLICITY in pedal attachments, the pedal pins being screwed into the cranks with left and right threads.
BIG GEAR WHEELS for easier propulsion.
BROOK’S SADDLES as per general desire.
PERFECT FINISH in every part of the machine.
Another feature—and an important one, too—is the price, and here again we have endeavored to meet the wishes of our Patrons,
the “ AUSTRAL SWIFT ”is £2O. Terms : £2l for six months’, and £22 for twelve months’, extension.

Bayswater, Sydney, October, 1898.
W. H. Malyon, Esq., Man. Chemist, Ballarat.
Dear Sir,—For years past (about ten), I have been
troubled with “ Acne ” in its worst form, and have sought
advice from the best Australian and London doctors, with
only fair results. A Victorian friend of mine, some four
or five weeks ago, sent me a tin of your “ Ligmolliene,”
and speaking very highly of its wonderful curative powers.
I naturally took same “ Cum Grano Salis,” and was in-
clined not to use it ; eventually I tried your ointment
with truly marvellous results, and am now entirely cured ;
and think people who suffer from skin diseases are greatly
indebted to you for your wonderful remedy. I shall
advise all my friends with skin troubles to use same.
Wishing “ Ligomolliene ” all the success it richly deserves,
Yours faithfully, GEORGE J. O'REGAN.

Strange Action of a Respectable
Community.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When the Daltons came to Woodside, about 10
years ago, the whole country-side came along in
its pony-carriage and leather-lined waggonette to
do homage to Mrs. Dalton —by far the prettiest
woman in the district. And right up till last
year it continued to worship; then something
happened.
One day tire new traveller for the Maoriland
Stock and Station Agency Co. rode up to the front
door, and was shown into the drawing-room,
where Mrs. Dalton rose to receive him and — i
stepped back with a blush and a frown. The
visitor seemed surprised too, and Dalton looked
on in blank astonishment.
Mrs. Dalton left the room.
Mr. Dalton got up, and, taking his pipe from
his pocket, looked at the amber mouth-piece for a
moment, and then remarked quietly : “I am very
sorry, but I shall have to ask you to go on to the
hotel for dinner. I don’t know why, but it is
painfully apparent that my wife declines to re-
ceive you, and, under such circumstances, of
course I won’t press you to stay.”
Mr. Dalton smiled pleasantly as he said it, and
looked at the man opposite with an expression
that said plainly: “You’d doit yourself, wouldn’t
you ? ”
The stranger drew himself up, and put his hands
into his pockets, “It’s a polite kick-out,” he said,
“ but you are quite right—if you think the lady
worth it!”
“What?” said Dalton, hotly.
“Oh, you do, eh ! Glad of it. Hope you won’t
be disillusioned as I was. She was my wife, you
know, long before she was yours. Inffacert —er —
I’m afraid you are either offenders against the
law or else you are living in a state of sin.”
* * .* »
Legally the blackmailer was powerless, but
socially he was omnipotent, and, to save the feel-
ings of the woman he loved, Dalton allowed him-
self to be bled white.
One day Mrs. Dalton found it out. She con
sidered awhile; then she went to Dalton, and-
putting her arms round his neok, told him quietly
that if he paid another penny she would leave
hiii!—go right away ! She couldn’t live with him
if she was to he the cause of his ruin.
“ But it’s only financial ruin,” urged Dalton.
“In this age and at our time of life that is
everythin </, dear,” she replied.
“But, if I refuse ho will blare the whole busi-
ness abroad, and close every door against us.”
“ Will that make you very unhappy, dear ?”
“Me? Good heavens, no! I’m thinking of
you. I don’t care if I never speak to one of them
again !”
She laughed; a silvery ripple of glee that cleared
a cloud from his spirit.
“Let them all go! We have a lovely home,
and we shall find happiness in our companion-
ships the pursuit of knowledge—study of beauty
in books, flowers, music,-and landscape. Let them
go, dear. ”
, Dalton shut down on the payments, and the
stoppage was promptly followed by the commence-
ment of a suit for divorce.
; The divorce was granted; it was not even
opposed, and as the “injured” husband left the
court he chuckled to a companion that it would
just about smash up the lady’s social sovereignty.
As soon as the decree was made absolute Dalton
married the lady on earth, as he had long pre-
viously married her in Heaven.
And then the community of Woodside did a
strange thing. After having paid social homage
to Mrs. Dalton during all those years she had
lived in “sin ” it ostracised her, now that she was
a “ respectable married woman.”

Random Notes at the Circus.
ELBOURNE people
who remember the
Demon and Crocodile
contortion act of the
Eclair Fr6res at the
Exhibition Building,
some four years ago,
will recognise an old
friend in Mens.
A.jax, the latest ad-
dition to Fitzgerald
Bros.’ Circus. Ajax,
late Eclair, is practi-
cally the famous
Demon and Crocodile
rolled into one. He
does all that he used to do himself, plus every-
thing his former partner did, and the total
effect of him, as he sits scratching his
spinal cord with his funny-bone, is truly
wonderful. German by birth, Ajax is a
citizen of the world. The Fitzes imported him
F. M. Jones' leap over 25 guns.
from Italy in the first instance. He left them to
go to Russia, or somewhere, and was last year re-
imported by Rickards—from London. Now he
has come back to the Fitzgeralds for 12 months,
and his young Australian wife will travel with
him. And she being the only visible rib apper-
taining to his boneless body, Ajax can almost con-
sider himself a native.
• • * •
Tom and Dan Fitzgerald blush somewhat when
chaffed about the inclu-
sion of foreign artists in
their onetime Young Aus-
tralian Circus, but they
explain that, thanks to
prosperity, the show out-
grew it s Australian
strength and compelled
them to summon aid
from abroad. The Circus
paid its first visit to
Melbourne in Glenloth’s
year (the Fitzes name
eaoh year after its Cup-
winner), and then the
co. consisted entirely
of native-born material.
What a first visit!
Starting at a pitch in
Swanston-street, next to the gaol, and subse-
quently travelling round the suburbs, the tent
stayed within the Melbourne district for seven
months. All the natives who principally assisted
in that success are still with the Fitzgeralds. The
Circus remains Australian at heart, and, indeed,
some of the most foreign-named artists on the pro-
gramme to-day are daughters of the soil.
La Belle Rose on the roll-
ing globe is Mrs. Tom
Fitzgerald—of this coun-
try. Ca va sans dire.
The scribe lapses into
French merely out of re-
spect for the lady’s nom
de Cirque. Mrs. Tom Fitz.
brings her native wit to
bear on the matter of
Joe Morris.
F. M. Jones.
spectacular fixings,
and is accountable
for much sweetness
and light and trim-
ness in the general
turnout of the co.
Mile. Sarina, the
trapeze artist, who
concludes her turn
with a pretty imita-
tion of Madame An-
tonio’s “one woman
ene rope” performance, is an Australian in
spite of her alias. Her husband, Captain
Jack Sutton, the “ Wild Wester,” is
attached to the Circus, and would
loom large on the bill if there were
any Red Indians about. Godayou, the
Japanese juggler, a most amiable
little gentleman, joined the Fitzes’
organisation in Newhaven’s year,
accompanied by a small son.
Godayou is said to have Pooh*
Bahian blood in his veins, two
charming daughters being quoted as cor-
roborative eviaenoe. One of them is the wife
of the Japanese Consul in Sydney, and her un-
married sister lives with her. Now, the alleged
nobility of Lycurgus, the plump
little Japaneseboy, is more open
to doubt, although he wears his
smart clothes with any amount
of swaggah. Lycurgus got left
behind in Australia together
Japanese Boy.
Lycurgus.
Cora Trevor.
The smallest per-
former.
Mr. Dan Fitzgerald.
with two monkeys, when Abel and Clair’s circus
sailed for India. The infant derelict slept with
the monkeys. Mrs. Tom Fitz took pity on the
toddler, wherefore her husband bought him from
the monkeys’ caretaker for .£2O cash. Circus life
is full of interesting items. You buy an elephant
one day and a Javanese princeling the next.
• • • •
Talking of elephants, Fitzgeralds’
Menagerie, which started with the
purchase of a lioness and cubs from
Sydney (or was it Adelaide?) Zoo,
promises to grow into a great specu-
lation. Herr Zaph, who came from
Hamburg a year or two ago to run the
lion and elephant and the tiger and
horse, is likely to stay with the show
as animal trainer-in-chief, and what
Zaph doesn’t know about caged beasts
of prey is not worth knowing. The
Menagerie ought to develope into a
school for performing animals. There
is no reason why the furry Bunyip of
Fitzgeraldian fiction shouldn’t
be trained by Zaph to put its
head into the lion’s yawn-
ing mouth, for the Bunyip
is a simple and conGding
novelty anyhow.
Herr Zaph and Hemic.
Which reminds i the scribe that
everybody seems ’to suspect the
fenuineness of the Yawning
lorse performance. Sevtm or
eight bronchos (true-blue Austra-
lian breed) are worked in the
ring by Dan Fitzgerald. One of
them wears a muzzle. Dan re-
moves the muzzle and tells the
horse to yawn, and he yawns like
a Lord Carrington till further
orders. Audience naturally as-
sumes that the removal of the
muzzle is the secret of the trick,
whereas the educated animal will
stretch his mouth whenever he is
told, in the stable as in the circus.
The muzzle is put on because this
particular steed is a stallion, and
the other bronchos are mares
mostly, therefor hee would affec-
tionately nip ’em with his huge
yellow teeth if allowed to take the liberty.
Educated horses are very human. Also, there
is a popular delusion re Commodore and Oscar,
the Russian pony that acts with elephant Lizzie.
Casual observers take it for granted that Oscar is
Commodore, but, as a matter of fact, he is much
bigger than the Fitzs.’ famous mascotte pony. It
is doubtful whether Commodore, in his 17th year,
could be persuaded to act with the elephant.
Much petting has made him so wilful that he
sometimes refuses, on principle, to work at a
day-show. Another point—all the Australian
horses and ponies in the tent are afraid of the
elephant, and one in particular—the horse that
Charlie Burshall somer-
saults upon—needs to be
carefully blindfolded when
he is taken round to the
ring, lest he should catch
sight of the ungainly mon-
ster and sink trembling to
the ground. The two-
hand English (or Flemish)
horses which George
Harmston brought out
with him are nob afraid
of the elephant, for they
have all been shipmates
together. As for Maho-
met, the Talking Horse,
he simply loathes and despises Lizzie since she
came along and put his “turn” into the shade,
and every time he sees her he expresses an earnest
desire to kick her out in one round.
• * • •
Dan Fitzgerald was a circus proprietor ere
brother Tom came into the business, and some of
the older hands were associated with him before
the present firm started. The oldest ring hands
are the Joneses—Billy and Frank M., but Bar-
Ponies Oscar and Commodore.
tello, Duckworth, and others, have been a long
time in the co. Joe Morris, the dummy clown,
joined the show at the outset, 11 years ago, bub
meantime he and his two “brothers” pub in
18 months with Rickards under the name of
the L’Estines. Joe, a capital tumbler and
“ dummy,” figures in the Steeplechase as
the man with a hunch on his chest, who
• leads his pony over the jumps. Frank M.
Jones gives a bright little sketch of an Irish-
man in the introduction to the steeplechase, and
is a conspicuously useful all-round man. He will
appear first as thehigh-vaulter—a singularly grace-
ful vaulter, with a trick of seeming to hang a-while
in mid-air; then he turns up as the fat clown,
next as a ring-master, and anon he is the clown
again. The dangerous feat of vaulting over 25
fixed bayonets whilst the gunners fire a volley is
performed by Frank M. Jones, and may the Lord
never forget to look after him when he makes his
spring ! In Paris, awhile ago, a vaulter jumped
short, and was fatally impaled upon the bayonets.
Clever Dan Feeley, of Fillis’s circus, bungled the
Bame act, and got badly wounded in one leg.
Mortification set in, and the leg had to be ampu-
tated. F.M.J. tells of his momentary terror the
first time he went over the bayonets. It hap-
pened at Mackay (Q.), and the discharge of the
guns put out all the lights in the tent. The
vaulter, feeling the effect of the firing in his eyes,
and finding himself in sudden darkness, reckoned
that he was a blind man, sure.
• • * *
Billy Jones, beloved by the Fitz. Brothers and
all of their 100 or so employes, is the Grand Old
Man of Australian circus life. As an apprentice
member of Noble and Jones’s Circus (remarkable
how many different Joneses have been eminent in
the business !), Billy came from Sydney to Mel-
bourne on the last trip of the old Shamrock
paddle-boat (Capt. Bell), in ’52 or thereabouts.
The oirous was pitohed at the place where the
Mr. W. Jones, the “ G.O.M.” of the ring.
Imperial Hotel now stands, opposite to the Old
White Hart, and boy Billy has a lively recollec-
tion of playing marbles on the site of Parliament
House. The horses were turned out to graze
among the stumps and bushes where the Exhibi-
tion is located these degenerate times.
* * * *
Billy must have been a “ star ” boy-rider, for
he mentions that, previous to the Eureka Stock-
ade, when he was performing on the road to
Buninyong, the diggers presented him a gold-
mounted whip, worth £5O. His youthful name,
“ The Nugget,” which stuck to him for years, was
hard earned, seemingly. He remembers that it
was not exactly pleasant to receive tuition from
indefatigable bosses—such as G. B. W. Lewis—
at seven o’clock on a cold morning, the
boss administering gentle encouragement with
a whip the while. Billy Jones talks in-
terestingly of all the old Circus Showmen—
Ashton, Foley, Burton —he stayed with poor
Burton till there was nothing left of his long-time
prosperous show—and Wilson, who brought Jem
Mace to Australia. And the older his reminis-
cences the more interesting they are. The early
circus “tents,” roofless and poleless—the rings
formed by driving pegs into the ground and inter-
twisting saplings to make a boundary—the seat-
ing accommodation of rough logs, and the lights,
contrived by pouring
fat into tins upon a
mud foundation, and
the trials and wor-
ries incidental to
travelling around
with those primitive
shows. One could
talk for hours with
Billy Jones on the
subject of his trea-
sured past and his
moving accidents by
flood and field.
Bringing him back
to the present for a
moment, it appears
that the old pro-
fessor thinks very
Head-dress of "Bobby."
highly of some of his pupils. Young Charlie
Burshall, just out of his apprenticeship, is not yet
a George Harmston, of course, but be promises
to be one of the best before he is muoh older.
Harry Caxdello, the ‘ ‘ mascotte ” boy of Fitz-
geralds’ Circus, and Ernie Shand, who was taken
on as an apprentice when they were appearing at
Melb. Alexandra, are riders after Billy Jones’s
own heart. And Jittle Lycurgus—well, Mr. Dan
asked Billy if he could teach the Java boy to ride
the two ponies in a week, and Billy said “Yes,”
Mr. Tom Fitzgerald and his Bunyip.
and when Mr. Dan saw how well the contract had
been carried out, he presented Billy with a “ fiver.”
• * • •

The licensing of what are called private
boarding-houses in Sydney is spoken of by local
police authorities as an urgently reform.
The amount of the license-fee might be nominal,
they they want is to be able to urge
the refusal of licenses, or of the renewals of licenses,
for causes shown.
A Melbourne man to The Bulletin on a
subiect now somewhat neglected
With a view to finding an entirely satisfactory religion,
I SStZZ&n&S. the way. and be,1,1. ol certain
i«r.<ii ut.iri lll l ist s The most beautiful point oi me
spir tist cwed is that any individual member of the per
suasion can saV or believe anything he or she may think
fit-as a revelation ;in fact, you can create a little god of
vour own and run him to suit yourself, and no man can
sav vou nay. From some of the revelations of the spirits
per media (or mediums) I gather that spiritualists con-
Rirler the devil dead, also, that there is no such thing as
evil-all deviltry being merely undeveloped good- There
are no surnames in spirit-land. The magnetic attractor,
in handwriting is but slight, there is much more in a lock
of hair (which is a circumstance I have frequently noticed
mvself). The society holds mediums meetings—which
are decidedly interesting. To become a medium-live
temperately, eating vegetables only, no drinks or heating
food ; remain quiet, shut out all thought of material
things, camp in a darkened room, and take pencd in
hand. Whatever spirit, if any, possesses you will then
discover itself. The last medium’s meeting I attended
consisted of about 60 persons, mostly females, presided
over by a chairman. After the singing of a hymn accom-
panied by an organ, and a continuance of semi-sacred
music, the mediums present "’ere requested to give
wav ” as soon as possible, which they did. Under the
excitement of the music or the subsequent chorus-singing
in the darkened room, or something, three or four
women, of different ages, contracted severe attacks of
spasms, tapping the floor continuously and shivering 1
violently. They were encouraged to shiver, and, above
all, to let the spirit have its way. Finally, one young
woman, after much shuddering and sobbing, broke loose
and rushed on the platform, where the chairman placed
her on a chair and seized her wrist, possibly to prevent
her breaking away altogether and soaring into the infinite
hereafter. She jerked out a few moral precepts and re-
marks on thepovverandgoodnessand incomprehensibility
of spirits, then sat down. Suddenly she was seized
with another violent spasm, then again addressed
the meeting in an entirely different voice, with
different diction and mannerisms. This peculiarity was
subsequently repeated by other mediums, but writer was
greatly exercised as to why two different spirits, speaking
through the same medium, should commit similar
o-rammatical errors; also, why the spirit of a deceased
French lady should speak in broken English when the me-
dium herself spoke good English. Both men and women
rubbed the forehead emphatically, some wept, while all
perspired profusely. Writer was just beginning to get
interested in the case of a young lady near by, who had
been rocking to and fro and tapping the floor vigorously
for some ten minutes, threatening shortly to break out in
a fine and large way, when the usher or verger, who had
presumably seen him making a note on the back of the
programme, courteously requested him to retire, as his
presence disturbed the harmony and was antagonistic to
the development of the spirits. Then he went across the
road and saw a wicked little heathen barmaid who had no
soul and who kept her spirits in a bottle. But it is a
beautiful and soul-satisfying religion—when you under-
stand it.
