The Poet-Governor.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“The Queen has approved of the appointment of Lord
Tennyson as Governor of South Australia. The second
Lord Tennyson has interested himself deeply in matters
of literary and artistic culture. He has contributed
articles and poems to various magazines, and has pub-
lished a version of ‘ Jack and the Beanstalk.’ ” — Daily
Paper.
We’re tuning our harps, we Austral bards, by one
and two and three,
To sing his praise, in lordly lays, the Peer from
over the Sea.
A score or more have laid in stock of pens and ink
galore,
And paper white whereon to write of the Poet-
Governor.
Right soon each den of editor-men shall cumbered
be with loads
Of Loyal Lyrics, and Welcome Songs, and Salu-
tation Odes;
Por it seems as how that Governors now no more
shall chosen be
Por their ancient blood, or love of the stud, or
wholesale piety.
Their race is run, their reign is done, their places
filled shall be
By gentlemen, who wield the pen, and wade in
poetry.
And, though ’tis hard no native bard may draw a
Guv’nor’s screw,
We drain our beers with loyal cheers, and “ Hal-
lam, here’s to you ! ”
The Adelaide folk have borne the yoke of Guv’-
nors sour too long,
But soon they’ll have fun with a Poet’s son, who’ll
fill their days with song.
And he with the grace of his lordly race will
album-verses write,
And at charity-shows will (through his nose) the
“ Queen of the May ’’ recite.
And we will dedicate Books to him, all bravely
bound in calf,
And he’ll answer free, with a line or three, and his
lordly autograph.
And we in the press these notes will print, and
head them, ev’ry one,
“ Smith’s Poems (or Jones’s) take the cake, says
Hallam, Lord Tennyson.”
And the present bard, whose lines are hard —too
hard indeed to quote—
Will stow away, if he cannot pay, on a South Aus-
tralian boat,
And go to Adelaide Gov’ment House and knock
at the door sans fear,
And say to the menial—“ Tell your lord that a
brother bard is here ! ”
And straightway Hallam, Lord Tennyson, on his
own poetic feet,
Will rush, will fly, with sparkling eye, his brother
bard to greet,
And on his neck will fall and sob—“ I’ve longed
for this for years !
I’ve longed—ah me—your face to see, and now—
excuse these tears ! ”
With swift right-wheel on haughty heel he then
will turn and shout — . ,
“ Bring wine ! bring beer ! bring whisky here ;
bring rum, and gin and stout !
Yea, spread the board ! if I’m * Lord Im a poet,
too, you’ll see, , , , ,
And, though times are hard, my brother bard
shall shake-down here with me.’
0, Adelaide, be not afraid, brave doings you will
see . ,
When the Bard and Lord with liquor stored sit
swapping poetry! , .
This singer here will Hallam cheer by reading him
elegies,
And sometimes list to Hallam’s rot to put him at
his ease.
And bards who are friends of the present scribe
shall goodly guerdon win,
They shall have the run of their teeth and throats
the Gov’ment House within.
And so-called bards who are not his friends, and
his lofty lines deride, .
They shall snort like whales, and gnaw their nails,
and curse in the cold outside.
And when statesmen come to worry the Lord with
dreary affairs of State,
Hallam will rise with fiery eyes and sweep them
from his plate.
He will read them his verse till they weep and
curse, and fly from his high abode -
And the present scribe will shatter the tribe with
a dithyrambic ode.
And when Parliament opes the Governor’s speech
will all be written in rhyme,
And Hallam, he, will sing it so free, and ’twill
fetch ’em every time. . . .
So here’s to Hallam, Lord Tennyson, may his
reign be lucky-starred !
And here’s to the Muse in her new court-shoes,
and here’s to the present Bard !
CREEVE ROE.
George Bell, proprietor of Dunedin Star,
and the “ grand old man ” of the Maoriland press,
is dead, aged 90.
“ 0.P.” philosophises on a vast subject :
Have often wondered whether it is reckless desperation
or shocking ignorance that makes the old maid chivvy
the young man matrimonially. Had an awkward attack
myself recently. Went out for a friendly walk with a
party of three, and unaccountably pulled up in a scrub
with one companion. The dear old girl plumped down
gaily and opened up right away on the venerable subject,
expressed her opinion in plaintive voice as to what con-
stituted a “ good man,” what she considered a reasonable
spell of engagement, and wound up by plunging matters
into a highly advanced stage of courtship. I felt shocked
and alarmed, but weakly yielded, trusting, on Micawber
lines, for something to turn up. It turned up, and
now I want to make a few public remarks to old maids
and those about to marry. It can’t be repeated too often.
Never chase a man with view to above : it’s fatal —it’s
madness. Nothing horrifies a man so much as being
pursued by a female with honorable intentions. A hippo-
potamus isn’t a circumstance to her. Attract his atten-
tion and then skedaddle—that’s the way. Also, throw
wheelbarrows and things in his path. But don’t skedad-
dle too fast. The aboriginals tumbled to this centuries
ago. Life is an obstacle-race, and a man gets to like it
that wav, and becomes an expert jumper from necessity.
Therefore make him jump as often as you can. It flatters
him. Also, most things are prized according to the time
and trouble and number of jumps that it takes to procure
them. And woman is one of them.
In Push Society.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He was a trifle embarrassed. Proi>osal is not the
vogue in pure push society. There matrimony is
usually the outcome of magisterial contact with a
situation made up, broadly, of a weeping woman,
an inopportune baby, and a reluctant youth, who
escapes supporting the girl by according her the
virtuous privilege of supporting him. So that he,
the exception, not the rule, had a lack of
precedent; wherefore his embarrassment. Ab-
stractedly he sought a venomous black plug and
—perhaps in deference to the emotions of the
moment—bit off a painfully small “taste.” In
the next room an asthmatic aecordeon began to
gasp and reach vainly after an air. Above its
shrieks for mercy and the shuffling of many feet
rose the voice of the M.C. in staccato command.
Soon the din had drifted into rhythmic move-
ment. He —the original he—looked relieved.
Contemporaneously he tightened the arm around
her waist and distorted his battered features into
what he hoped to be interpreted as an excess of
tender affection. Also he sighed—a sigh pregnant
with sentiment. He was of romantic disposition.
“Kate,” he said, “Kate, will yeh marry me?”
He looked at the request and, struck by its bold-
ness, added : “Beh me own little wife, will yeh?
Say no en I’ll go away en scragg meself straight.
I love yeh wif all me ’eart, I do,” he went on,
tearfully, “en I got nothin’ to offer yeh but a
true ’eart. Will yeh assept it ? Yeh all the worl’
to me. Yeh —yeh’re—the star of me life.” He
smacked his lips with satisfaction. “ Be me little
dicky-bird, en come en share me ’umble ’ome.
Make yeh ole man keep ’imself. Fire ’im out, en
come en make me the ’appiest ” .
He stopped to hug her closer and imprint a
hoggy kiss upon her neck. He had taken enough
inspiriting beer to be divinely self-satisfied. She
nestled closer, and, letting her head sink on his
breast, sank herself deep in the voluptuous bliss of
the embrace.
“Rats!” she sighed dreamily, “stow that.
D’yer think I don’t know what ye are ? How yeh
killed Red Sue less’n twelve months arter yer was
spliced ? Her that used ter starve for yeh ye’d
bash every day, and ye’d do the same for me—yeh
crool-’earted beast —yeh would !”
He gasped in grieved surprise. His romantic
nature was hurt—trodden on. She resettled her
head, and sighed on while his sense of out-
raged virtue deepened into just indignation, and
on his battered features grew an expression of
wrathful protest. “God knows the- rat-faced
little skirt didn’t deserve no better !”
“Your ’umble ’ome,” she continued with a sigh
of renunciation, ‘ ‘ God knows I’d have to keep
yer in it. Yer livin’ on yer old woman now, mak-
ing her wish she was dead. Ez long ez yeh can
get yer beer yer don’t ”
His outraged feelings triumphed. “ Well, if
this aint pure,” he burst in, his eyes ablaze,
and his whole romantic nature in arms at her
grossness, her sordid estimate of his affection.
“What in the ’ell are yer talking about! Is it
any of your bloomin’ business how I treated
Ginger Sue —eh ? or who I’m livin’ on ? Blast yeh!
I’ll knock the point off yer face off, I will. I’ve
got no stinkin’ pride, see, not me ! I arst yer a
civil questiun, an’ I expects a civil arnswer.
What’s my little ways got ter do with you ? I arst
yer, ‘Will Yer Marry Me?’ Now, I arst yer
again, straight out, ‘Wu.j. yer?”’ He caught
her by the breast of her blouse and swung her
against the wall.
“ Yes !” she said.
With a despairing shriek the aecordeon was
silent, and the rhythmic shuffling of the feet in the
next room broke again into discord.
W.D.O.
A Postponed Suicide.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“I was terribly struck on a girl once,” said Bill.
“ Her old man hud stacks of stuff, and, of course,
wouldn’t stand me at any price ; so Jennie and I
had to keep things very dark. I was workin’ odd
shifts at the Gonebung Extended at the time, and
the shifts were gettin’ odder every pay, so I de-
cided to try my luck prospectin’down Gippsland
way. I thought Jennie would die the night be-
fore I left, she cried so much ; and she made me
promise to write every week to the local post-
office, so that the old man wouldn’t know. Any
of you fellows ever been to Bulumwaal—down
past Bairnsdale? Terrible place for prospectin’.
No outcrops. I got broke in about three months,
and then I joined in with a new-ehum miner—a
man named Cullen —who had a few pounds, and
we started back Dargo way.
“Well, anyhow, we did no good—every hole a
duffer, and every reef a maiden —and about six
months after meeting Cullen I got desp’rate and
utterly low-spirited. I think Culleh’s damper had
a lot to do with it, but anyhow I reasoned it out
that ’twasn’t a fair thing for me to string Jennie
on any longer. I knew she wouldn’t give me up,
because she told me so in every letter, and she
wrote regularly every week. What made me more
miserable was that she was always telling me that
her people were worrying her to marry a man
named Hales, who had a farm close handy. I
thought she’d be better off with Hales, and, as I
had no relations to worry over me, I decided to
blow my head off that night with Cullen’s g in,
and leave Jennie free. After tea, I casuallj m n-
tioned to Cullen that, if ever anything happened
to me, he might drop a line to my girl, and told
him her name and the post-office.
“He said, ‘ That’s funny. I come from near
that part, but never knew you did. Is your girl
any relation to Jack Hales’ wife ? She used to be
a Miss Trotter, too ! ’
“ I laughed. * Why, Jack’s not married ! ’
“Cullen said, ‘Well, anyhow, I started a ter-
rible spree at his wedding, about a week before I
came up here. He married old Mick Trotter’s
daughter, Jennie. Same initial as your girl, too ! ’
“ I tell you I felt fair beat. This poor, inno-
cent country-girl must have got married about
two months after I left, and had been fooling me
all the time since. I decided not to suicide that
night—went ’possuming instead. Couple of days
after I got a letter from Jennie saying that her
people had forced her to marry Hales, that her
heart was broken, and that perhaps I had better
not come back as the sight of me would kill her.
“ Jennie is still alive. An’ me, too.”
W.H.D.

An Anaemic Lady Made Plump
and Happy.
“SHE NEVER FELT BETTER IN HER
LIFE.”
Mrs. Johns, of 00 Yurong-street, Sydney, is now
a pretty and vivacious lady of about 25, and, in
answer to the questions put to her, she said :—“ I
have been very ill, but at present I am enjoying
excellent health and spirits. Indeed, I don’t know
that I ever felt better in my life. I can safely say
that I owe my satisfactory recovery entirely to Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills. The first cause of my ill-
ness was, I think, the worry and extra work caused
by moving from place to place, for I have been a
great traveller during the last year or two. Any-
how, after my last journey, I began to feel very
low and depressed in spirits ; I could eat scarcely
anything, and began to get very thin. I was as
yellow as if I had had a severe fit of the jaun-
dice. I got from bad to worse ; the least exertion
wearied me, and my ordinary housework became
too much for me. My gums grew quite white, and
my lips looked a bluish-grey color. My sleep was
restless and disturbed, and I was so tired in the
mornings that I could hardly get out of bed, and
when I did I used to lay on the sofa, feeling I
hardly cared what happened to me. I saw a
doctor, who said I needed a strong tonic, and I
had several bottles of physic from him, besides
other bottles of tonics which I obtained from
chemists ; but I grew no better. I was in this
miserable and hopeless condition when I heard
from someone who had been cured by them about
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills, and I at once got some.
After taking a few I was agreeably surprised to
find a real and distinct change for the better. 1
got rid of that miserable depression of spirits,
which in itself was enough to keep me ill ; and in
a few days I was able to sleep the night through
without interruption. The Pills, too, gave me a
good appetite, and I began slowly to get cheerful
and fat again. My color came back, and after
taking six boxes I was completely cured and
found myself as strong as ever. I shall always
remember my cure with gratitude, and shall, you
may be sure, recommend this wonderful medicine
to any of my friends who mav have the bad for-
tune to suffer as I did.”
Relentless Indigestion.
A BAD CASE CURED.
“ I must tell you,” said Mr. Handley, who
resides at the rear of 94 Harris-street, Pyrmont,
Sydney, “ that I am a greengrocer, and am out in
all weathers, and used to have very little time for
my meals. The complaint came on gradually,
and at first I didn’t let it worry me; but I was
soon awakened. I got worse and worse, in spite
of various remedies. After eating I would feel a
most terrible pain in the lower part of my chest,
as if some hundredweight of lead were there, and,
though often hungry, 1 would refrain from eating,
because I knew the result. I became very thin,
and felt as though life were hardly worth living.
Meat 1 had almost to refrain from eating. Any
excitement brought on the pain, and I was unable
to climb a hill, so great was my exhaustion and
want of breath. Even bread would disagree with
me. I consulted three doctors, who told me I
was suffering from indigestion and dyspepsia.
I tried _ the hospital, and was six weeks in
one, being laid up on that occasion for three
months. This bad attack occurred about two
years ago, but I’ve had several since. I had the
most unpleasant taste in my mouth on rising, just
as if I had been sucking some copper article. My
skin and my chest-bone became so tender that I
could hardly bear the touch of my clothes. Well,
my wife, who has been ill for years, is taking a
course of Dr. Williams’Pink Pills, and she had
even then derived the greatest benefit from them.”
Here Mrs. Handley nodded a smiling assent.
“My wife thought Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills
might do me good, so I gave them a trial. The
first few doses drove away the pains in my head,
and I found, to my surprise and pleasure, that my
indigestion was slowly disappearing. That was
about six months ago, and I have never gone back
since. As time went on my taste for food re-
turned, and now I can eat with comfort. I can
honestly say iny indigestion is entirely a thing of
the past, and its disappearance is entirely owing
to Dr. Williams’Pink Pills. That terrible weight
on my chest and the unpleasant taste left me, and
also the retching that accompanied my complaint.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills are undoubtedly the
remedy of the age for dyspepsia, indigestion and
like comnlaints.”
Heat Apoplexy.
A WONDERFUL RECOVERY.
Half-a-century since, when Melbourne had
scarcely qualified itself for aught but the name of
a prosperous bush township, Mr. M. P. Ryan, of
the Melbourne Chilled Butter Co., Flinders-lane
West, had already attained some fame amongst
his juvenile compeers as a cricketer, to be realised
later on when he was classed in the front rank of
the “Emerald Hillites.” From that time to the
present, in all climates and colonies, he has, when
opportunity served, paid willing homage to the
national game. He and “ Mid-on ”of the Leader
had been foregathering a short time prior to our
representative meeting him, and Mr. Ryan was
dwelling with some gusto on a three days’ match
played early in the ’Bo’s at Wileannia, with the
thermometer at 116 in the shade all through.
“It was hot,’’ remarked Mr. Ryan, “and I
have cause to remember it.”
“ In which respect ? ”
“ More than one ; principally the impression it
made upon my brain. You must know this was
no unusual occurrence with us in those days.
Some would say it was carrying cricketing
enthusiasm too far. Be that as it may, the con-
tinual exercise in the heat resulted eventually in
an attack of heat-apoplexy or sun-stroke. The
attack was a severe one ; so severe I was totally
incapacitated from following my clerical duties.
All the usual remedies were used, and, after a
time, appeared to have some effect. But from
that date annually I was subject to the most
agonising pains in the head. Such an effect did they
have that, during warm weather, mental work was
out of the question. I was subject to sharp shoot-
ing pains in my head and a feeling as if a bolt had
been driven into my brain, dulling its action. The
least effort of the brain induced stupor, until a few
years since.”
“ Two years since ; why the change?”
“ Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People.
Three years this Christmas I was at Brighton,
and a gentleman friend, remarking my complete
mental exhaustion, recommended them. I had
visited doctors out of number, tried all sorts of
treatment, electric baths, massage, also hydro-
pathy, but as soon as the warm weather com-
menced so did my head. I consumed seven boxes
of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills in all; but neither the
great heat of last summer or the one preceding it
affected me in the least. Yes, you can use this.
I am well-known in Victoria and N.S.W.”
(Sgd.) Matthew P. Ryan.

The River and the Road.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The merrymaking’s over,
The riverside is still,
The Sun, a radiant rover,
Gone down behind the hill.
The red Road goes a-winding
Along the riverside ;
The River, no man minding,
Winds on to meet the tide.
O Naiad of green places !
I pray you pause and say
How many pretty faces
Looked down on you to-day ?
The River runs in silence
(A fern frond is her load)
Just here and just a mile hence
She curves to kiss the road.
And now the kiss is over,
And now the tryst is done,
By flats of fern and clover
'-'The River ripples on.
Again the Road turns to her,
Red-winding through the green,
The Road would pause and woo her
But gray rocks stand between.
And here he rounds a boulder
And hurries to her side ;
The River turns her shoulder;
She will not be bis bride.
O fickle River, straying
Tli rough green lands on and on,
A fern-tree heard you saying
“ The Road will come anon.”
3Sot so, but you will waken
To lonely days and sore ;
The Road a vow has taken
To play Love’s fool no more.
On high the sunset lingers
With one still star above,
And there the merry singers
Sing silverly of Love.
And now in distance dewy
They halt awhile, and so
Wave hands with “ Coo-ee, Coo-ft ! ”
Ho, laggard down below ! ”
If she should cease to worry
And say, “ I love but you ”
“ O hurry, hurry, hurry ! ”
And “ Adieu, Adieu, Adieu ! ’’
This one last chance I give her
To lighten my heart’s load,
And if she play the River
Then I shall prove the Road.
I caught her, heard her sighing,
Saw her face and felt its charm . . .
’T is sweet when Day is dying
To walk so, arm in arm.
RODERIC QUINN.
“Last Night.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Do you know what it is to be stranded in town
without a copper ? To belong also to that portion
of society that wears immaculate linen, and eats
its evening meal in swallowtails. To be by birth
a toff, and by position a vagrant; to still retain a
decent suit, boots and hat that are only rather
shabby, and a self-respect and pride which insists
on your refusing such invitations as you believe
may be prompted by pity rather than courtesy.
Though shivering with cold, you profess a rooted
dislike to greatcoats, and vote the man who carries
an umbrella a mollycoddle.
I had just refused to dine with a man who lived
out at Hawthorn, not so much because I feared
appearing a cadger, as because I had not the
necessary threepence for a tram-fare. I pleaded a
previous engagement, bade him “Good night” as
he swung on to the dummy, and departed with
affected briskness down Collins-street to —nowhere.
The white electric lights flared overhead, red and
green lights flashed past, the streets were rapidly
emptying, and I paused at the corner, near the
Equitable Buildings, wondering what was to be
done next. I might walk out to St. Kilda to see
an old schoolfellow. Possibly he would offer me a
bed, possibly he would not. A light rain began
to fall and I sought shelter under the verandah
opposite. I was hungry, cold, depressed; angry
with fate and myself. Through my brain surged
the ideas such conditions always suggest. I could
have burst into tears, yelled with laughter, or
punched the heads of the passers by.
I felt a hand on my arm, “You, Jack ! Who’s
the girl ?”
“ Lily, you ! Who’s what girl ?”
“The girl you are waiting for. She is very
late.”
“You are the only girl I ever waited for, the
only girl I ”
“ Don’t be silly. Where are you going to dine ?
I haven’t seen you for six months.”
“ Six centuries. I haven’t a bean, and I was
contemplating suicide—not dinner.”
“ Much too miserable a night for suicide. We’ll
commit it together, some glorious moonlight even-
ing, at the clean end of the Yarra. Come and
have tea with me.”
“My dear girl, I haven’t a red cent.”
“My dear boy, I have only a red herring.
Come and share it; we’ll make up a fire to sit
over, and try to remember a dinner we had one
evening at the White Hart. ”
Women are tactful. I had been flush at that
time, and we had had the best of dinners, and
afterwards gone to admire and laugh at Julius
Knight pretending to be Napoleon.
“We can make buttered toast to put the herring
on,” she said. “Do come; I want your advice,
and I’m very lonely.”
So away we went up the wet street, under the
glistening trees, the tram lines flashing in the
light, great splashes of red and green reflected in
pools as lamps swept past. The big Town Hall
loomed vaguely against a gray sky. We knew
Melbourne well. We knew the queer lanes and
alleys at the back of its broad streets. We knew
whore you can get the cheapest meals and lodgings,
as we also knew how comfortably one can be fed
at the Maison Doree. We knew it on foot, and
we’ve rolled through it in hansoms ; we have sat
in pit and dress circle. We could find our way
almost blindfold, and are intimate with many of
its identities.
We dived down a lane, turned up a right-of-
way, mounted some crazy stairs, unlocked a door
and were in Lily’s room.
“Times are bad.” She signified the lack of
furniture. Yet a great bunch of roses stood on
the mantel-shelf amongst numerous photographs ;
a handsome opera cloak hung over her bed, and a
silver-backed mirror lay on the deal table.
“You light the fire,” she said; “I’ll get the
other things ready.”
“ Are you hard up ?”
“Very. Had an engagement at the ‘Royal’
for a few months, got ill, and had to spend three
weeks in hospital. Have been in a fiowershop.
Hope good old Bland ’ll take me on again next
week. If not, am going to try Harry. ”
We made great piles of toast, and when the
butter gave out used dripping. We divided the
herring carefully, and talked without stopping.
When the meal was over we threw a rug on the
floor, and sat looking into the fire.
“Jack, I nearly love you sometimes.”
“ Only sometimes ! I quite love you, always
‘‘ Do you ?—but yours is such a battered old
withered heart, —all seams, dents, and patches,
though you are only twenty-four.” Her hand,
lying warm in mine, gave an affectionate little
squeeze. “ Why I love you is because you are
such a good mate, and because you are not
ashamed to lift your hat to me when you are with
your sisters.”
“I’m not a cad. You are a friend of mine ; so
why ”
“I know; that’s why I love you. Your coat
wants a stitch ; take it off and let me mend it for
you.”
I sat beside her in my shirt-sleeves, and watched
her quick fingers.
“You want a cigarette, of course; there are
several in a case behind that photograph of Baker.
They are beastly dry, I expect, but they’re
Egyptians ; old Casey gave them to me.”
“ I’m not jealous of him. When are you going
to marry me, Lily ?”
“ To-morrow, sweetheart. On nothing a week,
and quarrel till I run away with someone else.”
“Should we quarrel?”
“Of course. I’m a snarling hell-cat when I’m
in a temper, and you’re a most particular beast
sometimes.”
“ Then you won’t marry me ?”
“Yes, when you are a successful novelist, and
I’m a successful actress. ”
“When ”
“ Don’t be gloomy. There is a someday. Have
you ever thought what a jolly time someday will
be ? How many hopes fulfilled someday ? How
many ambitions realised someday? How many
lovers united someday? We’ll all be rich and
happy someday. Don’t sit so close to me or I
can’t sew.”
“Never mind the sewing. You haven’t kissed
me, Lily; that need not wait till someday.”
“I’ll kiss you when I say goodnight, not before.”
“And when are you going to say goodnight,
sweetheart? We’ve made it a lengthy business
sometimes. ”
“I’m going to say it very soon. Where are
you going to sleep ?”
I shrugged my shoulders.
“ If you like you can sleep in a.n empty room on
the next landing: it has had no tenant for a week.
We can force the door open somehow, and I’ll lend
you this rug.”
“Lily, I wish it were someday.”
“So do I, if we are going to be rich. If not,
it’s as well to be young.”
“ What is the good of being young if one can’t
have what one wants ?”
‘ ‘ Just as well, perhaps. Life is made up of the
things one can’t got and the things one doesn’t
want.”
“Stop moralising! How blue your eyes are,
dear ! I want to begin to say goodnight.”
“I’ll lend you this cloak as well.”
“I don’t want the cloak—l ”
“I thought you were a mate of mine! Say
goodnight !”
And I said goodnight.
MARS.
Drink item from Paris :
Petrolism is the new vice that threatens the brains, the
strength and purses of Parisians and Frenchmen, or,
rather, it is the old vice of alcoholism, in a new and
startling form. The taste of this disagreeable beverage
grows upon the consumer, it is said, until it develops
into an irresistible passion. The doctors have not yet
had opportunities to study its effects, and their views,
lacking a firm basis, are divergent. The consensus of
opinion, however, maintains that the intoxication pro-
duced by petroleum is of a melancholy nature which
tinges things in grey. The petroleum drinker is sad,
discontented, despairing. Unlike whisky, the mineral
oil never bestows “ Dutch courage ” upon the
quaffer, who is rarely quarrelsome or pugnacious.
Generally speaking, the “ petroliques ” sleep calmly
enough, and even their general health is not as rapidly
undermined as by the alcoholic beverages in common
demand. Here, however, opinions differ. Some doctors
hold that the gravest organic changes are caused by the
use, which is also an abuse, of petroleum, and that the
germs of mortal diseases are called into being. The
manner in which the latest batch of “ petroliques ” was
discovered is curious. A firm in the city furnished petro-
leum to certain retail shops. The head of one of the
latter complained of short measure. The wholesale mer-
chant denied the charge, and proved that he was right.
At last it was discovered that the retailer’s workmen
drank it. Another confraternity of tipplers was convicted
of the offence in a different way. A usually sober
and hard:vvorking man, who was happy though married,
began suddenly to stagger home in a state of half-seas-
over. His spouse, horrified at the prospect before her,
searched his pockets, and, to her surprise, found that his
funds were not lower than before. Neither was he ever
late, strange to say. Unable to solve the riddle, she con-
fided to one of her married friends. The latter had a
husband of her own, who was in the same boat. They
put their heads together, and, after some days of shadow-
ing and inquiring, the two sorrowing matrons learned
that their lords and masters were slaves to petroleum.
What shall it profit a teetotaller though he sup-
presses the wine when it is red and the beer
when it is long ? For if the human race only
starts a grey, cheerless, sunless drunk on
paraffin oil instead of the old, happy drunk
that it used to have, things are no further for-
ward, after all.
